North Hempstead v. Exxon Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


53 N.Y.2d 747; 421 N.E.2d 834; 439 N.Y.S.2d 342; 1981 N.Y. LEXIS 2373


January 6, 1981, Argued  
April 7, 1981, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporation -- Regulation of Self-Service Gasoline Stations

An order of the Appellate Division which reversed two judgments of the Supreme Court declaring Local Law No. 1 of the Local Laws of 1976 of the Town of North Hempstead constitutional and enjoining an oil company from operating a self-service gasoline station within the town, declared the law unconstitutional, and vacated the injunction, is reversed and the judgment reinstated. The local law prohibited dispensing gasoline from self-service pumps in order to reduce fire hazards and although there was impressive evidence to the contrary, it cannot be said, even in the absence of relevant legislative findings, that it has been shown beyond a reasonable doubt that there was no rational basis for the adoption of the ordinance, since the burden of proof is upon the party challenging the ordinance and the mere presence of evidence casting doubt upon the existence of the hazards sought to be alleviated by the legislation is not conclusive proof of irrationality; so long as there is evidence that the issue is at least debatable, the legislative judgment is not irrational.

 

231 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Roth


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


52 N.Y.2d 440; 420 N.E.2d 929; 438 N.Y.S.2d 737; 1981 N.Y. LEXIS 2282; 1981-1 Trade Cas. (CCH) P64,026


February 10, 1981, Argued  
April 2, 1981, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, licensed physicians, were indicted under the Donnelly Act, specifically N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 340. Defendants' motion to dismiss the indictment was granted. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York) affirmed the order and the State appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were physicians, indicted under the Donnelly Act, codified at N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 340, for organizing a group boycott and refusing to furnish professional services to nonemergency workers' compensation and no-fault insurance patients. The lower court affirmed the dismissal of the indictment. The court affirmed the decision. The court had previously examined the language and legislative history of the Donnelly Act and had held that it was not intended to apply to the legal profession. In the instant case, the court could discern no principled basis for distinguishing between the legal profession and the medical profession. It concluded that the medical profession was similarly insulated from liability under the Donnelly Act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the lower court, which affirmed the dismissal of the indictment against defendants.


HEADNOTES

Monopolies -- Exemption of Medical Profession from State Antitrust Laws

The medical profession is insulated from liability under the Donnelly Act (General Business Law, § 340), which prohibits "[combinations] whereby * * * [competition] or the free exercise of any activity in the conduct of any business, trade or commerce or in the furnishing of any service * * * is or may be restrained", and accordingly, an indictment charging defendants, licensed physicians, with a violation of the Donnelly Act by organizing a group boycott and concertedly refusing to furnish professional services to nonemergency workers' compensation and no-fault insurance patients was properly dismissed. The Donnelly Act was not intended to apply to the legal profession ( Matter of Freeman, 34 NY2d 1) and there is no principled basis for distinquishing between the legal and medical professions for purposes of the Freeman rule. Goldfarb v Virginia State Bar (421 U.S. 773), which held that the language and legislative history of the Federal antitrust laws did not support the conclusion that Congress intended to exempt the learned professions from the strictures of the Sherman Act, has no direct bearing upon a State court's analysis of an analogous provision enacted by the State Legislature.

 

232 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Sokolov v. Freeport


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


52 N.Y.2d 341; 420 N.E.2d 55; 438 N.Y.S.2d 257; 1981 N.Y. LEXIS 2232


February 12, 1981, Argued  
March 31, 1981, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs, and the judgment of Supreme Court, Nassau County, reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landlords sought review of a decision from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed a trial court's judgment that declared a rental ordinance of respondent village to be unconstitutional in that it allowed warrantless administrative inspections. The appellate court dismissed the landowner's complaint.


OVERVIEW: The landlords claimed that Freeport, N.Y., Village Ordinance art. IV (1978) (ordinance) violated the Fourth Amendment and the New York Constitution, which prohibited searches without warrants. The landlords also argued that being a landlord did not imply their consent to an administrative search of their rental properties by the village. The village claimed that the intrusion was minimal and that the landlords consented by going into the residential rental business. On review, the court held that the Fourth Amendment required a search warrant for the inspection required under the ordinance and that the landlords did not waive their constitutional right to be free of unreasonable searches by entering into the business of renting residential property. The court further held that it was not a consent to search when the landlords were compelled to allow the inspections to occur under the threat of prosecution and denial of their right to continue to use their property for rental purposes.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision from the appellate court that reversed the trial court's declaratory judgment that the village's rental permit ordinance was unconstitutional. The court reinstated the trial court's judgment.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Rental Permit Requirement -- Warrantless Inspection of Residential Property

A rental permit ordinance providing that an individual will be subject to criminal penalty if he rents his premises as a residential dwelling without first obtaining a permit, said permit to be renewed every two years or each time a vacancy occurs and to issue only if the property owner consents to an inspection of the premises, which inspection must occur within two business days after the property owner has notified the appropriate agency of a vacancy in the rental property, is unconstitutional insofar as it effectively authorizes and, indeed, requires, a warrantless inspection of residential rental property (US Const, 4th Amdt); it is beyond the power of the State to condition an owner's ability to engage his property in the business of residential rental upon his forced consent to forego certain rights guaranteed to him under the Constitution.

 

233 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Linda F.M.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


52 N.Y.2d 236; 418 N.E.2d 1302; 437 N.Y.S.2d 283; 1981 N.Y. LEXIS 2119


January 12, 1981, Argued  
February 24, 1981, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, adopted individual, appealed the decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) which affirmed an order of the trial court that denied petitioner's request for access to sealed adoption records for failure to evidence good cause to justify the grant of access to such records.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, adopted individual, challenged the lower court's affirmance of a trial court's denial of her request for access to sealed adoption records and the determination that petitioner's claims of psychological problems caused by her inability to discover the identity of her natural parents did not constitute good cause pursuant to N.Y. Dom. Rel. Law § 114. The court affirmed the judgment, as petitioner's alleged psychological problems failed to provide good cause. The natural parents retained a strong interest in their anonymity and the petitioner's problems were not credibly connected to a lack of knowledge of petitioner's natural parents' identities. The record supported findings that petitioner was troubled by mere general curiosity about her parentage rather than from concrete and compelling need. Therefore, when balanced against the interests of other parties to the adoption process, petitioner's allegations did not alone constitute good cause to allow access to sealed adoption records. Finally, given the difficulty to locate the natural parents by reasonable efforts, the decision to forego notice to the natural parents of petitioner's motion was proper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of petitioner, adopted individual's, request for access to sealed adoption records, as petitioner's alleged problems related to an inability to identify her natural parents insufficiently constituted good a showing of good cause for access to such records when balanced against the interests in anonymity of other parties to the adoption process.


HEADNOTES

Adoption -- Unsealing Adoption Records -- Good Cause Requirement

1. Petitioner, who learned, when she was over 30 years old, that she had been adopted as an infant is not entitled to discover the identity of her natural parents by obtaining access to the sealed adoption records upon allegations that her inability to discover the identity of her natural parents has caused psychological problems that have broken up a marriage and hampered development of her artistic and musical creativity; section 114 of the Domestic Relations Law provides that disclosure shall be granted only for good cause, and petitioner's problems, which are not credibly connected to her lack of knowledge of her natural parents' identities, do not constitute good cause.

Adoption -- Unsealing Adoption Records -- Notice to Natural Parents

2. In a proceeding by an adopted person to discover the identity of his natural parents by obtaining access to the sealed adoption records, notification of the proceeding should be given to the natural parents (see Domestic Relations Law, § 114) if the court finds that the petitioner has made a showing of entitlement and the natural parents can be located with reasonable effort in a manner that will not be likely to be self-defeating by revealing their identities to the adoptive parents or others. Accordingly, a decision to forego notice to the natural parents was proper where it would have been all but impossible to locate them by reasonable efforts and other means of protecting their interests sufficed.

Angelone v. Rochester


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


52 N.Y.2d 982; 420 N.E.2d 85; 438 N.Y.S.2d 287; 1981 N.Y. LEXIS 2220


January 13, 1981, Argued  
February 17, 1981, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs, in a memorandum.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Repayment of Real Property Taxes Collected in Excess of Constitutional Limitation

In an action to recover excess real property taxes paid and to declare unconstitutional a city ordinance of the City of Rochester which provided for a special tax levy in order to finance [**3] a refund of taxes illegally levied in prior years in excess of the city's constitutional debt limitation, an order of the Appellate Division, which affirmed a judgment declaring the ordinance unconstitutional, is affirmed for the reasons stated in the opinion at the Appellate Division, which concluded that the ordinance was unconstitutional, in the first place, because by providing for refunds to all taxpayers regardless of whether they had previously overpaid their taxes or not, the city was in some cases making refunds which were not based on obligations owed by the city, and thereby was making unconstitutional gifts (NY Const, art VIII, § 1), and, in the second place, the ordinance was unconstitutional because it failed to include the extra assessment used to fund the special refund account in the computation of the constitutional tax margin, and because the entire scheme was a fiction under which the amount of the levy was exactly the same as the amount of the refund, with no taxpayer actually paying or receiving anything. It is noted, however, that the Court of Appeals has no occasion to consider whether that portion of the city's ordinance which establishes an equitable claim [**4] for a tax refund on behalf of all taxpayers within the city, considered separately, would constitute an unconstitutional gift (see NY Const, art VIII, § 1).

236 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Dutchess Sanitation, Inc. v. Plattekill


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


51 N.Y.2d 670; 417 N.E.2d 74; 435 N.Y.S.2d 962; 1980 N.Y. LEXIS 2779


October 15, 1980, Argued  
December 22, 1980, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sanitation company challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York) that affirmed the trial court's denial of the sanitation company's motion for summary judgment and granting respondent town's cross motion. The sanitation company sought to vacate a prior injunction against it.


OVERVIEW: In a prior action, the sanitation company was enjoined from accepting refuse in violation of an ordinance forbidding anyone other than those who resided or conducted a business in the town from depositing refuse originating elsewhere than in the town. Until then, the sanitation company operated a tract of land accepting such substances. When the court later invalidated a New Jersey statute prohibiting the importation into that state of most out-of-state wastes, the sanitation company instituted an action to vacate the injunction. The trial court denied the sanitation company's motion for summary judgment on the ground of res judicata and dismissed the action. The appellate court rejected the res judicata rationale but affirmed on the basis that the ban was evenhanded. On review, the court reversed, holding that the application of the ordinance offended constitutional requirements. The court found that in the absence of a reason, apart from its origin, to treat the out-of-town garbage differently from an equivalent quantity of the local product, the town's ordinance violated the principle of nondiscrimination imposed by the commerce clause.


OUTCOME: The order affirming the dismissal of the sanitation company's action to vacate a prior injunction against it was reversed.


HEADNOTES

Judgments -- Vacatur -- Change of Circumstances

1. In an action to vacate an injunction, granted in a prior action in which the validity of a town ordinance prohibiting the dumping within the town of refuse that originates outside the town was upheld and in which a sanitary landfill operator was permanently enjoined from violating any of the terms of the ordinance, insofar as it prevents the disposal of refuse originating outside the State, the town is not entitled to summary judgment on the ground of res judicata; when there has been a change in the conditions that originally justified the issuance of a continuing injunction, which alteration in circumstances may be grounded on change in the interpretation of applicable law, settled principles do not leave a court of equity without power to determine that the injunction's survival, in all or in part, is no longer warranted. Accordingly, said injunction, which demanded obedience to legislation whose constitutional validity is found to have been undermined by subsequent authoritative adjudications, may be vacated or modified in the subsequent action.

Constitutional Law -- Interstate Commerce -- Waste Disposal

2. A State regulation that indirectly imposes a burden on commerce will be subject to constitutional scrutiny and will not be upheld unless its impact is reasonable when weighed against an important State interest which cannot be realized through a less burdensome means. Accordingly, a Town of Plattekill ordinance, duly enacted in the exercise of a delegation of State power (Town Law, § 130), which broadly forbids anyone other than those who reside or conduct an established business in the town from depositing within its boundaries any garbage, rubbish or other articles originating elsewhere than in the town, violates the interstate commerce clause of the United States Constitution (art I, § 8), as applied to plaintiff sanitary landfill operator which, it is undisputed, would serve out-of-State as well as out-of-town enterprises but for said ordinance; in the absence of a reason, apart from its origin, to treat the out-of-town garbage differently from an equivalent quantity of the local product, respondent's ordinance, in actual operation, violates the principle of nondiscrimination imposed by the commerce clause, which it may not do.

 

237 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Monroe-Livingston Sanitary Landfill, Inc. v. Caledonia


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


51 N.Y.2d 679; 417 N.E.2d 78; 435 N.Y.S.2d 966; 1980 N.Y. LEXIS 2780


October 15, 1980, Argued  
December 22, 1980, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant landfill corporation (corporation) sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a judgment of the trial court declaring that the Sanitary Landfill Ordinance (ordinance) of respondent town was valid and that its enforcement against the corporation's entire landfill operation on its property in the town was proper.


OVERVIEW: While the corporation was negotiating a contract with a county to handle all of its refuse, the town enacted the ordinance, part of which provided that refuse generated outside of the town would not be accepted at facilities licensed by the town board unless authorized and consistent with the regional comprehensive plan as it related to solid waste management. The corporation filed an action for a declaratory judgment, seeking a determination that the ordinance was unconstitutional. The corporation argued that N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 27, which dealt with solid waste disposal, superseded and preempted the town ordinance and that the town ordinance violated the interstate commerce clause of the federal constitution. The court held that the state had not preempted the area by legislation and that the town ordinance was not inconsistent with the state law because it did not prohibit something the state law would consider acceptable. The court further held that because the corporation was not engaged in the importation of refuse from out of state, there had been no showing that, in practice, the ordinance had the effect of inhibiting or discriminating against interstate commerce.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's decision that affirmed the trial court's judgment declaring the town ordinance constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Local Laws -- Pre-emption of Subject by State

1. Where local government is otherwise authorized to act, it will be prohibited from legislating on a subject only if the State pre-empts the field through legislation evidencing a State purpose to exclude the possibility of varying local legislation.

Municipal Corporations -- Sanitary Landfill Ordinance

2. A town's sanitary landfill ordinance providing that "Refuse generated outside of the Town * * * will not be accepted at facilities licensed by the Town Board" is a constitutional and legitimate exercise of the town's police power to protect the public health; ECL article 27, which deals with solid waste disposal, disclaims any State purpose to either supersede or preclude the enactment of local ordinances so long as they are consistent "with at least the minimum applicable requirements" of the regulations promulgated by the statute and speaks of the inclusion of local government in the planning and control of problems endemic to waste management; furthermore, the ordinance does not violate the interstate commerce clause of the Federal Constitution since it is not aimed at interstate refuse, but excludes any refuse from outside the town, and it does not affect interstate commerce by the threat of a ripple effect through the imposition of cumulative burdens.

Society for Ethical Culture v. Spatt


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


51 N.Y.2d 449; 415 N.E.2d 922; 434 N.Y.S.2d 932; 1980 N.Y. LEXIS 2740


November 18, 1980, Argued  
December 18, 1980, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner sought review a judgment of the Court of Appeals (New York), which reversed the judgment of a trial court and held that the subject property was properly declared a historic landmark pursuant to New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § 207-1.0(n).


OVERVIEW: The property owner contended that the landmark designation and the restrictions attached thereto constituted an unconstitutional taking of its property without compensation. The court disagreed and affirmed the judgment of the appellate court. The court held that the landmark designation did not physically or financially prevent, or seriously interfere with the carrying out of the property owner's charitable purpose so there was no taking by the City. The court rejected the contention that the designation prevented the most lucrative use of the property because the landowner did not have a right to use the property for its most lucrative use. The court held that the landmark designation did not interfere with the free exercise of the property owner's religious activities because the First Amendment protection afforded to religious organizations did not entitle the property owner to immunity from reasonable government regulation when the property owner acted in purely secular matters.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court, which upheld the City's landmark designation, was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Religious Corporations and Associations -- Landmark Designation of Association Property

Despite the burden landmark designations place on particular landowners, such designations, if not unreasonable, are not an undue imposition under proper circumstances, which standard, when related to commercial property, necessarily requires that the owner not be deprived by the regulation of a reasonable return on his property, but, when related to property owned by charitable organizations, is refined to permit the restriction as long as it does not physically or financially prevent, or seriously interfere with, the carrying out of the charitable purpose; there is no constitutional requirement that a landowner always be allowed his property's most beneficial use. Accordingly, landmark designation of a building owned by petitioner religious, educational and charitable organization because of its architectural and historical significance, which designation will prevent the exploitation of the full economic development of the property, is not unreasonable, nor, under these circumstances, does it amount to a confiscation without due compensation or an interference with the free exercise of the organization's religious purpose; although petitioner is entitled to First Amendment protection as a religious organization, this does not entitle it to immunity from reasonable government regulation when it acts purely in secular matters.

 

239 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Whidden


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


51 N.Y.2d 457; 415 N.E.2d 927; 434 N.Y.S.2d 936; 1980 N.Y. LEXIS 2741


October 8, 1980, Argued  
December 18, 1980, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), affirming a judgment of the county court, entering judgment of conviction of rape in the third degree under N.Y. Penal Law §§ 130.30, 130.25 (2); defendant contended that the statutes violated equal protection.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to statutory rape, N.Y. Penal Law §§ 130.30, 130.25 (2). Defendant appealed, challenging the constitutionality of the laws on the ground that they violated equal protection by imposing criminal sanctions specifically upon men only. The appellate division affirmed the conviction. On appeal, the court upheld the statutes and affirmed the conviction. The legislature was serving an important governmental interest, the prevention of early pregnancies, when it created different punishments based upon gender. The state was not required to produce legislative history to prove that the legislature was actually considering this particular justification when it enacted the law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction; the statutory rape statutes were constitutional because they served the important governmental interest of protecting underage girls from early pregnancies.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Equal Protection of Laws -- Tests

1. In evaluating whether a statute violates the equal protection clause, a court normally applies a "rational basis" test to determine whether the varied treatment of separate classifications of citizens rests on grounds wholly irrelevant to the achievement of the State's objective; where a statute's application differentiates on the basis of race, alienage or nationality, however, the classification is deemed suspect and a strict scrutiny test must be applied to determine whether the challenged law is necessary to promote a compelling governmental interest. Between these two tests, a third has developed to evaluate a constitutional challenge to a gender-based statute which must serve important governmental objectives and must be substantially related to achievement of those objectives.

Crimes -- Rape -- Statutory Rape

2. Although the statutory rape laws (Penal Law, §§ 130.30, 130.25, subd 2), which make it a felony for an 18- or 21-year-old male to have sexual intercourse with a consenting female under the age of 14 or 17, respectively, are gender based, they are not unconstitutional, since the problem of early pregnancy provides an ample justification for the legislative decision to deter sexual contact between older males and teen-aged girls by imposing criminal sanctions.

Statutes -- Legislative History

3. Undue reliance should not be placed on legislative history in determining whether to strike down legislation which, on its face, indicates that it was enacted for a valid purpose, and it would be anomalous to strike down statutes on a rationale which implicitly admits that the very same legislation could be immediately re-enacted on the basis of an explanation more perceptive than that which was originally offered; furthermore, it is illogical to require the production of legislative history documenting the underlying purpose for enactment or retention of a statute where, perhaps due to the statute's immutably sound basis, no such legislative history exists, and where legislation is supported by unrefuted, plausible and constitutionally sufficient justifications, a healthy recognition of the limits of judicial power compels the conclusion that the challenge to that legislation must be rejected.

 

240 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Onofre


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


51 N.Y.2d 476; 415 N.E.2d 936; 434 N.Y.S.2d 947; 1980 N.Y. LEXIS 2743; 20 A.L.R.4th 987


October 8, 1980, Argued  
December 18, 1980, Decided



DISPOSITION: In People v Onofre: Order affirmed.

In People v Peoples and Goss and People v Sweat: Orders reversed, convictions vacated and informations dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Prosecutor appealed from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which dismissed an indictment. Three defendants convicted by Erie County Court (New York) also appealed, challenging the constitutionality of N.Y. Penal Laws §§ 130.38 and 130.30, which prohibited deviant sexual behavior by consenting unmarried adults.


OVERVIEW: Four defendants were charged and convicted under N.Y. Penal Laws §§ 130.38 and 130.30 prohibiting certain sexual acts between non-married persons. Defendants claimed that the law was unconstitutional based on privacy and class distinctions. The court held that there was no rational connection between the proscribed acts and the classification, as the statute only applied to non-married individuals. Citing the right to be left alone, the court found that the statutes violated the defendants' constitutional right of privacy. The People failed to demonstrate how government interference with the practice of personal choice in matters of intimate sexual behavior out of view of the public and with no commercial component would serve to advance the cause of public morality or do anything other than restrict individual conduct and impose a concept of private morality chosen by the state. No showing was made as to how the statute banning consensual sodomy between persons not married to each other preserved or fostered marriage.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that the convictions be vacated and informations dismissed as it concluded that the imposition of criminal sanctions for certain sexual activity between consenting non-married adults was unconstitutional as it denied defendants their rights of privacy and due process.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Sodomy -- Consensual Sodomy

 Section 130.38 of the Penal Law, which criminalizes consensual sodomy or deviate sexual intercourse between persons not married to each other as defined in subdivision 2 of section 130.00 of the Penal Law, is violative of rights protected by the United States Constitution because the statute is broad enough to reach noncommercial, cloistered personal sexual conduct between consenting adults and because it permits the proscribed conduct between persons married to each other without sanction; section 130.38 violates the rights of defendants convicted thereunder to privacy and to equal protection of the laws.

People v. Dozier


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


52 N.Y.2d 781; 417 N.E.2d 1008; 436 N.Y.S.2d 620; 1980 N.Y. LEXIS 2866


October 8, 1980, Argued  
December 18, 1980, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed in a memorandum.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Rape

The Appellate Division properly affirmed a judgment convicting defendant, upon his plea of guilty, of rape in the third degree, in violation of subdivision 2 of section 130.25 of the Penal Law, which provided that: "A male is guilty of rape in the third degree when: * * * 2. Being twenty-one years old or more, he engages in sexual intercourse with a female less than seventeen years old." The Appellate Division correctly concluded that the Legislature did not act unreasonably when it fixed the age of female consent at 17 years, as opposed to some other, presumably [***3] younger, age; with respect to other claims of unconstitutionality urged by defendant to the effect that the statute unconstitutionally discriminates on the basis of sex or that it violates due process by eliminating the mens rea element, those issues were not properly raised in the lower court and thus are beyond review in the Court of Appeals.

 

242 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Greschler v. Greschler


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


51 N.Y.2d 368; 414 N.E.2d 694; 434 N.Y.S.2d 194; 1980 N.Y. LEXIS 2896


October 9, 1980, Submitted  
November 25, 1980, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order modified, with costs to defendant, in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff wife challenged the order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which dismissed her complaint against defendant husband pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3016(b) for failure to plead fraud in detail, and held unconstitutional N.Y. Gen. Oblig. Law §5-311 (amended by 1980 N.Y. Laws ch. 281, § 19); intervenor attorney general challenged that part of the order holding § 5-311 unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The wife alleged that her husband obtained a foreign divorce decree by fraud and that an incorporated separation agreement, in which she waived her right to alimony, violated public policy. The trial court denied the husband's motion to dismiss the complaint for failure to state a cause of action. The intermediate appellate court reversed the trial court's judgment. The court modified the intermediate appellate court's order and affirmed it as modified. It agreed that the complaint failed to plead fraud in detail as required by N.Y. C.P.L.R. 3016(b). However, the court concluded that the intermediate appellate court erred in holding unconstitutional N.Y. Gen. Oblig. Law §5-311 (amended by 1980 N.Y. Laws ch. 281, § 19). It reasoned that the cause of action attacking the validity of the separation agreement, in which the wife relied upon § 5-311, was rendered moot by dismissal of the claim that jurisdiction in the foreign divorce action was procured by fraud. The court found that the foreign divorce decree did no violence to public policy, because the public policy, as reflected in N.Y. Gen. Oblig. Law §5-311 (1980), was that a wife validly could waive her right to support.


OUTCOME: The court modified the intermediate appellate court's order and, as modified, affirmed it.


HEADNOTES

Fraud -- Sufficiency of Pleading

1. In an action to set aside a Dominican Republic divorce decree and the separation agreement which it incorporated alleging that the power of attorney authorizing plaintiff's appearance in the divorce action and the separation agreement were without effect because both were procured by fraud in that the defendant represented his earnings and assets to be "a certain amount" when in fact they were "materially in excess of that so represented", and that the plaintiff's waiver of support in the separation agreement was void and unenforceable because it violated the public policy of New York, the cause of action in fraud was not pleaded with sufficient particularity and was properly dismissed, since conclusory allegations as to the defendant's misrepresentations concerning his financial status fail to meet the statutory requirement that a cause of action based upon fraud be pleaded "in detail" ( CPLR 3016, subd [b]), and, moreover, when confronted with defendant's motion to dismiss, plaintiff failed to come forth with any facts or circumstances constituting the claimed fraud as required by law. However, it was error to address the merits of the second cause of action after having dismissed the fraud claim, since plaintiff, by failing to successfully challenge the jurisdiction of the Dominican Republic court on the ground that her power of attorney was obtained by fraud, was precluded from assailing the validity of the separation agreement incorporated into the foreign decree on the ground that it contained an impermissible waiver of her right to support under New York law.

Conflict of Laws -- Comity

2. Although not required to do so, the New York courts generally will accord recognition to the judgments rendered in a foreign country under the doctrine of comity which is the equivalent of full faith and credit given by the courts to judgments of sister States, and absent some showing of fraud in the procurement of the foreign country judgment or that recognition of the judgment would do violence to some strong public policy of New York State, a party who properly appeared in the action is precluded from attacking the validity of the foreign country judgment in a collateral proceeding brought in a New York court.

Husband and Wife -- Support

3. It is not offensive to the public policy of New York State to give full recognition to a Dominican Republic divorce decree which incorporates a separation agreement providing for the plaintiff wife's waiver of her right to support in contravention of former section 5-311 of the General Obligations Law, since foreign judgments generally should be upheld unless enforcement would result in the recognition of a transaction which is inherently vicious, wicked or immoral, and shocking to the prevailing moral sense, and, as recently amended, section 5-311 of the General Obligations Law provides that either spouse may waive his or her right to support as long as he or she is not likely to become a public charge (L 1980, ch 281, § 19). Accordingly, the Legislature has indicated that the present public policy of the State is that a wife validly can waive her right to support, and plaintiff's waiver of support as incorporated in the Dominican Republic divorce decree does no violence to the prevailing policy of the State.

 

243 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Washington


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


51 N.Y.2d 214; 413 N.E.2d 1159; 433 N.Y.S.2d 745; 1980 N.Y. LEXIS 2685


October 13, 1980, Argued  
November 13, 1980, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York) that affirmed his conviction for possession of a weapon.


OVERVIEW: Police were called to the home of defendant where he was found lying on the sofa with a gun in his pocket. Defendant was arrested, and statements about where he got the gun were taken from defendant in violation of his Miranda rights. The statements were suppressed on defendant's motion, but at trial, the prosecutor questioned defendant on cross-examination about the statements as a means of impeachment. A jury found defendant guilty of criminal possession of a weapon in the third degree, the judgment of which was affirmed on appeal. Defendant sought judicial review. The court reviewed the record and concluded that defendant's statements, although obtained in violation of defendant's Miranda rights, could be used as impeachment evidence. Prosecutors could use these statements either on cross-examination of defendant or on rebuttal by calling another witness to testify that defendant had made the statements. Any dispute as to whether the statements were actually made was a question for the jury.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment upholding defendant's conviction.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Confession -- Impeachment by Use of Prior Inconsistent Statements

The defendant's credibility may be impeached by use of prior inconsistent statements which were suppressed at a pretrial hearing where the court had found, contrary to the police officer's testimony, that the defendant had not been advised of his rights ( Miranda v Arizona, 384 U.S. 436), and had not made the statements attributed to him, and it is for the jury to decide whether the defendant had made them; statements obtained in violation of a defendant's rights under Miranda, and thus inadmissible as evidence in chief, may nevertheless be used to impeach the defendant's credibility if the trustworthiness of the evidence satisfies legal standards, and there is no reason why a disputed claim of fabricated evidence, involving only the credibility of witnesses, may not be resolved in the usual manner, by submitting the matter to the jury at trial.

 

244 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Payton


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


51 N.Y.2d 169; 412 N.E.2d 1288; 433 N.Y.S.2d 61; 1980 N.Y. LEXIS 2639


September 8, 1980, Argued  
October 14, 1980, Decided



DISPOSITION: Upon reargument, following remand by the Supreme Court of the United States, order modified and case remitted to the Supreme Court, New York County, for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal defendant sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) which affirmed a verdict convicting him of murder.


OVERVIEW: Criminal defendant was convicted of murder. After an appellate court affirmed his conviction, defendant appealed. On appeal, the court was only concerned with the admissibility of a shell casing. The court modified and remitted the matter because the exclusionary rule barred evidence of a shell casing from admission into evidence unless exigent circumstances existed to justify a warrantless arrest and because respondent state had to be given an opportunity to submit proof of any such circumstances. The court concluded that the exigency question could not be resolved on the existing record in view of the district attorney's objections.


OUTCOME: The court modified and remitted the matter because the exclusionary rule barred evidence of a shell casing from admission into evidence unless exigent circumstances existed to justify a warrantless arrest, and because respondent state had to be given an opportunity to submit proof of any such circumstances.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Arrest -- Warrantless Felony Arrest on Private Premises -- Application of Exclusionary Rule

1. Considerations impacting whether or not new decisional law is to be applied retroactively do not affect the outcome of the "first case" to decide the substantive point, which must be governed by the new law. Accordingly, unless the People can show exigent circumstances necessitating the police action in issue, the exclusionary rule applies to bar evidence obtained in the course of a warrantless entry by the police on private premises to arrest defendant for a felony, police practice which was expressly permitted under statutes then in existence (Code Crim Pro, §§ 177, 178), where, on appeal following affirmance of defendant's conviction, the United States Supreme Court found such warrantless entry violative of the Fourth Amendment to the United States Constitution in the absence of exigent circumstances ( Payton v New York, 445 U.S. 573).

Crimes -- Arrest -- Warrantless Felony Arrest on Private Premises

2. If an error of law is committed by the court at a suppression hearing which directly causes the People to fail to offer potentially critical evidence, the People should be granted a new hearing. Accordingly, where the hearing court held that if the police had probable cause to arrest defendant they had authority to enter his premises without a warrant regardless of the circumstances, making it unnecessary for the People to attempt to prove that there were exigent circumstances necessitating the warrantless intrusion, but that ruling was held to be erroneous on appeal, the People must be given an opportunity to submit proof of exigent circumstances when this alternative theory has become critical to avoid suppression of the fruits of the warrantless entry; the fact that the court's erroneous ruling precluding proof of the surrounding circumstances was prompted by the District Attorney's objections is not controlling, since the People had a right to rely on statutes then in effect permitting such warrantless arrests (Code Crim Pro, §§ 177, 178), which had been routinely employed by law enforcement officers for nearly a century, and to object to evidence which was irrelevant under the theory relied upon.

 

245 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


American Ins. Asso. v. Lewis


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


50 N.Y.2d 617; 409 N.E.2d 828; 431 N.Y.S.2d 350; 1980 N.Y. LEXIS 2460


June 2, 1980, Argued  
July 8, 1980, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, with costs, and judgment granted in favor of plaintiffs declaring the 1971 amendment to subdivision 1 of section 654 of the Insurance Law unconstitutional.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs appealed the judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term in New York County (New York) that denied plaintiffs' motion for summary judgment declaring N.Y. Ins. Law 654 (1) unconstitutional and enjoining its enforcement and the judgment of that court declaring said provision was not unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, large foreign corporations who wrote property and casualty insurance in various states, brought an action challenging N.Y. Ins. Law 654 (1), which declared that certain insurers who shared in the losses of a joint underwriting association fund up to a maximum of one percent of each insurer's total net worth. Plaintiffs argued that this "capping" provision was unconstitutional, claiming that the effect of the "cap" was to impose a tax on property beyond the jurisdiction of New York, that the statute was irrational and arbitrary, and that there was a denial of equal protection through discrimination against insurance companies similarly situated. The court ruled that it was fundamental to due process jurisprudence that the taxing power of a state may not extend to tangible or intangible property having no connection or relationship to the state. The "capping" provision of N.Y. Ins. Law 654 (1) was in violation of due process because it employed the general criterion of net worth without any effect at apportioning that value. Since there was no discernable means by which to assure that the tax was focused on intrastate measure of value, the provision was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: Judgment holding that the "capping" provision of N.Y. Ins. Law 654 (1) was not a tax but a proper exercise of regulatory authority over the insurance industry was reversed, and judgment granted in favor of plaintiffs where the effect of the provision was to impose a tax on property beyond the jurisdiction of New York, and such an imposition was an unconstitutional violation of the due process clauses of the federal and state constitutions.


HEADNOTES

Insurance -- Fire Insurance -- Property Insurance Underwriting Association

Subdivision 1 of section 654 of the Insurance Law, which imposes a "cap" or maximum on each insurer's liability for losses sustained by the Fair Access to Insurance Requirements program, a pool operated by the New York Property Insurance Underwriting Association to make fire and extended coverage available to persons who otherwise would be unable to obtain adequate protection in the private insurance market, which association insurers authorized to write policies in the property and casualty field are required to join as a condition to transacting business in the State, at 1% of the insurer's total net worth without regard to what portion of the insurer's net value is attributable to business transacted in New York and how much is attributable to business transacted in other States, is unconstitutional, since the effect of the provision is to impose a tax on property beyond the jurisdiction of New York, a violation of the due process clauses of the Federal and State Constitutions. The provision is not a mere license fee, which must be reasonably related to the cost of a licensing program, since the subject funds bear not even a rough correlation to the expense to which the State is put in administering its licensing procedure or to the benefits those who make the payments receive, but are applied to the substantive funding of the program, nor is the exaction any less a general revenue raising measure because it is allocable to a particular project and its amount is dependent on the size of the subsidy necessary to sustain the financial soundness of the project it supports; it is a compulsory contribution for the purpose of defraying the cost of government, levied against an arbitrary, unapportioned percentage of out-of-State property.

 

246 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Circus Disco, Ltd. v. New York State Liquor Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


51 N.Y.2d 24; 409 N.E.2d 963; 431 N.Y.S.2d 491; 1980 N.Y. LEXIS 2530


June 3, 1980, Argued  
July 8, 1980, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs, and judgment of Supreme Court, New York County, reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant liquor license applicant sought review of a decision by the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which vacated a judgment by the trial court that annulled a determination by respondent New York State Liquor Authority (Authority). The Authority had disapproved a liquor license application, which was appealed by the applicant.


OVERVIEW: The applicant applied for a special on-premise liquor license, which was disapproved by the Authority. The applicant sought review in an Article 78 proceeding. The trial court found in favor of the applicant, which the Authority appealed. The appellate court found in favor of the Authority, which the applicant appealed. The court reversed the appellate court and held that: (1) it was error for the appellate court to resurrect a moot issue as a basis for license denial; (2) license denial because of untimely disclosure of increased expenditures was arbitrary and capricious because the funds came from proper sources and there was no evidence of willfully misleading the Authority or prejudice to the public interest; (3) consideration of the presence of schools and churches was limited to the 200 foot requirement of N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law § 64-a(7); (4) the record did not support the Authority's conclusion of inadequate soundproofing; (5) parking and traffic concerns were not within the Authority's statutory purview; and (6) letters and petitions objecting to the application, if unsupported by statutorily permitted bases, did sustain a license denial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed, with costs, the appellate court judgment that vacated the trial court's annulment of the Authority's denial of the applicant's liquor license application. The court reinstated the trial court's judgment.


HEADNOTES

Intoxicating Liquors -- Licenses -- Judicial Review of Determination of State Liquor Authority

1. It is improper for the Appellate Division to consider, as a basis for upholding denial of an on-premises liquor license by the New York State Liquor Authority, a ground not considered by the authority or argued on appeal. Accordingly, where, following an initial determination by the authority disapproving petitioner's application for an on-premises license, based in part upon a finding that petitioner's principals were not experienced managers, petitioner obtained a professional manager and so advised the authority, and, apparently satisfied on the issue, the authority, in a reconsideration decision adhering to its original determination, made no mention of the point, which was not briefed or argued at Special Term or at the Appellate Division, it was error for the Appellate Division to resurrect the issue as a permissible basis for denial of the license; the Appellate Division must judge the authority's action solely by the grounds invoked by the authority, and the authority correctly based its decision on this point on the facts existing at the time of its reconsideration.

Intoxicating Liquors -- Licenses

2. An application for an on-premises liquor license may not be denied by the State Liquor Authority because an applicant which reported one increase in the expected cost to equip and decorate the subject establishment, in compliance with rules of the authority, failed to report a later increase, where investigation shows that the money came from proper sources and there is no evidence that the applicant willfully misled the authority; denial of a license to such an applicant, which denial would, as a practical matter, destroy a $ 500,000 investment in the establishment, is so disproportionate as to constitute an abuse of discretion.

Intoxicating Liquors -- Licenses

3. With respect to authorizations to the State Liquor Authority in subdivision 1 of section 17 of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law to issue or refuse to issue licenses and in section 2 of that law to determine whether public convenience and advantage will be promoted by the issuance of licenses to traffic in alcoholic beverages, "public convenience and advantage" is not an unconstitutionally vague standard; however, its meaning cannot be divorced from the rest of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law and its generality does not confer upon the authority the power to deny a license on a finding that an applicant's operation may have an adverse impact on the community, based on grounds not found in the statute.

Intoxicating Liquors -- Licenses

4. The State Liquor Authority may not deny an on-premises liquor license because of the existence of a school and churches which are located more than 200 feet from the establishment applying for said license; the explicit and detailed language of subdivision 7 of section 64-a of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, which defines the conditions that must be met before its rule barring issuance of a license for premises located within 200 feet of a school or church is applicable, leads compellingly to the conclusion that had the Legislature intended the authority to consider the presence of schools and churches more distant than 200 feet from a licensed establishment, it would have said so.

Intoxicating Liquors -- Licenses

5. Denial of a liquor license by the State Liquor Authority on the speculation that the subject premises will be operated in violation of law is impermissible; the likelihood of future violations can furnish a basis for denial only when there are facts in the record which rationally support doing so. Accordingly, although noise generated by a licensed establishment may furnish a basis for suspending an issued license when its level is such as to affect adversely and unreasonably the repose of area residents (Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, § 106, subd 6), the authority's denial of a petitioner's license because of noise potential cannot be sustained where there is no evidence to support the authority's conclusion that adequate steps had not been taken to soundproof the premises.

Intoxicating Liquors -- Licenses

6. Since parking and traffic are essentially problems for the police, a traffic control commission or zoning authorities, and since, presumably, traffic and parking congestion are taken into account when a zoning decision is made, such factors are not matters to be separately considered by the State Liquor Authority in relation to licensing an establishment that is a permitted zoning use, nor may the zoning authorities consider the service of liquor as a reason to exclude an establishment otherwise permitted by the zoning ordinance; moreover, whenever an administrative body renders an unfavorable determination because traffic congestion will result, there must be evidence to support that conclusion. Accordingly, the State Liquor Authority improperly denied petitioner's application for an on-premises liquor license for a discotheque, a permitted zoning use, based upon a finding that the area surrounding the discotheque cannot accommodate the parking and traffic problems that will result, where said conclusion is not founded upon anything more than the authority's generalizations concerning its past experience with other discotheques and their attendant crowds of patrons.

Intoxicating Liquors -- Licenses

7. Letters from community political leaders and a petition with signatures of community residents protesting petitioner's application for an on-premises liquor license for an establishment in the community cannot sustain a determination of the State Liquor Authority, unsupported as to bases for denial of such a license permitted under the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, to reject the application; the authority cannot deny an application without a reason other than the recommendation or views thus expressed, any more than it could properly grant a license application on the basis of outside recommendation and without reference to the statutory standards.
