Urban League of Rochester, Inc. v. County of Monroe


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


49 N.Y.2d 551; 404 N.E.2d 715; 427 N.Y.S.2d 593; 1980 N.Y. LEXIS 2174


February 13, 1980, Argued  
March 27, 1980, Decided



DISPOSITION: Reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant organization sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which affirmed the trial court's dismissal of its petition on the merits, in an action brought to challenge certain governmental actions of respondent county.


OVERVIEW: The organization filed suit to challenge the county manager's authority to appoint civil service commission members without the participation of the county legislature, as well as the commission being composed of five, rather than three, members, in contravention of applicable law. The trial court's dismissal of the petition on the merits was affirmed by the intermediate court. On appeal, the organization argued that the petition should have been granted on its merits; while the county took the opposite position on the merits, and further argued that the organization lacked standing to sue. The court, declining to address the petition on the merits, reversed the intermediate court and remitted the case back to the trial court to dismiss the petition for want of standing. The court based its ruling on its conclusion that the organization lacked standing because there was no close parallelism of institutional concern between the organizational objectives of the opposing litigants, and because the organization was not entitled to be recognized as a representative unit acting on behalf of the individual members whose interest and aggrievement were such as to accord them standing.


OUTCOME: The court, without reaching the merits of the petition, reversed the order of the intermediate appellate court and remitted the matter to the trial court with directions to dismiss the proceeding for the organization's lack of standing.


HEADNOTES

Parties -- Standing

The Urban League of Rochester does not have standing to challenge the continuing authority of the Monroe County Manager to appoint members to the five-member county civil service commission without participation of the County Legislature; there is no close parallelism of institutional concern between the organizational objectives of the opposing litigants, and the Urban League cannot claim entitlement to recognition as a representative unit acting on behalf of individual members whose interest and aggrievement are such as to accord them standing, since nothing suggests that the members of the Urban League generally or in any subgroup should be classified differently from citizen-taxpayers at large in Monroe County. Legal standing on the part of any citizen-taxpayer, without more, to obtain judicial scrutiny of the nonfiscal activities of the agencies of municipal government, will not be recognized, and the Urban League has not demonstrated that it falls within any of the categories of litigants whose right to standing have so far been recognized.

 

268 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Aldens, Inc. v. Tully


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


49 N.Y.2d 525; 404 N.E.2d 703; 427 N.Y.S.2d 580; 1980 N.Y. LEXIS 2172


February 4, 1980, Argued  
March 25, 1980, Decided



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayer sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals (New York), which affirmed the trial court's determination that respondent state had properly determined deficiencies arising from the taxpayer's failure to collect and remit local use taxes on goods sold to customers in areas within the state other than the four areas in which the taxpayer's offices were located.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer was a foreign mail-order business that was licensed to business within the state. The taxpayer maintained offices and employees at four different locations in the state. The court affirmed the judgments of the appellate court and the trial court finding that it was neither an undue burden on the taxpayer nor a deprivation of its right to due process for the state to require the taxpayer to collect local use taxes on goods sold and delivered by it to purchasers throughout the state, even if its only contact with the locality was by mail and common carrier. The court concluded that N.Y. Tax Law § 1254(a) required the taxpayer to collect and remit local use taxes on goods sold to customers anywhere in the state and that the taxpayer's maintenance of four offices was a sufficient nexus with the state for the state to ask a return in the form of tax collection for the privilege of doing business enjoyed by the taxpayer.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments of the appellate court and the trial court finding that the taxpayer was required by N.Y. Tax Law § 1254(a) to collect and remit local use taxes on goods sold to customers within the state but outside the taxing units in which the taxpayer maintained a place of business.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Sales and Use Taxes -- Local Use Taxes -- Mail-Order Sales

1. When a foreign mail-order business corporation is licensed to do business within the State and, through a wholly owned subsidiary, maintains offices and employees doing business at four different localities in the State, it is neither an undue burden on it in violation of the commerce clause of the Constitution of the United States (art I, § 8), nor a deprivation of its right to due process (US Const, 14th Amdt, § 1), for the State to require the corporation to collect local, as distinguished from State, use taxes on goods sold and delivered by it to purchasers at every locality within the State (Tax Law, § 1254, subd [a]), despite the fact that petitioner's only contact with the localities in which it does not maintain offices is by mail and common carrier, or to require that it pay the local use taxes that were not paid during the period prior to its notification that it had an obligation to collect such taxes.

Taxation -- Sales and Use Taxes -- Local Use Taxes

2. The State Tax Commission is without authority, by promulgation of a Model County or City Sales Tax Resolution or otherwise, to amend or to contravene the unambiguous language of subdivision (a) of section 1254 of the Tax Law, which sets out the obligation of a vendor to collect local use taxes, nor would such a resolution foreclose the Tax Commission from collecting taxes as required by the statute on any theory of estoppel.

 

269 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Quinton A.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


49 N.Y.2d 328; 402 N.E.2d 126; 425 N.Y.S.2d 788; 1980 N.Y. LEXIS 2077


January 3, 1980, Argued  
February 20, 1980, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged, on constitutional grounds, an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a finding of the family court that he had committed acts, which, if committed by an adult, would have constituted robbery in the first degree and burglary in the second degree.


OVERVIEW: Appellant juvenile sought relief from a judgment that affirmed a finding that he had committed acts, which, if committed by an adult, would have constituted the crimes of robbery in the first degree and burglary in the second degree, which were both designated felonies. Since he was found to have caused serious physical injury to a 73-year-old woman, at the subsequent dispositional he was ordered to restrictive placement. Statements of a codefendant were used to implicate appellant. On appeal, the court held that N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act, §§ 753(1) and 753-a(2) was constitutional, because it did not involve discrimination against a suspect class; thus, a strict scrutiny standard of review was inapplicable. However, the family court erred in admitting statements of codefendant into evidence to be used against appellant; therefore, the court reversed the adjudication against the appellant on that basis.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the lower court, which affirmed the family court's finding that appellant had committed acts which, if committed by an adult, would have constituted crimes. The court found that the family court erred in admitting statements of appellant's codefendant into evidence to be used against him.


HEADNOTES

Infants -- Juvenile Delinquents -- Mandatory Restrictive Placement

1. Subdivision 2-a of section 753-a of the Family Court Act, which provides that where a youth between 14 and 15 years of age is found to have committed a designated felony act involving the infliction of serious physical injury upon a person who is 62 years of age or more, the Family Court is required to order restrictive placement of the juvenile in a secure facility operated by the Division for Youth, is constitutional; since Family Court may not order restrictive placement until after it affords a juvenile a statutorily required dispositional hearing on notice, the statute fully comports with procedural due process strictures, and it may properly be taken that the Legislature concluded that the increase in juvenile crime and in crimes of violence against senior citizens were causally related, and it is rational to further conclude that juvenile offenders who perpetrate crimes of violence on older persons, most exposed to depredation and least able to protect themselves, may properly be classified for treatment purposes in a different category from other juvenile offenders, and that their rehabilitation may best be attempted, and hopefully accomplished, in the controlled setting of restrictive placement. Moreover, it was rational for the Legislature to have afforded juveniles who commit crimes of violence against the elderly treatment disparate from those who perpetrate crime against the general populace, and the Legislature was entitled to take into account not only the rehabilitative needs of juveniles but the special problems juvenile crime poses to the elderly as well; equal protection does not demand that classifications be ideal, and where the Legislature undertakes to act in areas fraught with rehabilitative and sociological uncertainties, courts are reluctant to intervene.

Infants -- Juvenile Delinquents -- Use of Confessions at Hearing

2. It was reversible error, at a fact-finding hearing in connection with the commission of a designated felony act involving the infliction of serious physical injury upon a person 62 years of age or more, to admit inculpatory statements made by petitioner and his alleged accomplice where the court expressly stated that the accomplice's detailed statement could be used to supply critical details absent from the statement of petitioner, the accomplice having admitted taking $ 15 from the complainant and striking her, and petitioner having admitted taking an undisclosed sum of money from a woman but having denied inflicting any injuries upon the victim; a confession may generally be considered only against its maker.

Constitutional Law -- Due Process of Law -- Procedural Due Process

3. The essence of procedural due process is that a person must be afforded notice and an opportunity to be heard before government may deprive him of liberty or a recognized property interest.

Statutes -- Presumption of Constitutionality

4. It is presumed that the Legislature has investigated and found the existence of a situation showing or indicating the need for or desirability of legislation enacted by it.

 

270 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Fantis Foods, Inc. v. Standard Importing Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


49 N.Y.2d 317; 402 N.E.2d 122; 425 N.Y.S.2d 783; 1980 N.Y. LEXIS 2076


January 7, 1980, Argued  
February 14, 1980, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Third party defendant dairy cooperative sought review from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court of New York County (New York), which denied its motion to dismiss the third party complaint of third party plaintiff importer.


OVERVIEW: The dairy cooperative sold feta cheese to the importer. The dairy cooperative was located in Greece, had no offices or assets in New York, and was not licensed to do business in New York. The feta cheese was sold to the importer and was weighed and delivered in Greece. After it was weighed, the dairy cooperative was to ship the cheese to Chicago. Instead, the dairy cooperative sold the same cheese to the importer's competitor. The bills of lading were altered, but the importer was able to get possession of 300 barrels of a 1200 barrel total. The competitor sued the importer for diversion of the 300 barrels of cheese. The importer filed a third party action against the dairy cooperative for conversion of the cheese. The dairy cooperative contended that there was no personal jurisdiction over it and that the contract made the Greek courts the exclusive forum for disputes between it and the importer. The court agreed with the dairy cooperative and noted that the claim was essentially one for breach of contract and any conversion damage was consequential and, thus, not a sufficient basis for jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's order and ordered the importer's third party claims dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- Jurisdiction -- Breach of Contract

1. "Long-arm" jurisdiction under CPLR 302 (subd [a], par 3), which confers upon the courts in this State personal jurisdiction over a nondomiciliary who commits a tortious act without the State causing injury to person or property within the State, is limited to a cause of action arising out of such tortious conduct. Accordingly, where two distinct claims were pleaded against defendant, a Greek exporting concern, by a New York corporation, one for the conversion, in Greece or on the high seas en route from Greece, of goods which, it is claimed, had been accepted by the buyer and were to be shipped by the seller from Greece to Chicago via New York, and another cause of action for breach of the contract to sell and ship said goods to the buyer, it was error not to dismiss the contract claim for lack of personal jurisdiction over the nondomiciliary defendant; while a breach of contract may give rise to a separate tort liability, the two are conceptually distinct liabilities, and the cause of action for breach of contract is conceptually unrelated to the tortious nature of the act which constitutes the breach.

Courts -- Jurisdiction -- Tort

2. Under CPLR 302 (subd [a], par 3, cl [ii]), which authorizes New York courts to exercise personal jurisdiction over a nondomiciliary who commits a tortious act without the State causing injury to person or property within the State if he expects or should reasonably expect the act to have consequences in the State and derives substantial revenue from interstate or international commerce, the mere residence or domicile of the injured party within the State is not sufficient predicate for jurisdiction, which must be based upon a more direct injury within the State and a closer expectation of consequences within the State than the indirect financial loss resulting from the fact that the injured person resides or is domiciled there. Accordingly, an action for loss of profits brought by a New York corporate buyer against a Greek exporting concern, based upon a conversion of the buyer's goods which allegedly occurred while those goods were still in Greece or while they were on the high seas en route to Chicago, the ultimate destination of the goods, was dismissed for lack of personal jurisdiction over the nondomiciliary defendant since the only connection between the claimed conversion and any injury or foreseeable consequences in New York is the fact that the buyer is incorporated in New York and maintains offices here, too remote a connection with New York to satisfy the requirements of CPLR 302 (subd [a], par 3, cl [ii]).

Damages -- Conversion -- Loss of Profits

3. The usual measure of damages for conversion is the value of the property at the time and place of conversion, plus interest; profits are generally disallowed, although they may be recoverable if they may reasonably be expected to follow from the conversion.

 

271 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Sisters of St. Joseph v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


49 N.Y.2d 429; 403 N.E.2d 150; 426 N.Y.S.2d 444; 1980 N.Y. LEXIS 2117


January 8, 1980, Argued  
February 14, 1980, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order modified, with costs to appellants, and the case remitted to Supreme Court, Kings County, for a hearing in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged the judgment from the Supreme Court, Kings County (New York), which granted respondent religious-based healthcare charity's motion for summary judgment. The city had sought declaratory relief, arguing that the charity's property leased to another charity was subject to real property taxation pursuant to N.Y. Real. Prop. Tax Law § 421(2).


OVERVIEW: The charity, which had leased some of its property to another charity, sought to claim all of its property as qualifying for tax-exempt status under the state's real property tax laws. The court analyzed N.Y. Real Prop. Tax Law § 421 and held that: 1) the charity was liable for the portion of its property leased to another charity that was not used exclusively for tax-exempt purposes, 2) no tax loophole would be established, for the legislature could not have intended its express mandate to be so easily circumvented, 3) the imposition of real property taxes, under these appropriate circumstances, did not infringe upon the charity's right to practice freely its religion; and 4) once the legislature granted the charitable tax-exempt status, it had the power to restrict that benefit without offending the state constitution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part, reversed in part, modified the order, with costs to the city, and remitted the case to the trial court for a hearing on the issue of whether the charity's carrying, maintenance, and depreciation charges of the property exceeded the rental income.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Exemptions

1. The Legislature intended that property owned by a religious and charitable organization be subject to taxation pursuant to subdivision 2 of section 421 of the Real Property Tax Law if the rental income derived from such property exceeds the carrying, maintenance and depreciation charges thereon, even if the property is used exclusively for tax-exempt purposes by the lessee thereof; moreover, the owner religious and charitable organization is liable for real property taxes under the statute, where the lessee religious and charitable organization subleases the property to a third religious and charitable organization for an amount exceeding the permissible charges and said lessee, rather than the owner, is the recipient of the rental payments, even when the owner organization itself derives no pecuniary benefit from the demised premises and the property is used exclusively for tax-exempt purposes.

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Religion

2. The imposition of taxes, pursuant to subdivision 2 of section 421 of the Real Property Tax Law, upon property owned by plaintiff religious and charitable organization where the rental income derived from such property exceeds the carrying, maintenance and depreciation charges thereon, even though the property is used exclusively for tax-exempt purposes, does not infringe upon plaintiff's right to freely exercise its religion (US Const, 1st Amdt; NY Const, art I, § 3); indeed, the very existence of the lease itself militates against a finding that the property in question was to be used for the practice of plaintiff's religion.

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Religion

3. Subdivision 2 of section 421 of the Real Property Tax Law, which imposes a tax on property owned by a religious and charitable organization if the rental income derived from such property exceeds the carrying, maintenance and depreciation charges thereon, even if the property is used exclusively for tax-exempt purposes by the lessee thereof, does not run counter to the mandate contained in section 1 of article XVI of the New York Constitution that tax exemptions may be altered or repealed, except those exempting real or personal property used exclusively for religious, educational or charitable purposes that is owned by any corporation or association organized or conducted exclusively for one or more of such purposes and not operating for profit, since, when section 1 of article XVI became effective, the applicable tax statutes as interpreted by the courts of this State did not allow for an exemption where a tax-exempt organization leased its property to another such organization. Accordingly, subdivision 2 of section 421 of the Real Property Tax Law works to bestow upon tax-exempt organizations an additional benefit which is not constitutionally mandated; it is inconceivable that the Legislature, having granted an additional benefit, does not have the power to restrict that benefit without offending the State Constitution.

Kupferman v. New York State Board of Social Welfare

274 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Solnick v. Whalen


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


49 N.Y.2d 224; 401 N.E.2d 190; 425 N.Y.S.2d 68; 1980 N.Y. LEXIS 2035


December 12, 1979, Argued  
January 8, 1980, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff nursing home operators brought an action against defendant state official for a declaration that any attempted recoupment of alleged overpayments were illegal by reason of a lack of due process and for an order enjoining recoupment without a full adversary due process hearing. The Appellate Division (New York) held that the action was not time-barred, and directed a hearing to recoup overpayment.


OVERVIEW: The court held that a critical question was which statute of limitations determined the time within which the declaratory judgment action must have been brought. The court held that there was another form of judicial proceeding which was available to the nursing home owners for resolution of their challenge to the downward revision of their reimbursement rates. The court held that the nursing home owners could have brought an action to review the determination made by the agency. However, the court held that this action would have been time barred when the declaratory judgment action was commenced. The court held that the declaratory judgment action to challenge a determination adjusting Medicaid reimbursement rates for lack of procedural due process was time-barred because it was brought more than four months after notification of the rejection of its appeal from the audit on which the adjustments were based and more than four months after notification of its adjusted reimbursement rates.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment with costs, and dismissed the action.


HEADNOTES

Limitation of Actions -- Declaratory Judgment Action

In order to determine whether there is a period of limitation prescribed by law for the commencement of a particular declaratory judgment action, it is necessary to examine the substance of that action to identify the relationship out of which the claim arises and the relief sought; if these factors reveal that the rights of the parties sought to be stabilized in the declaratory judgment action are, or have been, open to resolution through a form of proceeding for which a specific limitation period is statutorily provided, then that period limits the time for the commencement of the declaratory judgment action, and, if no other form of proceeding exists for the resolution of the claims tendered in the action for declaratory relief, the six-year "catch-all" Statute of Limitations of CPLR 213 (subd 1) will then be applicable. Accordingly, a declaratory judgment action brought to challenge a determination of the Commissioner of Health adjusting Medicaid reimbursement rates for an individual nursing home facility for lack of due process attending the procedure by which rate reductions were determined, is time-barred when it is brought more than four months after plaintiffs, the owners and operators of the home, were notified that their appeal from the audit on which the adjustments were based had been rejected and more than four months after they were notified of the adjusted reimbursement rates set by defendants, since the challenge would have been barred by the four-month limitation period for a CPLR article 78 proceeding ( CPLR 217), an alternate form of judicial proceeding available to plaintiffs; the fact that the challenge to defendants' determination is stated in terms of a constitutional objection, violation of procedural due process, does not serve to make an article 78 proceeding, the customary vehicle for review of administrative determinations, unavailable, nor does the proscription against the use of article 78 proceedings to challenge the constitutionality of general legislative enactments cover situations such as this in which the attack is addressed to individualized rates established for a particular litigant, a determination more accurately classified as administrative than legislative.

 

275 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Cooper v. Morin


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


49 N.Y.2d 69; 399 N.E.2d 1188; 424 N.Y.S.2d 168; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 2476


October 11, 1979, Argued  
December 19, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order modified, with costs to plaintiffs, in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a class of female county jail inmates, sought relief against defendant county manager for violations of the United States and New York Constitutions. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York) denied declaratory and injunctive relief and attorneys' fees but ordered the institution of a system of contact visitation. The parties appealed.


OVERVIEW: The inmates, 90 percent of whom were pretrial detainees, were permitted short visits with no physical contact. Penalties for infractions of jail rules were not specifically related to the offense. The inmates claimed that they had a constitutional right to contact visitation and to a system relating penalties to offenses and sought attorneys' fees. The county agreed that contact visits were desirable but claimed that they would increase costs and argued that disciplinary penalties were not arbitrary. The court held that the unavailability of contact visits was rationally related to a legitimate governmental objective and did not violate federal due process or equal protection. However, under the balancing test required by N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6, the county's economic considerations did not outweigh pretrial detainees' rights to maintain relationships. The inmates did not show that the jail's disciplinary rules established a pattern of abuse, especially as hearings were available to protect against arbitrary action. The inmates were not entitled to attorneys' fees under federal law, and the trial court did not abuse its discretion in refusing to award fees under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 909.


OUTCOME: The court modified the order of the lower court so as to provide that a program of contact visits of reasonable duration had to be instituted within a reasonable period of time. The court otherwise affirmed the order of the lower court.


HEADNOTES

Prisons and Prisoners -- Contact Visitation for Pretrial Detainees

1. Although pretrial detainees are not entitled to contact visits of reasonable duration as a matter of Federal constitutional right, since the prohibition against contact visits, which is related to security concerns, is, as a matter of Federal due process, reasonably related to a legitimate governmental objective, and since the Federal equal protection clause does not mandate that pretrial detainees be granted contact visitation privileges inasmuch as there are rational bases for differentiating between sentenced prisoners and persons detained while awaiting trial, they are entitled to contact visitation of reasonable duration pursuant to section 6 of article I of the New York Constitution, which states that no person shall be denied life, liberty or property without due process of law; the only legitimate purpose for pretrial detention is to assure the presence of the detainee for trial, and when so fundamental a right as the maintenance by pretrial detainees of relationships with family and friends is involved the security measures adopted must be both reasonable and necessary to the maintenance of security. The imposition on the detainee's rights of a system of noncontact visitation is unreasonable when sustained only by the argument that contact visitation requires additional expenditures and personnel.

Prisons and Prisoners -- Disciplinary Rules

2. A constitutional challenge to the disciplinary rules of a county jail must fail where it is shown only that in two instances severe penalties have been imposed where sentenced inmates showed disrespect for, or disobedience of, prison personnel; before a penalty is imposed, an inmate can obtain a hearing as to the offense, and detainees are thus adequately protected from arbitrary action.

Attorney and Client -- Attorneys Fees

3. In a class action brought on behalf of all women inmates of a county jail alleging six causes of action under the Federal Civil Rights Act (US Code, tit 42, § 1983) and five causes of action based upon claimed violations of the Federal and State Constitutions, plaintiffs are not entitled to attorneys fees under the Civil Rights Attorney's Fees Awards Act of 1976 (US Code, tit 42, § 1988) or under CPLR 909, since they have not prevailed on any of their Civil Rights Act causes of action, and since it was not an abuse of discretion for the Trial Judge to conclude that, despite the fact that plaintiffs prevailed on their State constitutional claim, justice did not require recovery of the attorneys fee ( CPLR 909).

Courts -- Court of Appeals -- Power to Reach Claims Raised in Complaint

4. Where a complaint presents both State and Federal constitutional claims, the Court of Appeals may consider the State claims although the trial court and the Appellate Division did not.

Prisons and Prisoners -- Disciplinary Rules -- Penalties for Violation

5. The penalties for violations of prison regulations cannot be grossly disproportionate to the offense.

 

276 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Salla v. County of Monroe


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


48 N.Y.2d 514; 399 N.E.2d 909; 423 N.Y.S.2d 878; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 2425


October 15, 1979, Argued  
November 29, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant County of Monroe (county) appealed the order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the order that granted summary judgment to plaintiffs, an out-of-state corporation and its employees, found N.Y. Lab. Law § 222 unconstitutional, and enjoined the county from enforcing § 222 by terminating the corporation's contract for failure to comply with the statute.


OVERVIEW: The out-of-state corporation was awarded a contract to construct a sanitary sewer line for the county, but the corporation terminated its employees, who were Pennsylvania residents, because the corporation found it necessary to accede to the county's insistence on strict enforcement of § 222, which mandated preferential employment to New York citizens on public works projects. The corporation and its employees filed an action seeking a declaration that § 222 was unconstitutional and an injunction against the county. The trial court granted summary judgment to the corporation and its employees, found § 222 unconstitutional, and enjoined the county from enforcing § 222 by terminating the corporation's contract for failure to comply with the statute. The appellate division affirmed the order, and the county appealed. The court affirmed and held that § 222 offended the privileges and immunities clause of U.S. Const. art. IV, § 2. The state's interest in creating such a disparity in treatment did not outweigh the constitutional concern for the right of a citizen on one state to pursue his vocation in another.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that granted summary judgment to the out-of-state corporation and its employees, held unconstitutional the New York statute that mandated preferential employment of New York citizens, and enjoined the county from enforcing the statute by terminating the corporation's contract for failure to comply with the statute.


HEADNOTES

Labor -- Preference in Employment -- Violation of Privileges and Immunities Clause

1. A resident of one State is constitutionally entitled to travel to another State for purposes of employment free from discriminatory restrictions in favor of State residents imposed by the other State; when such right, protected by the privileges and immunities clause of the United States Constitution (art IV, § 2), which requires that each State accord substantial equality of treatment to the citizens of other States, is allegedly infringed by a State statute, a court must first determine whether perfectly valid independent reasons for the disparity of treatment exist, and then it must also decide whether, quantitatively or qualitatively, the degree of discrimination bears a truly close relationship to the reasons assigned. Accordingly, section 222 of the Labor Law, which provides that in the construction of public works, the State, its subdivisions and the contractors they employ must give preference in employment to citizens of the State of New York who have been residents of the State for at least 12 consecutive months immediately prior to the commencement of their employment, offends the privileges and immunities clause, since it creates a potentially absolute barrier to out-of-State public works contractors who seek to market their skills in a legitimate manner within the borders of the State, in conflict with the national policy of economic unity, and since the State's interest in allocating its funds to their beneficial owners, its citizens, even if it could be achieved to any degree by such limitation, is insignificant in comparison.

Appeal -- Court of Appeals

2. Although it may be well-settled law that corporations are not "citizens" within the meaning of the privileges and immunities clause (US Const, art IV, § 2), and thus cannot seek protection under the clause, the Court of Appeals need not address the consequences, if any, of this doctrine in a case, brought by a Pennsylvania corporation and two of its employees, Pennsylvania residents, alleging that a New York statute creating a preference in employment on public works contracts for New York citizens (Labor Law, § 222) violates that clause, where none of the parties raised the issue below.

 

277 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Illardo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


48 N.Y.2d 408; 399 N.E.2d 59; 423 N.Y.S.2d 470; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 2410


October 10, 1979, Argued  
November 27, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal defendant challenged an order of the Erie County Court (New York) which reversed an order of the city court that had dismissed an information brought by plaintiff that charged defendant with obscenity in the second degree under N.Y. Penal Law § 235.05.


OVERVIEW: Criminal defendant was charged in an information brought by plaintiff for the knowing promotion of obscene material under N.Y. Penal Law § 235.05(1) for selling a magazine to a plainclothes police officer. Defendant alleged that the affirmative defenses provided in N.Y. Penal Law § 235.15(1), (2) were constitutionally vague. Further, defendant contended that because the affirmative defenses could not be severed from the remainder of the statute, the defect rendered the proscription in N.Y. Penal Law § 235.05(1) invalid as well. The court held that the defenses in N.Y. Penal Law § 235.15(1), (2) complied with the dictates of due process. The court affirmed the county court's determination that the information against defendant should stand.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the county court's determination that the information brought by plaintiff against defendant for obscenity in the second degree should stand because the affirmative defenses were not constitutionally infirm and complied with the dictates of due process.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Obscenity -- Validity of Affirmative Defense

1. The constitutional requisite that a statute be informative on its face does not preclude the Legislature from using ordinary terms to express ideas that find adequate interpretation in everyday usage and understanding; it is enough that the language used conveys sufficiently definite warnings as to proscribed conduct when measured by common understanding and practices. Accordingly, and considering the presumption of constitutionality overlaying duly enacted statutes, subdivision 1 of section 235.15 of the Penal Law, which provides that it is an affirmative defense in any obscenity prosecution that the allegedly obscene material was disseminated to persons or institutions having scientific, educational, governmental or other similar justification for possessing such material, is not so imprecise as to render it void for vagueness; the words "scientific", "educational" and "governmental" are within the compass of the ordinary citizen and the final phrase, "other similar justification", though susceptible of a wide interpretation, is designed to save the Legislature from spelling out in advance every contingency in which the statute could apply, employing the principal of ejusdem generis in relying on the courts to give content to the phrase where foreseeable circumstances are too numerous or varied for particular enumeration, and is, under that canon of statutory construction, limited in its effect by the specific terms that precede it; although there may be imaginable instances in which some difficulty may be encountered in determining on which side of the line a particular fact situation falls, this alone does not render a statute's language impermissibly vague, and this reasoning is certainly no less applicable when the language defines not a criminal offense, but an affirmative defense.

Crimes -- Obscenity -- Validity of Affirmative Defense

2. The equal protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution is not violated by statutory distinctions so long as the legislative classification is not arbitrary and can be said to bear a fair and substantial relation to some manifest evil reasonably perceived by the Legislature; if the classification has some reasonable basis, it does not offend the Constitution simply because it lacks mathematical nicety or because in practice it results in some inequality. Accordingly, subdivision 2 of section 235.15 of the Penal Law, which provides an affirmative defense in a prosecution for obscenity for certain nonmanagerial employees of motion picture theatres, is not fatally underinclusive in violation of the equal protection clause for failure to include bookstore employees, since the statute might be seen as addressing itself to the phase of the problem which seems most acute to the legislative mind; on this basis, and bearing in mind the heavy burden that an appellant must shoulder to demonstrate a statute's invalidity, it cannot be said, without more, that the classification is unconstitutional, since, so long as it has a rational basis, it is not for the courts, but for those who enacted the statute, to choose the particular course taken to accomplish an end.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Affirmative Defenses -- Standing

3. The fact that a defendant has not yet proceeded to trial on a criminal charge does not divest him of standing to raise the constitutionality of the statutory defenses to such charge. Accordingly, defendant, who is charged with promotion of obscene material (Penal Law, § 235.05, subd 1), may raise the constitutionality of section 235.15 of the Penal Law, which provides affirmative defenses to such a charge, since, the moment the prosecution against him was mounted, defendant gained the right to rely on any legitimate defense, including those incorporated in section 235.15.

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Speech -- Obscene Material

4. Where First Amendment rights are involved, to forestall the possibility of inhibiting the exercise of free speech, a statute's imprecision may not be lightly overlooked, but, since obscene matter falls outside the scope of the First Amendment to the United States Constitution, the amendment plays, at best, a peripheral role in the scrutiny of a statute prohibiting the promotion of such material; moreover, where the statute deals not with a proscription itself, but, instead, with affirmative defenses, statutory provisions which become relevant only after an arrest is made and charges are filed, any uncertainty in its terms is far less likely to be an inducement to irresponsible law enforcement, which the constitutional requisite that a statute be informative on its face, in addition to ensuring that citizens can conform their conduct to the dictates of law, serves to avoid.

 

278 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Corning v. Laurel Hollow


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


48 N.Y.2d 348; 398 N.E.2d 537; 422 N.Y.S.2d 932; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 2405


September 11, 1979, Argued  
November 21, 1979, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargued November 12, 1979.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A couple brought suit against plaintiff village officials after they conducted warrantless searches of the couple's home. After the couple's suit was dismissed, the officials sought to recover their legal fees and costs from defendant village. The officials challenged the decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York) in the village's favor.


OVERVIEW: The couple brought a civil rights suit against the officials, who were initially represented by the county district attorney but later retained private counsel. The court held that they were not entitled to recover the expenses in hiring counsel, stating that allowing such a recovery would enable all public officials to employ counsel whenever it was deemed desirable and thus unilaterally empower them to create municipal debt. Moreover, it noted, their counsel performed no services for the village. It rejected the argument that the officials were acting as agents of the village, stating that they were the very people in whom the decision to search the home was vested and were more properly characterized as principals. Furthermore, it noted, N.Y. Const. art. VIII, § 1 prohibited the gift of public funds to assist a purely private purpose. Because the interests of the village were not implicated by the lawsuit and because there was no statute authorizing the village to provide the officials a defense, the state constitution prohibited it from reimbursing the officials for any expenses incurred in defending themselves in their personal capacities.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's decision.


HEADNOTES

 Municipal Corporations -- Reimbursement of Village Officials for Legal Fees

A municipality may expend its funds only to meet its lawful obligations incurred as a result of the performance of its governmental function and, conversely, it may not compensate a person who performs an act which the government had no duty to undertake. Accordingly, former high-ranking officials of a village are not entitled to reimbursement from the village for their costs, disbursements and legal fees incurred in asserting a successful defense to a civil rights action, to which the village was not a party, brought against them as a result of acts performed in their official capacities and based on the implementation of a policy over which they alone had control, since there is no principal involved to whom the officials, as agents, may look for reimbursement and, more fundamentally, without the benefit of authorizing legislation, reimbursement by the village of these expenses would constitute a gift of public funds for a purely private purpose, a matter expressly forbidden by our Constitution (NY Const, art VIII, § 1); the public owes no duty to defend or even aid in the defense of such a charge, although the municipality does have the power to enact an ordinance empowering it to defend its officials who may, in the future, be charged with violating the law in the performance of their duties, since such a considered policy decision would raise no constitutional objections, for the cost of the defense would simply be considered additional remuneration.

 

279 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lavender


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


48 N.Y.2d 334; 398 N.E.2d 530; 422 N.Y.S.2d 924; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 2403


October 10, 1979, Argued  
November 20, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed and the remaining counts of the indictment dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, the president and sole stockholder of a corporation in the home improvement work business, appealed from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which upheld his conviction on one count of abandonment of a home improvement contract or project in violation of New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § B32-358.0, struck his sentence of imprisonment, and affirmed his fine.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, the president and sole stockholder of a corporation in the home improvement work business, was convicted of abandonment of, or willful failure to perform, without justification, a home improvement contract, in violation of New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § B32-358.0, a misdemeanor under § B32-365.0, and was sentenced to six months' imprisonment and a fine of $ 1,000. On appeal, the sentence was vacated but the conviction and fine were affirmed, and the defendant sought review. The court held that the provision was directed not at the fraud involved in receiving money on a contract with the present intention not to perform, but solely at the failure to perform the services necessary to carry out the contract, and violated U.S. Const. amend. XIII and 42 U.S.C.S. § 1994.


OUTCOME: The order that affirmed the defendant's conviction was reversed and the remaining counts of the indictment were dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Contracts -- Home Improvement Contract

1. Subdivision 1 of section B32-358.0 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York prohibits abandonment of or willful failure to perform, without justification, any home improvement contract or project engaged in or undertaken by a contractor, violation of which prohibition is a misdemeanor (Administrative Code, § B32-365.0). Accordingly, where evidence does not connect defendant, the president of an incorporated home improvement business, with either of two home improvement contracts, either at the inception of those contracts or later, there is insufficient evidence to present an issue for the trier of fact concerning a violation of section B32-358.0 of the Administrative Code by defendant, but with respect to a third home improvement contract, settled after the matter was before the District Attorney's staff, where there is evidence that defendant had twice visited the house and promised the homeowner that the work would be completed and that he had signed a false certificate of completion, there was clearly sufficient evidence to present a fact issue.

Constitutional Law -- Involuntary Servitude

2. When the State undertakes to deal with fraud involved in contracts for labor or services, it must respect the constitutional and statutory command that it may not make failure to labor in discharge of a debt any part of a crime; it may not directly or indirectly command involuntary servitude, even if it was voluntarily contracted for. Accordingly, section B32-358.0 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York, which prohibits an abandonment of or willful failure to perform, without justification, a home improvement contract, violation of which is a misdemeanor (Administrative Code, § B32-365.0), is unconstitutional because it violates the proscription of the Thirteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution against involuntary servitude and the acts of Congress in furtherance thereof (US Code, tit 42, § 1994; tit 18, § 1581, subd [a]), since the provision is not directed at the fraud involved in receiving money in relation to such a contract with the present intention not to perform, but solely at the failure to perform the services necessary to carry out the contract.

 

280 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Fullilove v. Beame


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


48 N.Y.2d 376; 398 N.E.2d 765; 423 N.Y.S.2d 144; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 2407; 22 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P30,572


September 5, 1979, Argued  
November 20, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff construction contractors filed a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding against defendants, mayor, deputy mayor, and others. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) affirmed a special term's conversion of the proceeding into a declaratory judgment action, grant of summary judgment to the contractors, and enjoinment of the enforcement of executive order regulations. The mayor and others appealed.


OVERVIEW: The special term declared unconstitutional, illegal, and unenforceable rules and regulations promulgated by mayoral executive orders relating to equal employment contract compliance for construction contractors. The rules and regulations impermissibly required actual employment of persons solely by virtue of their status as minority group members and compelled a quota, however vaguely defined, rather than merely increasing the pool of eligibles for employment. The court agreed, holding that no executive authority existed to initiate affirmative action without legislative authority. The state legislature and the state city council were required to condemn discriminatory hiring practices, but nowhere in the law had the city council prescribed affirmative action to redress discriminatory effects. A distinction was found between the legislative policy of prohibiting discrimination and the mayoral policy of mandating employment "percentages." Similarly there was a difference between permitting employers to voluntarily adopt an affirmative action program and mandating that it be done involuntarily under threat of legal sanctions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the appellate division, which affirmed the decision of the special term.


HEADNOTES

Civil Rights -- Discrimination in Employment -- Affirmative Action

Although the executive clearly has the power and obligation to implement programs designed to prohibit discriminatory hiring practices, and, indeed, the State Legislature has stated that voluntary use of a State-approved affirmative action plan by private employers shall not be an unlawful discriminatory practice (Executive Law, § 296, subd 2), there is a vast difference between permitting employers to voluntarily adopt an affirmative action program and mandating by executive order that this be done involuntarily under threat of legal sanctions, and there is also a distinction between executive affirmative action which would merely enlarge the pool of persons eligible for employment based on discrimination-free selection and affirmative action in hiring practices; under our governmental system, the central feature of which is distribution of powers, the executive does not have the authority to initiate such affirmative action without legislative authorization.

 

281 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cruz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


48 N.Y.2d 419; 399 N.E.2d 513; 423 N.Y.S.2d 625; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 2411


October 11, 1979, Argued  
November 20, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent appealed an order from the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department, (New York), in which the court found that N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1192 was unconstitutional because it was vague.


OVERVIEW: A judgment finding that N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1192 was unconstitutional because it was vague was reversed. In reversing the lower court's decision, the appeals court held that the Constitution required only reasonable precision. It did not impose impossible standards. Thus, if the general class of offenses to which a statute was directed was plainly within its terms, the statute would not be struck down as vague. The appeals court continued and held that a statute, which employed terms having an accepted meaning, "long recognized in law and life" could not be said to be so vague and indefinite as to afford defendant insufficient notice of what was prohibited or inadequate guidelines for adjudication even though there could be an element of degree in the definition as to which estimates might differ. According to the appeals court, although § 1192 did not discuss whether a driver was intoxicated in terms of degree, it had long been recognized that the standard for intoxication was where the driver was incapable of employing physical and mental abilities which he was expected to possess in order to operate a vehicle as a reasonable, prudent person.


OUTCOME: The appeals court reversed the finding that the driving under the influence statute was unconstitutional because the statute was not vague where the statute employed a long recognized meaning as to the term intoxication.


HEADNOTES

 Constitutional Law -- Due Process of Law -- Criminal Statutes

1. It is a fundamental requirement of due process that a criminal statute must be stated in terms which are reasonably definite so that a person of ordinary intelligence will know what the law prohibits or commands; this concept insures that the defendant will receive adequate warning of what the law requires so that he may act lawfully, and it serves to prevent arbitrary and discriminatory enforcement by requiring boundaries sufficiently distinct for police, Judges and juries to fairly administer the law. The Constitution only requires reasonable precision, and does not impose impossible standards; accordingly, if the general class of offenses to which the statute is directed is plainly within its terms, the statute will not be struck down as vague, even though marginal cases could be put where doubt might rise.

Motor Vehicles -- Operating Vehicle While under Influence

2. Subdivision 1 of section 1192 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law, which prohibits driving while the ability to operate a motor vehicle is impaired by the consumption of alcohol, and subdivision 3, which prohibits driving while in an intoxicated condition, are not unconstitutionally vague in a case where the driver has refused to submit to any scientific test for determining the amount of alcohol he has consumed, because the prohibition against driving while impaired is not a vague and inadequate concept in that the question in each case is whether the particular defendant has actually impaired, to any extent, the physical and mental abilities which he is expected to possess in order to operate a vehicle as a reasonable and prudent driver, and because the term "driving while intoxicated" is a familiar concept, intelligible to the average person, indicating a greater degree of impairment which is reached when the driver has voluntarily consumed alcohol to the extent that he is incapable of employing the physical and mental abilities which he is expected to possess in order to operate a vehicle as a reasonable and prudent driver; a statute which employs terms having an accepted meaning "long recognized in law and life" cannot be said to be so vague and indefinite as to afford the defendant insufficient notice of what is prohibited or inadequate guidelines for adjudication.

Statutes -- Construction

3. Words and phrases used in a statute should be given their ordinary meaning when the Legislature has given no indication that a different meaning was intended.

 

282 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Westchester Rockland Newspapers, Inc. v. Leggett


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


48 N.Y.2d 430; 399 N.E.2d 518; 423 N.Y.S.2d 630; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 2412; 5 Media L. Rep. 2009


October 16, 1979, Argued  
November 20, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Appeal dismissed, without costs, upon the ground that no substantial constitutional question is directly involved. Oral application for leave to appeal granted. Judgment reversed, with costs, and the petition granted to the extent of directing the release of the transcripts of the competency hearing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant newspaper sought to vacate an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which had affirmed the order of respondent, a county court judge, that had excluded the public and press from a pretrial mental competency hearing in a criminal case where the accused was charged with rape.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sought relief from a closure order issued at the accused's request in a well-publicized criminal case for a pre-trial mental competency hearing wherein accused was charged with rape. Appellant successfully argued it had the right to hear and report matters of public interest and the closure order was improper. The court held even in a case that received pre-trial publicity, the court must not assume that public attendance at a competency hearing jeopardized the accused's right to a fair trial. If that question was present, the burden of proof was on the accused. Since the accused had served notice that he intended to raise his mental condition at trial through his request for competency exam, it was too late for him to claim the hearing should be closed to the public in order to prevent disclosure of the fact that he may have had a mental problem.


OUTCOME: The court reversed because it was error to exclude appellant from the hearing and deny it access to transcripts of the proceedings. There was no showing that there was testimony concerning the details of the rape, and no showing of a need for the court to exercise its inherent power to exclude.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Fair Trial -- Exclusion of Press and Public from Suppression Hearing

1. In a highly publicized case a defendant may request that a suppression hearing be closed to the public, and the court does not lack the inherent power to grant the request if it is necessary to preserve the defendant's right to a fair trial, since if such hearings were open to the public and the press in a well-publicized case, it is most likely that the substance of the evidence would be disclosed to the community from which the jurors would be drawn, even though the court might ultimately rule that the evidence should not be submitted to the jury at trial, and this would not only destroy the purpose for which the hearing was held, but would, perversely, have the very opposite effect of that intended (see Matter of Gannett Co. v De Pasquale, 43 NY2d 370, 443 U.S. 368, 99 S Ct 2898).

Appeal -- Court of Appeals -- Mootness

2. A petition seeking to have an order excluding the public and the press from a pretrial competency hearing and denying access to transcripts of the proceedings set aside on the ground that the order violates the right of the press to hear and report matters of public interest and concern is not, on appeal to the Court of Appeals, moot to the extent that it seeks to gain access to the transcripts, although the hearing has been concluded; additionally, the Court of Appeals traditionally retains jurisdiction in such cases despite a claim of mootness, because of the importance of the question involved, the possibility of recurrence, and the fact that orders of this nature quickly expire and thus typically evade review.

Appeal -- Court of Appeals -- Appeal on Constitutional Grounds

3. An appeal to the Court of Appeals on constitutional grounds must be dismissed if the determination below did not necessarily reach the constitutional issue.

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Press -- Reconciliation with Right to Fair Trial

4. The right of the public and the press to attend court proceedings is not absolute; all court proceedings are presumptively open to the public, but when this would jeopardize the right of the accused to a fair trial, the competing interests must be balanced and reconciled as far as possible.

Crimes -- Pretrial Competency Hearing

5. The purpose of a pretrial competency hearing is to determine whether the accused is mentally competent to stand trial, and the inquiry focuses narrowly on the defendant's present mental capacity to understand the proceedings against him and to assist in his own defense ( CPL 730.10, subd 1); the test is whether he has a sufficient present ability to consult with his lawyer with a reasonable degree of rational understanding, and whether he has a rational as well as factual understanding of the proceedings against him.

Crimes -- Pretrial Competency Hearing -- Exclusion of Press and Public from Hearing

6. At a pretrial competency hearing, evidence relevant and admissible to establish the defendant's capacity to understand the legal proceedings and to assist his attorney would ordinarily reveal little or nothing about his possible guilt of the crimes charged, and it is not foreseeable that public disclosure of the proof would frustrate the purpose of such a hearing; thus even in a case which has received some publicity, a court cannot assume that public attendance at a competency hearing will necessarily jeopardize the defendant's right to a fair trial, and if there is in fact such a risk in a particular case, the defendant has the burden of establishing it.

Crimes -- Pretrial Competency Hearing -- Exclusion of Press and Public from Hearing

7. The fact that a defendant may be charged with sexual offenses does not mean that a pretrial competency hearing should automatically be closed to the public, since although section 4 of the Judiciary Law grants the public the right to attend all court proceedings except that in all proceedings for divorce, seduction, abortion, rape, assault with intent to commit rape, sodomy, bastardy or filiation, the court may, in its discretion, exclude therefrom all persons who are not directly interested, the exception must be narrowly construed, and applies only when there will be testimony concerning the details of the specific acts mentioned in the statute and then only when the commission of one or more of the specified acts is the issue to be decided; the purpose of the exception is to grant the Judge the "power to refuse to turn his courtroom into a peep show" , and it is not aimed at shielding the public from testimony of all mental disorders which may have a sexual basis.

Crimes -- Public Trials -- Exclusion of Press and Public from Pretrial Proceeding

8. Initially, a motion to exclude the public from a pretrial proceeding must be made on the record, in open court, and in support of the motion the defendant must demonstrate a strong likelihood that evidence relevant and admissible at the particular hearing would prejudice the defendant's trial if it were disclosed to potential jurors or would involve sordid matters expressly covered by section 4 of the Judiciary Law, which provides that the court may exclude the public in certain sex offense cases; if, during the course of the argument, it becomes necessary for counsel to introduce or tender specified items of proof, the public disclosure of which would create the very prejudice sought to be avoided or itself involve disclosure of such sordid matters, defense counsel may further request that this portion of the argument be continued in camera, in the presence of counsel for the defendant and the prosecutor but out of the hearing of the public, and in any event, all proceedings on the motion, whether in open court or in camera, should be recorded for appellate review. Should the court decide that it is necessary to close the hearing in order to protect the defendant's rights or pursuant to section 4 of the Judiciary Law, the reasons for closure shall be given in open court.

Crimes -- Pretrial Competency Hearing -- Exclusion of Press and Public from Hearing

9. It was error for the court to exclude the press and the public from a pretrial mental competency hearing and to deny access to transcripts of the proceedings in a highly publicized criminal case in which the defendant was charged with rape, where there was no showing that there would be testimony concerning the details of the offense (see Judiciary Law, § 4), and where there was no showing of need for the court to exercise its inherent power to exclude the public from the hearing.

Constitutional Law -- Public Trials -- Right of Access to Court Proceedings

10. The public and the press have no constitutional right of access to court proceedings under either the First Amendment of the United States Constitution or the similar provision in the New York Constitution (NY Const, art I, § 8).

Constitutional Law -- Public Trials -- Right of Access to Court Proceedings

11. Any right of access to court proceedings accorded to the public and the press must also take into account the equally important rights of the accused; complete freedom to speak and publish at will is the ideal, and essential limitations on that freedom should be few and carefully scrutinized.

 

283 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Prink v. Rockefeller Center, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


48 N.Y.2d 309; 398 N.E.2d 517; 422 N.Y.S.2d 911; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 2401


September 6, 1979, Argued  
November 15, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs. Question certified answered in the affirmative.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed from a judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed an order of the trial court that directed plaintiff to appear for a further oral examination in plaintiff's wrongful death action.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff filed a wrongful death action against defendant and others. Plaintiff appealed from an order of the trial court that directed plaintiff to appear for a further oral examination to answer questions concerning conversations with the decedent pertaining to his seeing a psychiatrist and concerning conversations between plaintiff and decedent's psychiatrist occurring after decedent's death. The court held that plaintiff could not refuse to testify concerning her husband's mental state by claiming spousal privilege or physician-patient privilege. The appellate court affirmed. Plaintiff appealed. The reviewing court held that since decedent himself could not have prevented disclosure of the information sought had he survived, plaintiff, as his personal representative, could not do so in an action brought under N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law § 5-4.1. The court found that the circumstances of decedent's death precluded the assertion of the privileges.


OUTCOME: The order directing plaintiff to appear for a further oral examination was affirmed because plaintiff could not assert privileges that decedent would not have been able to assert had he survived.


HEADNOTES

Witnesses -- Privileged Communications -- Waiver of Spousal and Physician-Patient Privilege in Wrongful Death Action

1. In a wrongful death action wherein it was alleged that plaintiff's deceased husband was killed when he fell from a 36th floor window, statements made by the deceased's psychiatrist to a medical examiner and to plaintiff after her husband's death do not constitute a waiver of the spousal or physician-patient privilege, making otherwise privileged statements admissible, since a recipient of confidential information may not destroy the privilege; however, such statements are admissible since a wrongful death action is authorized only if it could have been maintained by the decedent had he survived ( EPTL 5-4.1) and if decedent had survived and brought the action he could not have successfully resisted defendants' demand, in their effort to establish that his injuries resulted from attempted suicide rather than defendants' negligence because the privilege against self incrimination does not permit a plaintiff to claim affirmative relief and at the same time refuse to disclose information bearing upon his right to maintain his action and for essentially the same reason, the decedent as plaintiff could assert neither the physician-patient privilege nor the conjugal privilege to foreclose inquiry concerning whether his injury was the result of an attempt at suicide. By bringing a personal injury action in which mental or physical condition is affirmatively put in issue, privilege is waived, and it is judicially noticed that many apparently accidental deaths are in fact suicides and that a wrongful death complaint predicated upon an alleged accidental fall from a 36th story window is sufficiently equivocal in that respect to put in issue, by plaintiff's affirmative act in bringing the action, decedent's mental condition.

Husband and Wife -- Privileged Communications

2. The spousal privilege ( CPLR 4502, subd [b]) is not terminated by death alone, and applies unless waived in some manner; the privilege belongs not to the witness but to the spouse against whom the testimony is offered.

Physicians and Surgeons -- Privileged Communications

3. The physician-patient privilege ( CPLR 4504), which belongs to the patient, is not terminated by death alone, and applies unless waived in some manner.

Witnesses -- Privileged Communications -- Testimony by Eavesdropper

4. An eavesdropper may testify concerning an otherwise privileged communication.

 

284 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Cook v. Binghamton


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


48 N.Y.2d 323; 398 N.E.2d 525; 422 N.Y.S.2d 919; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 2402


October 8, 1979, Argued  
November 15, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order modified, with costs to defendants, in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant municipalities appealed from the order of the Supreme Court, Appellate Division, Third Judicial Department (New York), which declared 1977 N.Y. Laws 965 unconstitutional insofar as it diminished plaintiff disabled firemen's wage payments under former N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 207-a and permanently enjoined the municipalities from reducing the level of the payments.


OVERVIEW: Under former N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 207-a, the disabled firemen were paid the full salaries they received while on active duty. The firemen alleged that 1977 N.Y. Laws 965, which significantly diminished the payments they would receive, was an unconstitutional impairment of their contractual rights to the payments under the former law. The court noted that the circumstance did not represent a contract-in-fact bargained for by the parties couched in contractual terms. While acknowledging that a legislative enactment could be treated as a contract if its language manifested a legislative intent to create private rights of a contractual nature, the court concluded that the former statute did not give rise to a contractual relation either in fact or by implication, and thus there could be no impermissible impairment of the obligation of contract. The court noted that the right to compensation grew out of the rendition of services, and not out of any contract between the government and the firemen. Furthermore, the former statute contained no words of contract, or any hint in its terms that the consent or agreement of any person was required for the statute to have its intended effect.


OUTCOME: The court modified the order to the extent of declaring that disabled firemen receiving benefits under the statute at issue could not be separated from service until they reached the mandatory retirement age of 70 unless they had voluntarily elected additional benefits available under state statute. The court affirmed the order as modified.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Impairment of Contractual Rights

1. The principal function of a legislative body is not to make contracts but to make laws which declare the policy of the State and are subject to repeal when a subsequent Legislature shall determine to alter that policy; nevertheless, it is established that a legislative enactment may contain provisions which, when accepted as the basis of action by individuals, become contracts between them and the State or its subdivisions with the protection of section 10 of article I of the United States Constitution, the contract clause.

Constitutional Law -- Impairment of Contractual Rights

2. In considering whether a State statute impairs the obligation of a contract (US Const, art I, § 10), the existence of the contract and the nature and extent of its obligation become Federal questions which are to be determined by an appraisal of the statutes of the State and the decisions of its courts.

Statutes -- Presumption of Constitutionality -- Burden of Proof

3. Every legislative enactment carries a strong presumption of constitutionality, and the burden of showing the unconstitutionality of a statute rests on the party attacking its validity.

Constitutional Law -- Impairment of Contractual Rights

4. In considering whether a State statute impairs the obligation of a contract (US Const, art I, § 10), a statute will generally be treated as a contract when its language and the circumstances manifest a legislative intent to create private rights of a contractual nature enforceable against the State.

Constitutional Law -- Impairment of Contractual Rights

5. In considering whether a State statute impairs the obligation of a contract (US Const, art I, § 10), legislative acts which fix salaries and compensation, or which set the term and tenure of public officers, are not presumed to create a contract; the presumption is that such a law is not intended to create private contractual or vested rights but merely declares a policy to be pursued until the Legislature shall ordain otherwise.

Municipal Corporations -- Firemen -- Payments to Disabled Firemen

6. Chapter 965 of the Laws of 1977, which amended section 207-a of the General Municipal Law by providing that a fireman who became disabled because of injury or illness incurred as a result of the performance of his duties is no longer entitled to receive the full amount of his regular salary or wages until his disability has ceased but shall receive instead his regular salary or wages only until he reaches his mandatory retirement age or attains the age or performs the period of service specified by applicable law for the termination of his service, does not work an impairment of rights guaranteed by the contract clause of the Federal Constitution (US Const, art I, § 10), since the statute contains no words of contract but rather expresses a public policy as determined by the Legislature and the matters addressed by the statute are among those classes of legislative acts long presumed to create no private contractual rights, and since no consummated unilateral contract arose at the moment of the injuries to plaintiffs, firemen disabled by illness or injury incurred in the line of duty who were receiving full salary and medical expenses pursuant to former section 207-a of the General Municipal Law prior to the effective date of chapter 965; moreover, the operation of chapter 965 does not impair plaintiffs' rights as members of a public pension system in violation of section 7 of article V of the State Constitution, which construes membership in any pension or retirement system as a contractual relationship which shall not be diminished or impaired, since the fact that there can be no constitutional impairment of pension system benefits does not create a constitutional right to stay in public employment.

Municipal Corporations -- Firemen -- Payments to Disabled Firemen

7. Inasmuch as chapter 965 of the Laws of 1977 amended section 207-a of the General Municipal Law by providing that a fireman who became disabled because of injury or illness incurred as a result of the performance of his duties is no longer entitled to receive the full amount of his regular salary or wages until his disability has ceased but shall receive instead his regular salary or wages only until he reaches his mandatory retirement age or attains the age or performs the period of service specified by applicable law for the termination of his service, disabled firemen receiving benefits under section 207-a of the General Municipal Law may not be separated from service until they reach the mandatory retirement age of 70, unless they have voluntarily elected additional benefits under section 384 of the Retirement and Social Security Law, since apart from an accidental disability retirement, the only age specified for involuntary retirement occurs at age 70, or at age 62 in the case of those who have voluntarily elected certain additional benefits, and if the additional benefit is not chosen, the member is not subject to separation from service at age 62 and is not subject to mandatory retirement until age 70.
