Pavle-Marty Cab Corp. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


48 N.Y.2d 784; 399 N.E.2d 945; 423 N.Y.S.2d 915; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 2444


October 17, 1979, Argued  
November 15, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Administrative Law -- Delegation of Legislative Power

The Appellate Division properly affirmed a judgment of the Supreme Court which held that a rule of the New York City Taxi and Limousine Commission requiring fleet operators of taxicabs to provide service to the public for a minimum of two shifts of nine hours each per day was not an unconstitutional denial of equal protection, but rather a reasonable regulation for the public benefit such that resultant economic hardships to owners of two-car operations were not grounds for complaint inasmuch as plaintiffs knew the rules when they obtained their additional taxicab and fleet owner class license. The challenged rule properly implements section 2300 of chapter 65 of the New York City Charter since the Legislature, although it cannot delegate its lawmaking power to an administrative agency, may declare its will, and after fixing a primary standard, endow such agencies with the power to fill in the interstices in the legislative product by promulgating rules and regulations consistent with the enabling legislation.

 

286 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Gordon


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


48 N.Y.2d 266; 397 N.E.2d 1309; 422 N.Y.S.2d 641; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 2380


October 15, 1979, Argued  
November 13, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, without costs, and matter remitted to the Appellate Division, First Department, for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant applicant challenged a judgment from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which denied the applicant's motion for admission to the New York Bar without the certification of respondent Committee for Fitness and Character and determined that N.Y. C.P.L.R. 9406(2) was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The applicant was employed in New York when he took the bar examination, but was transferred to another state before his admission into the New York Bar was final. The Committee for Character and Fitness deferred action on the applicant's request for admission and the applicant petitioned for admission without their certification. The court determined that N.Y. C.P.L.R. 9406(2) was unconstitutional because it violated the Privileges and Immunities Clause (clause). Under the clause, a state was prohibited from discriminating against nonresidents merely to further its own parochial interests of those of its residents, unless the discrimination concerned a matter that directly implicated the state's sovereignty. The court found that the clause intended to secure the right to pursue one's chosen occupation free from discriminatory interference, which included the profession of law. The court determined that no valid reason had been advanced to support why the admission to practice of law in New York was based upon residency. The court noted that other less drastic alternatives were available to protect the interest of the state in supervising attorneys.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that denied the applicant admission to the New York Bar without certification from the Committee for Character and Fitness and determined that a residency requirement was constitutional. The case was remitted to the trial court for further proceedings regarding the applicant's request for admission to the New York Bar.


HEADNOTES

Attorney and Client -- Residency Requirements for Admission to Bar

1. CPLR 9406 (subd 2), which provides that a person may not be admitted as a member of the Bar of the State of New York unless he furnishes proof that he has been an actual resident of the State for six months immediately preceding the submission of his application for admission to practice, is violative of the privileges and immunities clause of article IV of the Federal Constitution, since a State may not premise an individual's right to engage in his chosen occupation within its borders solely on residence; although a State may differentiate between residents and nonresidents in matters which directly implicate its sovereignty, such as voting or entitlement to public office, the right to pursue one's chosen occupation free from discriminatory interference is the very essence of the personal freedom that the privileges and immunities clause was intended to secure. CPLR 9406 (subd 2) works an invidious discrimination against nonresidents since exclusion based solely upon residence is a criterion which serves no purpose other than to deny persons the right to pursue their professional career objectives because of parochial interests.

Constitutional Law -- Privileges and Immunities

2. Where a State imposes a wide-ranging restriction which significantly impairs the efforts of nonresidents to earn a livelihood, the discriminatory action must surmount two distinct hurdles; first, the governmental interest claimed to justify the discrimination must be carefully examined to determine whether that interest is substantial, that is, whether noncitizens constitute a peculiar source of the evil at which the statute is aimed, and assuming that nonresidents do indeed present a problem which the State may legitimately address, the inquiry then focuses upon whether the means adopted to achieve that goal are narrowly drawn and are the least restrictive alternatives available.

 

287 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Mobil Oil Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


48 N.Y.2d 192; 397 N.E.2d 724; 422 N.Y.S.2d 33; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 2346; 5 Media L. Rep. 2145


September 5, 1979, Argued  
October 23, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of an order of the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York) which reversed a judgment of conviction entered against respondent oil company for its violation of Westchester County, N.Y., Local Law No. 9 (Local Law No. 9) (1974), a measure directed to gasoline price advertising.


OVERVIEW: Local Law No. 9 directed that all gasoline price advertising in the county be done by uniform pump-top signs identifying the selling price. The oil company appealed its trial court conviction for violating the ordinance by curb-side advertising. The appellate court reversed the adjudged conviction on the ground that Local Law No. 9, § 3 was an unconstitutional regulation of protected commercial speech. On the state's appeal, the court held that: (1) overbreadth analysis was inapplicable to commercial speech; (2) Local Law No. 9 had to be construed as a whole, with its various sections considered together and with reference to each other; (3) a general statutory clause only applied where a particular clause did not; (4) statutes enacting regulations malum prohibitum were strictly construed; and (5) Local Law No. 9, § 3 could not be justified on other grounds, nor was it a permissible time, place, or manner regulation, nor did the oil company have commercially effective and fiscally practicable advertising alternatives available to it.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order reversing the oil company's conviction for violating a municipal ordinance directed at the permissible content and context of gasoline price advertising.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Speech -- Regulation of Commercial Speech

1. Reasonable government regulation of the time, place and manner of distribution of commercial speech is constitutionally permissible; the content of commercial speech cannot be restricted unless there are available alternate channels for communicating such information or the restriction can be justified on other grounds, such as regulation to prevent deception. Accordingly, that section of a county local law banning all signs on or near service station premises which refer directly or indirectly to the price of gasoline, except required uniform price signs atop the gasoline pumps, which, when read, as it must be, in the context of the entire law, is addressed only to truthful commercial speech rather than deceptive advertising, is unconstitutional as applied to the service station owner in violation of the First Amendment to the United States Constitution since it makes speech impermissible solely on the basis of content, where the county has not demonstrated that the place of the speech produces a detrimental "secondary effect" on society, it is not clear that useful consumer information is not withheld from the public by said law, and serious questions exist concerning the adequacy of available alternate sources of conveying such information to the public.

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Speech -- Regulation of Commercial Speech

2. The First Amendment overbreadth doctrine, under which a person may challenge a statute that infringes protected speech even if the statute constitutionally might be applied to him, is not applicable to commercial speech. Accordingly, a county local law which bans all signs on or near service station premises which refer directly or indirectly to the price of gasoline except required uniform price signs atop the gasoline pumps, cannot be found to be unconstitutional as applied to defendant service station owner where defendant, who was convicted and fined under the statute for displaying a large sign reading "Check Our Low Low Low Prices", introduced no evidence to establish that its prices are lower than its competitors'; absent such evidence of truthfulness, the presumption of the constitutionality of the statute and the failure to negate the possibility of deception, at which the statute is purportedly aimed, required that the law be upheld in a challenge by defendant, since the statute might constitutionally be applied to it.

Statutes -- Construction

3. A statute or ordinance must be construed as a whole and its various sections must be considered together and with reference to each other; a general provision of a statute applies only where a particular provision does not.

Statutes -- Construction

4. Statutes enacting regulations malum prohibitum are to be strictly construed.

 

288 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Spears v. Berle


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


48 N.Y.2d 254; 397 N.E.2d 1304; 422 N.Y.S.2d 636; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 2379; 13 ERC (BNA) 2160; 9 ELR 20796


September 6, 1979, Argued  
October 18, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Appeal taken as of right dismissed, without costs, upon the ground that the order appealed from does not finally determine the proceeding within the meaning of the Constitution. On the court's own motion, leave to appeal granted ( CPLR 5602, subd [a], par 2). Order reversed, without costs, and the matter remitted to Supreme Court, Sullivan County, for a hearing in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Commissioner of Environmental Conservation of the State of New York challenged the judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which determined that respondent landowners had been deprived of all reasonable return on their property and granted their relief of an interim permit to extract humus, sand, and stone, from their wetlands.


OVERVIEW: The landowners' properties were both classified as wetlands. The landowners applied to the commissioner for a permit allowing extraction of humus, sand, and stone from the wetlands. After a public hearing, where undisputed evidence showed that the proposed mining activities would lead to all but complete destruction of the wetlands, the commissioner denied the application. The landowners sought an order directing the commissioner to issue the permit, or institute condemnation proceedings. The lower court found that the landowners had been deprived of all reasonable return on their property and granted the requested relief. The court reversed and remitted the action for an evidentiary hearing on the taking issue. The court held that the issue was whether the denial of the interim permit by the commissioner had a rational basis and was supported by substantial evidence. The court held that the landowners conceded that the proposed mining activity would virtually destroy the wetlands, and that the issuance of a permit by the commissioner was proscribed by N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 24-0705(3) of the Freshwater Wetlands Act.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that granted the landowners' application for a freshwater wetlands interim permit. The court remitted the action for an evidentiary hearing on the takings issue.


HEADNOTES

Environmental Conservation -- Freshwater Wetlands -- Land Use Regulations

1. A land use regulation is deemed so onerous that a taking has occurred when it renders the property unsuitable for any reasonable income, productive or other private use for which it is adapted and thus destroys its economic value, or all but a bare residue of its value, which a landowner challenging such regulation should demonstrate by producing "dollars and cents" evidence as to the economic return that could be realized under each permitted use. Accordingly, in a hearing pursuant to the Freshwater Wetlands Act ( ECL 24-0101 et seq.) to determine whether an interim permit to allow regulated activities on freshwater wetlands should be issued, petitioners' bare conclusion that no activity, other than a proposed mining activity, would afford a reasonable return on the property is, standing alone, insufficient to make out a case of confiscatory application of the act, and the issue should be remitted to the Supreme Court to afford the parties a reasonable opportunity to litigate the taking issue in the proper forum.

Environmental Conservation -- Freshwater Wetlands -- Judicial Review of Administrative Action

2. ECL 24-0705 affords a property owner judicial review of an administrative decision denying him a permit for proposed regulated activities upon freshwater wetlands, in which a court is authorized to determine whether the denial of a permit had a rational basis and was supported by substantial evidence and, if the administrative decision is sustained, whether the regulation of the particular land has become so rigorous as to amount to a taking without just compensation ( ECL 24-0705, subd 7), on which aspect of the proceeding there must be an evidentiary hearing at which the landowner and the State may produce expert testimony and other evidence bearing upon the regulation's effect on the value of the subject parcel; if application of the wetland regulations would result in a taking, the remedy is to direct the Commissioner of Environmental Conservation to either grant the requested permit or institute condemnation proceedings. Accordingly, a formal declaration as to the constitutionality of the act in any particular instance is unnecessary.

Environmental Conservation -- Freshwater Wetlands

3. In a hearing pursuant to the Freshwater Wetlands Act ( ECL 24-0101 et seq.) to determine whether an interim permit to allow regulated activities on freshwater wetlands should be issued, where petitioners property owners conceded, in essence, that a proposed mining activity would virtually destroy the wetlands, issuance of a permit by the Commissioner of Environmental Conservation (Commissioner) was proscribed by the act itself ( ECL 24-0705, subd 3). Accordingly, the Commissioner's decision to deny such application was not unwarranted or improper, particularly where petitioners failed to supply the data required to support its application.

Appeal -- Court of Appeals -- Finality of Order

4. In a CPLR article 78 proceeding to review a determination of the Department of Environmental Conservation under the Freshwater Wetlands Act ( ECL 24-0101 et seq.), remittal of the matter by the Appellate Division to the Commissioner of Environmental Conservation for further proceedings as to costs renders such order nonfinal; accordingly, said order is not appealable as of right to the Court of Appeals; however, the court on its own motion may grant leave to appeal.

 

289 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Flanagan v. Board of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


47 N.Y.2d 613; 393 N.E.2d 991; 419 N.Y.S.2d 917; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 2187


May 29, 1979, Argued  
July 10, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs, the order of Special Term reinstated insofar as it granted plaintiff's motion for summary judgment and denied defendant's cross motion, and the case remitted to Supreme Court, Suffolk County, for assessment of damages.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff school principal challenged the judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which held that his lawsuit against defendant school district for violating the terms of his employment contract was to be dismissed, because he failed to file a notice of claim as required under N.Y. Educ. Law § 3813(1).


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff public school principal was hired by defendant school district and entered into an employment contract. After defendant terminated plaintiff's position, plaintiff was dismissed prior to the expiration of the employment contract. Plaintiff filed suit against defendant claiming contract violations, and the trial court found defendant liable. The appellate court reversed, holding that plaintiff failed to file a notice of claim as required under N.Y. Educ. Law § 3813(1). Plaintiff challenged the ruling, and the court held that even though plaintiff was required to file a notice of claim, prior to the commencement of his action, under the circumstances of this case, § 3813(1) could not be interposed as a bar to plaintiff's claim. The court stated that defendant failed to raise the issue before the trial court, and brought it up for the first time in an appellate court brief. The court noted that while service of a notice of claim was a statutory condition precedent, which did not have to be pleaded as an affirmative defense, the defense was one, which if not raised before the court of original jurisdiction, was waived. The court remanded for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court, holding that plaintiff school principal was not required to give notice to public school district prior to filing suit as required under state law, because the school district waived its right to notice, and therefore the notice requirement could not act as a bar against plaintiff's lawsuit.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- Teachers -- Abolition of Position

The plaintiff's failure to file a notice of claim pursuant to subdivision 1 of section 3813 of the Education Law does not bar his action to enjoin the termination of his position as a school principal and for damages for the breach of his contract, since this issue was not raised in the Supreme Court, and while service of a notice of claim is a statutory condition precedent which does not have to be pleaded as an affirmative defense, the defense is one which if not raised before the court of original jurisdiction is waived; furthermore, section 2510 of the Education Law, which provides that when a board of education abolishes a position, the services of the teacher having the least seniority in the system within the tenure of the position abolished shall be discontinued, does not destroy plaintiff's rights under his three-year contract.

 

290 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Hofbauer


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


47 N.Y.2d 648; 393 N.E.2d 1009; 419 N.Y.S.2d 936; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 2204


June 5, 1979, Argued  
July 10, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, state and department of social services, sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed an order of the family court dismissing a petition against respondent parents that alleged child neglect in violation of N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 1012(f), para. i, cl. A.


OVERVIEW: After respondent parents rejected conventional treatment for their ill child, appellant department of social services filed a petition against respondents alleging child neglect in violation of N.Y. Fam. Ct. Act § 1012(f) para. i, cl. A. The family court dismissed the petition, and the appellate division, after allowing appellant state to intervene, affirmed. The court affirmed the order of the appellate division. The court held that the factual findings below indicated that although respondents' chosen treatment plan was less than conventional, it had not proven detrimental to the child's welfare. The court further held that appellants had failed to establish that respondents' actions constituted neglect because respondents' alternate course of treatment had been advised by their duly licensed physician and had not been expressly rejected in the field.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed an order of the appellate division, which affirmed an order of the family court dismissing appellant social services' petition against respondent parents alleging child neglect. Respondents' decision regarding the care of their ill child had not been rejected in the medical field and was based on the advice of their skilled physician.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child -- Abused or Neglected Child

The most significant factor in determining whether a child is being deprived of adequate medical care, and, thus, is a neglected child within the meaning of section 1012 (subd [f], par [i], cl [A]) of the Family Court Act, is whether the parents have provided an acceptable course of medical treatment for their child in light of all the surrounding circumstances and the court's inquiry should be whether the parents, once having sought accredited medical assistance and having been made aware of the seriousness of their child's affliction and the possibility of cure if a certain mode of treatment is undertaken, have provided for their child a treatment which is recommended by their physician and which has not been totally rejected by all responsible medical authority; accordingly, a child suffering from Hodgkin's disease whose parents failed to follow the recommendation of an attending physician to have their child treated by radiation and chemotherapy, but, rather, placed their child under the care of a duly licensed physician advocating nutritional or metabolic therapy, including injections of laetrile, is not a neglected child within the meaning of the statute inasmuch as it cannot be said, as a matter of law, that the parents have failed to undertake reasonable efforts to ensure that acceptable medical treatment is being provided their child where there are findings supported by the record that numerous qualified doctors have been consulted by the child's physician and have contributed to the child's care, that the parents have both serious and justifiable concerns about the deleterious effects of radiation treatments and chemotherapy, that there is medical proof that the nutritional treatment being administered the child was controlling his condition and that such treatment is not as toxic as is the conventional treatment, and that conventional treatments will be administered to the child if his condition so warrants.

 

291 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hardy


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


47 N.Y.2d 500; 392 N.E.2d 1233; 419 N.Y.S.2d 49; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 2133


June 8, 1979, Argued  
July 9, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the People of the State of New York, sought review of an order of the Monroe County Court (New York) that affirmed a judgment granting respondent defendants' motion to dismiss an information.


OVERVIEW: Respondent defendants operated an establishment in which liquor was sold. Following police observations of liquor being sold and loud and abusive crowds gathered at the club, defendants were arrested for selling liquor without a license in violation of N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law § 100(1), para. b, and for violating § 44-11A of the Municipal Code of the City of Rochester by keeping a place of public resort by which the peace, comfort, or decency of its neighborhood was disturbed. The court granted defendants' motion to dismiss, and on appeal the judgment was affirmed. Appellant, the People of the State of New York, sought review. The court reversed, holding that under the N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law, private clubs had to be licensed if they sold liquor, that § 44-11A of the Municipal Code was constitutional, and that whether a private club was a public resort within the meaning of the latter provision was a question for the fact finder.


OUTCOME: The order dismissing the information was reversed; the informations were reinstated, and the matter remitted for further proceedings on the informations because private clubs were required to be licensed to sell liquor and the municipal law was constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Intoxicating Liquors -- Licenses -- Sale of Alcoholic Beverages by Private Club

1. A private club incorporated pursuant to State law as a nonprofit corporation which sells liquor only to members is not exempt from New York liquor license requirements, since subdivision 1 of section 100 of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law forbids a sale by any person of any alcoholic beverage without obtaining a license, subdivision 22 of section 3 of that law defines "person" to include a corporation, and "clubs" are defined to include an organization of persons under the Not-For-Profit Corporation Law (Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, § 3, subd 9); the policies and regulations established by the Legislature and the State Liquor Authority would become meaningless if their requirements could be avoided by simply incorporating as a private club.

Crimes -- Disturbance of Peace at Public Resort

2. Whether or not a private club incorporated pursuant to State law as a nonprofit corporation was being operated, through the indiscriminate sale of membership cards for a nominal fee to anyone who sought entry, as a place where the public had a right to go, in violation of section 44-11A of the Municipal Code of the City of Rochester which provides that no person shall knowingly keep any place of public resort by which the peace, comfort, or decency of a neighborhood is disturbed, public resort being defined as any place where the public has a right to go (Rochester Municipal Code, § 44-1), is a question of fact.

Constitutional Law -- Prohibiting Disturbance of Peace at Public Resort

3. Section 44-11A of the Municipal Code of the City of Rochester, which provides that no person shall knowingly keep any place of public resort by which the peace, comfort, or decency of a neighborhood is disturbed, is not void for vagueness since the test of whether a law possesses the certainty and definiteness mandated by due process is whether the language of the statute would indicate to reasonable persons the nature of the conduct it proscribes, and the provision in question provides an objective rather than a subjective standard by which disturbance is to be measured.

 

292 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Bennett v. County of Nassau


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


47 N.Y.2d 535; 393 N.E.2d 446; 419 N.Y.S.2d 451; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 2152


June 4, 1979, Argued  
July 9, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs, and the order of Supreme Court, Nassau County, reinstated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff nonjudicial county employees (employees) brought an action against defendant county to receive cash payment for accumulated vacation and sick leave upon the transfer of their positions to the state. The trial court granted the employees motion for summary judgment. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York) reversed. The employees appealed.


OVERVIEW: The employees claimed that they were terminated from the county and that they were entitled to be paid their accumulated vacation and sick leave time in cash. The county claimed that the transfer of the employees from county to state service did not constitute a termination and that the action was premature. The court reversed the appellate division and reinstated the order of the trial court. There was no question that the employees transferred from the county to state service were terminated and that there had been a separation from the service of the county. Because the employees had not been discharged for cause they became entitled to cash payment for accumulated time as if they had retired. The option to transfer accumulated benefits was construed as optional, not mandatory, therefore there was no impairment of the employees' rights because whatever accumulated leave or vacation time was transferred was done of the employees' own free will. The employees were entitled to judgment against the county for the cash value of the accumulated vacation time and sick leave they did not voluntarily transfer to the state.


OUTCOME: The order of the appellate division granting judgment in favor of the county was reversed and the order of the trial court granting the employees' motion for summary judgment was reinstated.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- Unified Court System -- Transfer of Accumulated Leave

The rights of County Court nonjudicial employees under their contract with the county to cash payment for accumulated vacation pay and sick leave upon separation from the service of the county accrued upon transfer to State service, pursuant to court unification, of their positions. Since the provisions of section 220 (subd 6, par [d], now § 39) of the Judiciary Law for transfer to the credit of such employee with the State of accumulated vacation pay and sick leave cannot be justified as a reasonable and necessary exercise of the police power, the county having made no showing that the State's take-over of the court system faced it with any dire financial problem, and since the statute should be construed to avoid constitutional infirmity through impairment of contract rights that would result if the statute were construed to be mandatory, its transfer provisions should be construed as optional.

Mt. Vernon Fire Ins. Co. v. Travelers Indem. Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


47 N.Y.2d 575; 393 N.E.2d 974; 419 N.Y.S.2d 899; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 2183


May 29, 1979, Argued  
July 3, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff excess liability insurer sought review of an order by the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed as modified a judgment of the trial court. The appellate division had upheld the dismissal the excess liability insurer's complaint against defendant primary insurer and also declared that the excess liability insurer was to provide coverage to its insured.


OVERVIEW: A clause in an excess liability policy entered into between the excess liability insurer and the insured provided that the risks assumed were subject to all the exclusions and limitations of the primary insurance in all respects. The primary policy afforded the insured the statutory minimum coverage on each of the seven units of its fleet of trucks and vans; it was expressly inapplicable while any trailer covered by the policy was used with any tractor owned or hired by the insured and not covered by like insurance in the company. But the tractor in the accident was covered by the primary insurance, but not the excess policy. The court affirmed, finding that the excess liability insurer could not rely upon the exclusion in the primary policy. The court stated that there was an exceptionally strong principle of construing exclusions strictly against an insurer, whose form policy language was not only its own but "non-negotiable" as well. Because the primary insurer could not rely upon the exclusion clause, neither could the excess liability insurer. The exclusion clause remained dormant or came to life in accordance with the terms of the primary insurance policy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the appellate division, which had affirmed the dismissal of the excess liability insurer's complaint against the primary insurer and ordered that the excess liability insurer was obligated to provide its insured with excess insurance coverage on the claim at issue.


HEADNOTES

Insurance -- Automobile Liability Insurance

A clause in an excess automobile liability insurance policy providing that the risks assumed were subject to all exclusions of and shall follow the primary insurance, the primary policy providing that coverage was inapplicable "while any trailer covered by this policy is used with any [tractor] owned or hired by the insured and not covered by like insurance in the company", is construed as providing excess insurance coverage for a tractor-trailer tandem rig, the tractor and trailer having been expressly covered by the primary policy, and the trailer alone having been included in the excess insurance policy, since the excess insurer may not use the clause to gain an exemption for itself where its author, the primary insurer, would not be entitled to call the clause into play.

Ames Volkswagen, Ltd. v. State Tax Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


47 N.Y.2d 345; 391 N.E.2d 1302; 418 N.Y.S.2d 324; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 2080


May 29, 1979, Submitted  
June 12, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner auto dealers appealed from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which affirmed the trial court declaration that N.Y. Tax Law § 1137-A was valid and constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The auto dealers did not challenge the State's authority to tax sales, their only argument being that the State had no power to impose a tax on sales before they are actually consummated. The court held that the legislature validly and constitutionally imposed upon vendors, who had already collected sales taxes upon their sales made between specified dates, the statutory obligation to accelerate their sales tax payments by estimating their sales and resultant taxes thereon for the balance of that month, in order to pay their full monthly tax liability prior to the close of the state's fiscal year.


OUTCOME: The order finding the statute constitutional was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Sales and Use Taxes -- Advance Payment of Tax

1. The Legislature may validly impose upon vendors, who have already collected sales taxes on their sales made between March 1 and March 20, the statutory obligation to accelerate their sales tax payments which become due on March 20 by estimating their sales and resultant taxes thereon for the balance of that month, in order to pay their full monthly tax liability prior to the close of the State's fiscal year on March 31 (Tax Law, § 1137-A), since measures enacted in the exercise of the taxing power for the purpose of raising revenues violate the due process clause only if the act is so arbitrary as to compel the conclusion that it does not involve an exertion of the taxing power, but constitutes the direct exertion of a different and forbidden power; the Legislature has nearly unconstrained authority in the design of taxing measures unless they are utterly unreasonable or arbitrary, and the advance payment of taxes on income not yet earned is neither new, novel nor improper.

Actions -- Form of Action -- Challenge to Constitutionality of Statute

2. A CPLR article 78 proceeding, as such, does not lie to challenge the constitutionality of a legislative enactment; accordingly, such a proceeding should be converted into an action for a declaratory judgment.

In re K.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


47 N.Y.2d 374; 391 N.E.2d 1316; 418 N.Y.S.2d 339; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 2083


May 3, 1979, Argued  
June 12, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother challenged the judgment of the Supreme Court, Appellate Division (New York), which affirmed the trial court's order that it would be in her child's best interests to be freed for adoption by the foster parents with whom she had resided throughout a prolonged litigation. The mother had temporarily placed her child in the custody of respondent social services department, but had refused requests to give the child for adoption.


OVERVIEW: Appellant mother temporarily placed her daughter with respondent social services department for foster care. Respondent sought permanent custody of the child, and the trial court granted the request. On appeal, the appellate division affirmed. On further appeal, appellant claimed that the state was prohibited from taking her child when the child had not been permanently surrendered, abandoned, or neglected by appellant. The court reversed the judgment of the appellate division, holding that there was no legal basis for offering the child for adoption against appellant's wishes. The court noted that the prolonged separation of appellant and her daughter was due to litigation initiated or necessitated by respondent's actions and was not the result of any parental neglect. The court held that respondent had repeatedly failed, either in the family court or on appeal, to prove that appellant was guilty of statutory abandonment or permanent neglect or to show that she was unfit. The court held that absent such a finding the courts could not permanently sever all parental ties. N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law §384-b(4). The court ordered that the child be returned to appellant's custody.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate division, affirming an award of custody of appellant mother's daughter to respondent social services department was reversed because there was no showing that appellant neglected or abandoned her child. The court found that appellant had, through uncommon effort, preserved parental ties throughout the child's life. Absent proof of abandonment, neglect, or unfitness of appellant, custody could not be taken from her.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child -- Custody

A mother who temporarily placed her infant daughter with the Department of Social Services for foster care, and who, despite financial and legal obstacles, maintained ties with her daughter, is entitled to have custody of the child, now 13 years old, where the department repeatedly denied the mother's requests and for several years sought to offer the child for adoption by attempting to prove, in successive proceedings and on a variety of grounds, that the mother was unfit; in the various proceedings it was held that the mother had not abandoned or neglected the child and that she was not otherwise unfit, and the prolonged separation of mother and daughter was due to litigation initiated or necessitated by the department's actions and was not the result of any parental neglect. Absent a finding of statutory abandonment, permanent neglect, or that the mother is unfit, a court may not permanently sever all parental ties (Social Services Law, § 384-b, subd 4), even though some might find adoption to be in the child's best interests.

Spector v. State Com. on Judicial Conduct


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


47 N.Y.2d 462; 392 N.E.2d 552; 418 N.Y.S.2d 565; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 2116


March 29, 1979, Argued  
June 5, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Determined sanction accepted, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner justice sought review of the determination of respondent State Commission on Judicial Conduct (commission), which concluded that the justice should be admonished for his appointments that gave the appearance of impropriety.


OVERVIEW: The commission served a formal complaint on the justice alleging charges of misconduct arising out of the justice's appointment of attorneys as guardians ad litem, receivers, referees, and a committee. The referee concluded that certain appointments were not free from the appearance of impropriety but did not find any actual impropriety. The appointments were those of sons of other justices who had appointed the justice's son. The commission concluded that the cross appointments gave the appearance of impropriety in violation of Canon 4 of the Canons of Judicial Ethics and section 33 of the Rules Governing Judicial Conduct, N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 22, part 33, and determined that the appropriate sanction was that the justice be admonished. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the justice's participation in the cross appointments created a circumstantial appearance of impropriety, in effect permitting the inference that each of the judges involved was securing appointments for their own sons, which was against the canons condemning nepotism.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the commission and accepted the commission's determined sanction of admonishment.


HEADNOTES

Judges -- Disciplinary Proceedings -- Nepotism

Nepotism in the judiciary is to be condemned, and disguised nepotism imports an additional component of evil because, implicitly conceding that evident nepotism would be unacceptable, the actor seeks to conceal what he is really accomplishing; even if it cannot be said that there is proof of the fact of disguised nepotism, an appearance of such impropriety is no less to be condemned than is the impropriety itself; the appointment of his son by any Judge would be both unthinkable and intolerable whatever might be the son's character and fitness or his father's peculiar qualification in the circumstances to assess such character and fitness, and, as such, an arrangement for cross appointments would not only offend the antinepotism principle, but it would go a step further, seeking to accomplish the objectives of nepotism while obscuring the fact thereof; accordingly, where petitioner Justice was charged by the State Commission on Judicial Conduct with misconduct arising out of his appointment of certain attorneys as guardians ad litem, receivers, referees and in one instance the committee of an incompetent in judicial proceedings pending before him, such appointments being of the sons of two other Judges during periods when such other Judges were making similar appointments of petitioner's son, it was appropriate that petitioner be admonished for the appearances of impropriety.

 

297 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Thornton v. Roosevelt Hospital


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


47 N.Y.2d 780; 391 N.E.2d 1002; 417 N.Y.S.2d 920; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 2055


March 28, 1979, Argued  
May 10, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a tort action involving the death of a patient, appellant executor sought review of the order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which denied appellant leave to plead causes of action sounding in strict products liability against respondent manufacturer.


OVERVIEW: Respondent hospital injected a patient with thorium dioxide made by respondent manufacturer. Twenty years later the patient died and appellant executor brought an action against respondents, alleging that the injection caused the patient to contract cancer. The trial court dismissed the negligence and warranty actions as time-barred and granted leave to re-plead an amended complaint based upon strict products liability. The appellate court struck the grant of leave to re-plead, and appellant sought review. On appeal, the court affirmed the appellate court. The court held that a strict products liability action arose at the time of injection, not the time of discovery of the alleged deleterious effect. A strict products liability action was governed by the three-year statute of limitations for torts under New York law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's order. Appellant executor's cause of action in strict products liability for the injection of a chemical compound into the decedent arose at the time of the injection and was barred by the three-year tort statute of limitations.


HEADNOTES

Limitation of Actions -- Strict Liability

An order of the Appellate Division which modified and, as modified, affirmed orders of the Supreme Court by striking those portions of the orders granting leave to replead causes of action sounding in strict products liability, is affirmed. In this action sounding in strict products liability based upon allegations that a thorium dioxide substance manufactured by one of the defendants and injected into decedent resulted in the onset of cancer, the cause of action accrued at the time of invasion of decedent's body, and not at the time the decedent's cancerous condition became apparent, and, inasmuch as such theory of liability is grounded in tort rather than contract, a three-year Statute of Limitations is applicable; accordingly, plaintiff's claim being interposed some 20 years after the decedent's injection, the action is time-barred, irrespective of whether the cause of action is couched in terms of strict products liability; nor may plaintiff invoke the so-called "discovery" rule, wherein in the context of medical malpractice an action may be commenced after discovery of a "foreign object" in the body of a patient, since, by statute, chemical compounds do not constitute "foreign objects".

 

298 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. St. Agatha Home for Children


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


47 N.Y.2d 46; 389 N.E.2d 1098; 416 N.Y.S.2d 577; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 1934


March 28, 1978, Argued  
May 1, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution sought review of the decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed the decision of the trial court convicting defendants, private child care organization and an employee, for violation of the town zoning ordinance, and which dismissed the information against the child care organization and the employee.


OVERVIEW: The town charged the private child care organization and one of its employees with operating a nonsecure detention center in an area restricted to one-family occupancy in violation of the town zoning ordinance. The trial court convicted defendants of the violations. The appellate court reversed the decision of the trial court and dismissed the information. On the prosecution's appeal, the court affirmed the decision of the appellate court, reversing the conviction and dismissing the indictment. The court determined that N.Y. County Law § 218-a required the county to provide accessible and adequate nonsecure detention facilities, notwithstanding any other provision of law, that the county decided to meet the statutory requirement through privately operated homes, that the county approved the location and funded the home, and that local zoning ordinances cannot overrule the county's statutorily mandated decision.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the appellate court, reversing the conviction of the child care organization and its employee for violation of the zoning ordinance, and dismissing the information.


HEADNOTES

Counties -- Detention Center for Persons in Need of Supervision -- Violation of Local Zoning Ordinance

Defendants' convictions of operating a nonsecure detention center for persons in need of supervision in an area restricted to one-family occupancy in violation of a local zoning ordinance were properly set aside since subdivision B of section 218-a of the County Law, which provides that notwithstanding any other provision of law, each county board of supervisors shall provide conveniently accessible and adequate nonsecure detention facilities for the temporary care and maintenance of alleged juvenile delinquents and persons in need of supervision, authorizes and requires a county to provide adequate facilities of the type described despite any conflicting law or local ordinance, and the county having determined to fulfill its obligation through the vehicle of privately operated homes, that decision may not be overruled by application of a local zoning ordinance.

 

299 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Huntington v. Park Shore Country Day Camp, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


47 N.Y.2d 61; 390 N.E.2d 282; 416 N.Y.S.2d 774; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 1967


March 20, 1979, Argued  
May 1, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff town sought to enjoin defendant property owner from operating tennis courts for commercial purposes in a residential district. The property owner challenged the decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed the lower court's judgment and granted the request for injunctive relief.


OVERVIEW: The town brought an action to enjoin the property owner from operating commercial tennis courts on its residentially zoned property. The property owner counterclaimed for a declaration that the zoning ordinance was unconstitutionally discriminatory. The lower court denied the application for a permanent injunction, and the town appealed. The appellate court reversed, and the property owner sought review. The court held that the zoning ordinance, which permitted private, nonprofit clubs but not commercial enterprises to operate tennis courts in a residential district, was not unconstitutionally discriminatory because a commercial venture had characteristics which could potentially render it more burdensome to a residential neighborhood than the same activity carried on by a nonprofit club and it was not shown that the town was acting other than in a manner rationally related to the preservation and fostering of the values of a residential district. The judgment of the appellate court was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's order granting the town's request to enjoin the property owner from operating commercial tennis courts on its residentially owned property.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning

1. A town zoning ordinance which permits private, nonprofit clubs but not commercial enterprises to operate tennis courts in a residential district is not unconstitutionally discriminatory since there are characteristics of a commercial venture that may render it, at least potentially, more burdensome to a residential neighborhood than the same activity carried on by a nonprofit club, i.e., there may be more crowds, noise, traffic and other forces disruptive of the serenity of the neighborhood and it is reasonable to foresee that a private club conducted on a nonprofit basis, while providing a desirable recreational area, may in other ways avoid at least some of the drawbacks of commercial ownership; moreover, it is a legislature's right and, particularly in matters of zoning and planning, its obligation to anticipate future problems and to enact measures to guard against them, though in fact, the anticipated events may never come to pass, and it cannot be said that the town was acting other than in a manner rationally related to the preservation and fostering of the values of a residential district.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning

2. Zoning ordinances, like all legislative enactments, are invested with an exceedingly strong presumption of constitutionality, and one challenging such an ordinance shoulders the very heavy burden of demonstrating beyond a reasonable doubt that it is unconstitutional; a classification scheme devised by a local legislature would have to be sustained if it could be said to be reasonably related to some manifest evil which need only be reasonably apprehended, and if on any interpretation of the facts known or reasonably to be perceived, a zoning measure falls within the embrace of a town's authority to regulate property as a means of promoting the general welfare of the community (Town Law, § 261), it is insulated from attack.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Delimiting Permitted Uses

3. Since the separation of business from nonbusiness uses is an appropriate line of demarcation in delimiting permitted uses for zoning purposes, business uses may be excluded from residential districts, whose primary purpose is to provide an environment for safe, healthful and comfortable family life rather than the development of commercial instincts and the pursuit of pecuniary profits, although there are commercial enterprises whose activities will not necessarily undermine the values fostered by such districts, but may even serve to complement and enhance the tenor of residential living, and flexibility to pursue such an accommodation is imparted to zoning authorities by provision for the granting of a special exception or special use permit.

 

300 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Consolidated Edison Co. v. Public Service Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


47 N.Y.2d 94; 390 N.E.2d 749; 417 N.Y.S.2d 30; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 1987; 5 Media L. Rep. 1060


March 22, 1979, Argued  
May 1, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant power companies, in consolidated appeals, challenged orders of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court, Third Department (New York) which held that orders of respondent Public Service Commission of the State of New York (PSC) prohibiting promotional advertising by the power companies and limiting their use of billing inserts did not exceed the PSC's statutory authority or impinge upon the companies' U.S. Const. amend. I rights.


OVERVIEW: Respondent PSC issued an order banning promotional advertising by appellant power companies and another order prohibiting their use of billing inserts to disseminate their position on controversial matters of public policy. The power companies challenged these orders in separate proceedings consolidated on appeal. The appellate division upheld the orders and the power companies sought review. The court affirmed. The PSC's orders did not exceed its statutory authority under the broad supervisory powers granted to it by N.Y. Pub. Serv. Law §§ 4, 5, 65, 66 and the specific power to regulate billing granted by N.Y. Pub. Serv. Law § 66(12-a). The billing insert limitation did not impinge upon the power companies' U.S. Const. amend. I rights because it was content neutral, other means of communication were available, and a significant governmental interest was served by protecting the privacy of consumers who were a captive audience for materials included in the power companies' bills. Promotional advertising was not protected commercial speech in view of the power companies' noncompetitive market and the public policy importance of discouraging excessive electrical consumption.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed two orders of the appellate division which affirmed orders of respondent PSC prohibiting promotional advertising by respondent power companies and limiting their ability to use billing inserts to disseminate their views on controversial matters of public policy.


HEADNOTES

Public Utilities -- Promotional Advertising

1. The Legislature has conferred upon the Public Service Commission (PSC) vast regulatory and supervisory powers over public utilities licensed to operate in the State (Public Service Law, §§ 4, 5, 65, 66) as well as imposing a duty upon the PSC to encourage all persons and corporations to formulate and carry out long-range programs for the preservation of environmental values and the conservation of natural resources (Public Service Law, § 5, subd 2); accordingly, the PSC possesses sufficient statutory authority to prohibit the promotional advertising of electricity inasmuch as it possesses ample power to prescribe reasonable measures designed to prevent wasteful consumption or unneeded expansion of utility services and the PSC, in its expertise, could have reasonably concluded that promotional advertising might tend to increase injudicious and unnecessary consumption of electrical power.

Public Utilities -- Inclusion of Inserts in Utility Bills

2. The Public Service Commission (PSC) does not exceed its jurisdiction by prohibiting the inclusion of inserts in utility billing envelopes inasmuch as it possesses the statutory authority to regulate not only the format and informational content of the bill itself but the entire billing communication so as to assure simplicity and clarity (Public Service Law, § 66, subd 12-a); furthermore, control of the billing procedure, a process necessarily adjunct to the furnishing of utility service, fits neatly into the PSC's supervisory role as prescribed by the Legislature which invested the PSC with all powers needed to carry out the purposes of the Public Service Law as well as power to supervise generally the operation of electric and gas corporations and electric and gas plants.

Public Utilities -- Dissemination of Views on Controversial Matters of Public Policy

3. An order of the Public Service Commission, providing that utilities under its jurisdiction discontinue the practice of using material inserted in bills rendered to customers as a mechanism for the dissemination of the position of such utilities on controversial matters of public policy, does not violate the utilities' right of freedom of speech, for it does not extend to all speech of a prescribed content but only to one manner of communication, no one viewpoint is singled out for special treatment and the general right to express ideas in other forums is not effected.

Public Utilities -- Promotional Advertising

4. An order of the Public Service Commission prohibiting promotional advertising by electric utilities is not violative of the right of freedom of speech in that extensive governmental regulation has always been deemed a necessary co-ordinate to their monopoly status and, under the circumstances, the importance of the free flow of commercial information is diminished since it is difficult to discern how the promotional advertising of electricity might contribute to society's interest in informed and reliable economic decisionmaking in view of the noncompetitive market in which electric corporations operate; furthermore, taking judicial notice of the present energy crisis, promotional advertising may be affirmatively detrimental to the society inasmuch as, if permitted, it would only serve to exacerbate the crisis and this constitutes a compelling justification for the ban.

Administrative Law -- Delegation of Legislative Power

5. An administrative agency is possessed of only those powers expressly delegated by the Legislature, together with those powers required by necessary implication; nevertheless, the Legislature on occasion broadly declares its will, specifying only the goals to be achieved and policies to be promoted, while leaving the implementation of a program to be worked out by an administrative body and, in such cases, the sheer breadth of delegated authority precludes a precise demarcation of the line beyond which the agency may not tread; therefore, a realistic appraisal of the particular situation is necessary to determine whether the administrative action reasonably promotes or transgresses the pronounced legislative judgment.

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Speech

6. Analysis in the First Amendment area proceeds on one of two tiers, depending upon the nature of the restriction which government has imposed: at one level, government regulation designed to suppress traditional communicative activity because of its content or potential impact is subject to the most rigorous scrutiny and, absent some compelling justification, content-oriented restrictions may not stand; on the second tier are those governmental measures which, although not directly aimed at all communication, inhibit the free flow of information or ideas and the validity of such a restraint is gauged by balancing the various competing interests, with due regard for the status of First Amendment rights in our constitutional scheme; thus, so long as a facially neutral regulation does not unduly constrict the exercise of protected rights, it is not unconstitutional.

Constitutional Law -- Freedom of Speech

7. Government may impose reasonable restrictions upon the time, place and manner of communication; however, to constitute a valid time, place and manner restriction, a regulation must be content neutral, supported by a significant governmental interest, and not foreclose alternative channels of expression.

 

301 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Nicholas v. Kahn


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


47 N.Y.2d 24; 389 N.E.2d 1086; 416 N.Y.S.2d 565; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 1932


February 8, 1979, Argued  
April 26, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Orders modified, without costs, in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, public service commission and state department of public service, sought review of a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York) that reversed the lower trial court's judgment that dismissed petitioner employees' action that sought to invalidate respondent commission's rules regarding personal investments of petitioners.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner employees filed suit after their requests for exemption from rules promulgated by respondent public service commission and state department of public service regarding their personal investments were denied. The lower trial court dismissed their actions but the lower appellate court reversed. The court found dismissal of the actions was not proper. The court first found that respondents had the authority to promulgate rules with respect to conflicts of interest caused by petitioners' personal investments. However, the court found that petitioners' contention that the denials of their requests for an exemption from the operation of the rules was arbitrary and capricious was correct. Therefore, the court modified the lower appellate decision and ruled that respondent's rules could not be enforced against any employee who had sought to be exempted from their operation.


OUTCOME: The court modified the lower appellate court order that reversed the lower trial court's judgment dismissing petitioner employees' action that sought to invalidate respondent public service commission's rules regarding the personal investments of petitioners.


HEADNOTES

Public Utilities -- Public Service Commission -- Investments by Employees, Spouses and Children

1. Although an administrative agency possesses no inherent legislative power, it may constitutionally exercise its authority by promulgating rules within the boundaries of its legislative delegation; accordingly, rules promulgated by the Chairman of the Public Service Commission which prohibit employees of the commission and the Department of Public Services, along with their spouses and minor children, from owning any interest in certain business concerns whose performance is related, at least in part, to companies regulated by the commission, are well within the legislative delegation and are therefore valid inasmuch as under the code of ethics for State officers and employees (Public Officers Law, § 74) and its enabling legislation (Executive Law, § 74), the Legislature has recognized that the task of implementing and defining the ethical considerations set forth in the statute are to be vested in the person ultimately responsible for the performance of the commission's function, i.e., its chairman.

Public Utilities -- Public Service Commission -- Investments by Employees, Spouses and Children

2. Rules promulgated by the Chairman of the Public Service Commission, which prohibit employees of the commission and the Department of Public Service, along with their spouses and minor children, from owning any interest in certain business concerns whose performance is related, at least in part, to companies regulated by the commission although permitting a limited class of employees and their families to apply for an exemption from the operation of the rules, may not be applied, in their present form, to those employees who have previously sought an exemption until such time as a valid exemption rule is promulgated or the chairman determines that no exemptions from the operation of the rules are warranted under circumstances where that portion of the rules pertaining to the right of an employee to secure an exemption vests that decision in the unfettered discretion of the chairman, thereby circumventing the procedural safeguards available to those applying for an exemption through judicial review inasmuch as any denial of an exemption by the chairman is arbitrary and capricious as a matter of law due to the lack of guidelines.

Administrative Law -- Delegation of Legislative Power

3. Although oftentimes combining legislative, executive and judicial functions, administrative agencies are but creatures of the Legislature and are possessed only of those powers expressly or impliedly delegated by that body; the Legislature cannot delegate its lawmaking power to an administrative agency; however, there is a manifest distinction between the legislative power to be exercised only by that body and an ancillary power to implement the policies enacted by law, i.e., the cornerstone of administrative law is derived from the principle that the Legislature may declare its will, and after fixing a primary standard, endow administrative agencies with the power to fill in the interstices in the legislative product by prescribing rules and regulations consistent with the enabling legislation.

 

302 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


303 West 42nd Street Corp. v. Klein


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


46 N.Y.2d 686; 389 N.E.2d 815; 416 N.Y.S.2d 219; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 1904


February 13, 1979, Argued  
April 3, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs, and the matter remitted to Supreme Court, New York County, for a hearing in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner corporation sought review of an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which dismissed petitioner's discriminatory enforcement claim brought against respondents, chairman and board, after code provisions were allegedly enforced discriminatorily against petitioner as part of a crackdown on businesses catering sexually explicit material.


OVERVIEW: In order to accommodate a tenant's intended use of petitioner corporation's building as an adult book store, petitioner applied for a permit to reconstruct the building. After petitioner was told to install sprinklers at two exits, petitioner was issued a temporary certificate of occupancy. However, a modification of the temporary certificate was issued, ordering sprinklerization of the entire building. Thereafter, petitioner brought a discriminatory enforcement claim against respondents, chairman and board, alleging code provisions had been discriminatorily enforced against petitioner as part of a crack down on businesses catering sexually explicit material. When the lower court dismissed petitioner's claim, petitioner sought review, and the court reversed. The court held that an evidentiary hearing had been required because petitioner had established a prima facie case of discriminatory enforcement. The court held petitioner had established a prima facie case because petitioner had demonstrated that both disparate impact and invidious motive existed. Moreover, the court found petitioner's demonstration of illegitimate motive had been direct as well as indirect.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a judgment dismissing petitioner corporation's discriminatory enforcement claim brought against respondents, chairman and board, after code provisions were allegedly enforced discriminatorily as part of a crackdown on businesses catering sexually explicit material. Petitioner had established a prima facie case by demonstrating the existence of both disparate impact and invidious motive.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Equal Protection

An evidentiary hearing before a judicial tribunal regarding unconstitutional discrimination in the administering of admittedly valid laws is mandated whenever one asserting such a violation can demonstrate, on the strength of sworn affidavits and other proof supplying factual detail, a reasonable probability of success on the merits of his claim; the underlying right is equal protection of the laws (US Const, 14th Amdt, § 1; NY Const, art I, § 11) and, to invoke the right successfully, there must be not only a showing that the law was not applied to others similarly situated but also that the selective application of the law was deliberately based upon an impermissible standard such as race, religion or some other arbitrary classification. Accordingly, the dismissal of a discriminatory enforcement claim without an evidentiary hearing is an abuse of discretion as a matter of law where petitioner corporation, who leased part of its office building for use as an adult bookstore and two adult theatres, made a prima facie showing of the merits of its assertions that local fire safety provisions were being discriminatorily enforced against it as part of a crackdown on businesses catering sexually explicit material inasmuch as such allegations demonstrate different treatment based solely on the nature of the business and also demonstrate an illegitimate motive, i.e., the strict enforcement of fire safety provisions as a means of reducing the number of sex-related businesses.

 

303 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Marro v. Bartlett


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


46 N.Y.2d 674; 389 N.E.2d 808; 416 N.Y.S.2d 212; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 1903


February 13, 1979, Argued  
March 29, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant retired judge sought review of a decision by the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which reversed the trial court's ruling reversing respondent State Administrator of the Administrative Board of the Judicial Conference's denial of the retired judge's application for certification.


OVERVIEW: A retired judge sought to be certified pursuant to N.Y. Judiciary Law § 115 for a further two-year term as a judge. When his application was denied without a hearing by the Administrative Board of the Judicial Conference he filed an action in the trial court seeking an order directing that he be certified. The trial court annulled the denial of the judge's application and directed the board to certify the judge. The appellate court reversed the decision of the trial court and dismissed the petition; the judge sought review. The court determined that the board had the nearly unfettered discretion in determining whether to grant applications of former judges for certification. In the absence of claims of substance that there had been a violation of statutory proscription or promotion of a constitutionally impermissible purpose not related to the certification process, the board's certification determinations were not subject to judicial review. The decision of the appellate court was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The decision of the appellate court that reversed the decision of the trial court and dismissed the retired judge's petition was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Judges -- Certification of Retired Justice to Remain in Office

1. The Administrative Board of the Judicial Conference has nearly unfettered discretion in determining whether to grant applications (Judiciary Law, § 115, subd 1) of former Supreme Court Justices for certification for additional judicial service beyond retirement at age 70 (NY Const, art VI, § 25), a discretion which is not subject to judicial review in the absence of claims of substance that there has been a violation of constitutional or statutory proscription unrelated to the certification process itself.

Judges -- Certification of Retired Justice to Remain in Office

2. Appellant, a retired Supreme Court Justice (NY Const, art VI, § 25), is not entitled to a predetermination hearing or to any statement of the reasons for the action of the Administrative Board of the Judicial Conference regarding the rejection of his application to be certified to continue in office (Judiciary Law, § 115, subd 1) in that due process requirements are virtually nonexistent since the function of the certification is that of initiatory action, rather than that of determining a continuing entitlement; no property right is involved inasmuch as appellant was already retired by constitutional mandate, and no "stigma" attaches to the denial of certification, at least in the absence of any public disclosure of the reasons therefor.
