Bacon v. Bacon


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


46 N.Y.2d 477; 386 N.E.2d 1327; 414 N.Y.S.2d 307; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 1800


January 11, 1979, Argued  
February 15, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff mother appealed a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which modified an order of the Supreme Court at Special Term entitling the mother to a hearing limited to the question of the father's liability for medical expenses incurred in connection with plaintiff child's corrective surgery.


OVERVIEW: The mother and child claimed that Family Ct. Act § 516, which permitted a mother and putative father to enter into a binding agreement for the support of their out-of-wedlock child and barred other remedies of the mother or child for the support and education of the child, was constitutionally infirm on equal protection grounds. The court held that by furnishing an incentive to settle, the statute served to prevent the illegitimate child's support from becoming lost in the intricacies of the adjudicatory process. Therefore, the court held that the statute was thus related, in a substantial respect, to permissible and salutary governmental interests and represented a balanced approach to the sensitive problem it addressed. Accordingly, the court affirmed the appellate court's dismissing the complaint and substituting a provision declaring that the mother and child were not entitled to an increase in support payments or to the establishment of a fund for private schooling and college.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the appellate court dismissing the mother's complaint for support of the parties' minor child and substituting a provision declaring that the mother and child were not entitled to an increase in support payments or to the establishment of a fund for private schooling and college.


HEADNOTES

Children Born Out of Wedlock -- Support

 Section 516 of the Family Court Act, which permits a mother and a putative father to enter into a binding agreement for the support of their out-of-wedlock child and bars other remedies of the mother or child for support and education of the child, is not violative of the constitutional guarantee of equal protection of the laws; the statute encourages putative fathers to settle paternity claims, protects the interests of the child and mother by requiring that the agreement be judicially reviewed and approved, and helps to ensure that the child will not be without support from the father. By furnishing an incentive to settle, the statute serves to prevent the child's support from becoming lost in the intricacies of the adjudicatory process and is, therefore, related, in a substantial respect, to permissible and salutary governmental interests.

 

305 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cwikla


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


46 N.Y.2d 434; 386 N.E.2d 1070; 414 N.Y.S.2d 102; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 1795


January 9, 1979, Argued  
February 8, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Orders reversed and a new trial ordered. Cross appeal by the People dismissed.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Both defendants and the State appealed from orders of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) which affirmed, after modification, judgments convicting defendants of burglary in the first degree and possession of a dangerous instrument, and sentencing them to indeterminate terms of imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted of burglary in the first degree and possession of a dangerous instrument as a misdemeanor. They appealed, contesting the prosecution's refusal to disclose its correspondence with the parole board on behalf of its principal witness, and arguing that N.Y. Penal Law § 10.00(13), under which a handkerchief was found to be a dangerous instrument, was unconstitutional and void for vagueness. The appellate division affirmed, modifying defendants' sentences. Defendants and the State appealed. The court found that the existence of an agreement between the prosecution and a witness, made to induce the testimony of the witness, was evidence that had to be disclosed under Brady principles; the prosecutor's failure to respond to the defense counsel's specific and relevant request to produce such evidence bordered on prosecutorial misconduct; and a handkerchief used as a gag, which resulted in the victim's asphyxiation, was a dangerous instrument under § 10.00(13), and such statute was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the convictions for burglary and use of a dangerous instrument because the prosecutor failed to disclose to defense counsel the correspondence from the District Attorney's office advising the parole board of a principal prosecution witness' co-operation in the trial of defendants, his accomplices.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Fair Trial -- Duty to Disclose Exculpatory Material

1. A prosecutor is under a duty to disclose to defense counsel correspondence between the office of the District Attorney and the Parole Board advising of the co-operation of a principal prosecution witness in the trial of the witness' accomplices and expressing the hope that such co-operation will be taken into account when the witness is considered for parole. Material evidence which is in the possession of the prosecution and which is exculpatory in nature must be turned over to the defendant in order to give meaning to the constitutional right to a fair trial and when the reliability of a given witness may well be determinative of guilt or innocence, nondisclosure of evidence affecting credibility falls within that general rule; moreover, where defense counsel makes a specific request which particularizes the relevant materials sought to be produced, the failure of the prosecutor to respond to the request is seldom, if ever, excusable.

Crimes -- Possession of Dangerous Instrument

2. A handkerchief used as a gag, which led to the death of a victim from asphyxiation, is a dangerous instrument within subdivision 13 of section 10.00 of the Penal Law; such subdivision, as so interpreted, is not unconstitutional as void for vagueness.

Crimes -- Privilege against Self Incrimination

3. A defendant's constitutional privilege against self incrimination was not violated where, for the purpose of facilitating identification, he was ordered to wear a wig and facial hairs so as to conform his appearance at the lineup to his appearance at the time of the crime; furthermore, requiring defendant to wear a wig and facial hairs under such circumstances was not a significant infringement of the right, if any, which he may have to determine his appearance as he could have immediately returned to his prior appearance following his participation in the lineup.

Crimes -- Identification of Defendant

4. It is proper for a trial court to permit testimony of a prior identification pursuant to CPL 60.25, providing that an identification witness may testify that he has previously identified the defendant prior to trial if he is unable at the proceeding to identify the defendant on the basis of present recollection, where the court made a determination that a witness did not possess sufficient present recollection to permit an in-court identification of the defendant; such a ruling must be accorded the same effect as a statement by the witness that he cannot now identify the defendant.

 

306 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Wein v. Comptroller of New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


46 N.Y.2d 394; 386 N.E.2d 242; 413 N.Y.S.2d 633; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 1793


January 8, 1979, Argued  
February 6, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought review of an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which affirmed a judgment and dismissed the taxpayers suit against respondent comptroller for lack of standing.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer commenced the action as a citizen taxpayer and sought a declaratory judgment that certain bond anticipation notes issued by the state were issued in violation of the constitution. The case was dismissed and the lower court held that N.Y. State Finance Law art. 7-A denied a taxpayer standing to maintain an action with respect to the issuance of state bonds or bond anticipation notes. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that whether the taxpayer relied on the statute or the case law, he lacked standing to challenge the issuance of state bonds or bond anticipation notes.


OUTCOME: An order of the lower court dismissing the taxpayer's suit against the comptroller was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Parties -- Standing to Challenge State Bond Anticipation Notes

1. Subdivision 1 of section 123-b of the State Finance Law, which grants a citizen taxpayer standing to maintain an action with respect to State expenditures, does not apply to revenue raising; accordingly, a citizen taxpayer cannot claim standing to challenge the constitutionality of the issuance of State bond anticipation notes under the statute.

Parties -- Standing to Challenge State Bond Anticipation Notes

2. A citizen taxpayer does not have standing to challenge the constitutionality of the issuance of State bond anticipation notes under the rule adopted in Boryszewski v Brydges (37 NY2d 361), which permits a citizen taxpayer to test the constitutionality of a State statute authorizing the expenditure of State money, despite the fact that the Legislature in enacting article 7-A of the State Finance Law authorizing citizen-taxpayer actions specifically disclaimed any intent to abridge or alter rights of action or remedies now or hereafter existing (State Finance Law, § 123-i); in suits not covered by subdivision 1 of section 123-b of the State Finance Law which grants a citizen taxpayer standing to maintain an action with respect to State expenditures, the question of standing is to be resolved by the courts under common-law principles and since section 123-b does not apply to taxpayer suits involving revenue raising through State bonds and bond anticipation notes, there is a reasonably clear legislative intent to prevent taxpayer challenges in these cases and it would be inappropriate for the courts to confer standing since such a determination would, in effect, render that portion of the statute a nullity.

 

307 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Corkum v. Bartlett


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


46 N.Y.2d 424; 386 N.E.2d 1066; 414 N.Y.S.2d 98; 1979 N.Y. LEXIS 1794


January 11, 1979, Argued  
February 6, 1979, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, court employees and employee representatives, sought review of a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which reversed a decision of the trial court enjoining respondent chief administrator from promulgating a classification plan for nonjudicial employees of the unified court system.


OVERVIEW: The chief administrator attempted to hold hearings on a proposed classification plan for nonjudicial employees of the unified court system and a proposed administrative policy of the chief judge concerning the establishment of a classification plan. The proposed policy included delegating the responsibility for promulgating a plan to the chief administrator. Petitioners filed an action to enjoin the chief administrator from promulgating a plan and argued that the delegation of the authority for the plan and any plan thereby promulgated violated N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 28 and N.Y. Jud. Ct. Acts Law § 211. The court affirmed the decision of the appellate court and found that under N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 28(b), the chief judge had broad authority to delegate administrative powers to the chief administrator. Furthermore, under N.Y. Jud. Ct. Acts Law § 211(1)(d), those administrative powers clearly included personnel practices affecting nonjudicial personnel. The court also found that the chief judge's policy proposal complied with N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 28(c) because it provided detailed standards for the chief administrator to follow in promulgating the plan.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's reversal of the trial court's decision in favor of petitioners in their action to enjoin the chief administrator from promulgating a classification plan for nonjudicial employees of the unified court system.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- Unified Court System -- Classification System for Nonjudicial Personnel

The Chief Administrator of the courts has the power to establish a classification plan for nonjudicial employees of the unified court system when directly delegated that authority by the Chief Judge of the Court of Appeals, and he may conduct public hearings (Judiciary Law, § 212, subd 1, par [h]) in furtherance of that purpose, as well as hearings on the proposed administrative policy of the Chief Judge concerning the establishment of a classification plan and an appeals procedure, which policy had been approved by the Court of Appeals after consultation by the Chief Judge with the Administrative Board of the courts, inasmuch as subdivision b of section 28 of article VI of the New York State Constitution provides that the Chief Administrator shall supervise the administration and operation of the unified court system, and with respect to supervision or management, as distinguished from policy formulation, the Constitution places no limitations on the duties the Chief Judge may delegate to the administrator; neither consultation with the Administrative Board nor approval of the Court of Appeals is a prerequisite to the exercise of supervisory powers by the Chief Administrator, although such consultation and approval are required before the Chief Judge may establish standards and administrative policies (NY Const, art VI, § 28, subd c), and subdivision b's grant to him of "such additional powers and duties as may be provided by law" is merely a further source from which to add to the broad administrative authority of that office. The hearings, both the one required on the proposed declaration of policy and the one volunteered on the classification plan and title standards, may be held jointly, since although the latter could not be adopted until after the former, it is implicit that the actual adoption of the plan or any revision of it would not take place until the guidelines established by the policy or any revised version of it had first been adopted, and, as long as the plan is promulgated subsequent to and consistent with a prior establishment of policy, the dual presentation at the public hearing is to be viewed as nothing more than a permissibly expedient way of preventing unnecessary delay in the implementation of a classification system during a time of transition from the former system of court administration to the present one.

Monroe v. Carey

309 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Hotel Dorset Co. v. Trust for Cultural Resources


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


46 N.Y.2d 358; 385 N.E.2d 1284; 413 N.Y.S.2d 357; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 2425


November 27, 1978, Argued  
December 27, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) which reversed, on the law, an order of the lower court granting defendants' motions for summary judgment, and granted summary judgment to plaintiff declaring N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law arts. 13-E and 13-F unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff challenged the constitutionality of the New York State and New York City Cultural Resources Acts, N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law arts. 13-E and 13-F, which provided support for the continuation of financially troubled cultural institutions and museums in the state, and the immediate effect of which was to permit the Museum of Modern Art to realize income through tax equivalency payments that would be made upon the completion and sale or rental of condominium apartments to be constructed above the museum facilities. The court reversed the order of the appellate court and granted judgment to defendants declaring the challenged legislation constitutional, holding that even though only one museum presently met the statutory specifications, absent a showing that other institutions could not also meet them, the legislation was not unconstitutional as special legislation applicable only to a single enterprise. The court found that the legislature had clearly stated that these statutory plans comprise general legislation applicable statewide to institutions and were not special or local legislation.


OUTCOME: The order of the appellate court was reversed and the order of the trial court was reinstate because the court found the challenged legislation was not special or local legislation, and as such was constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- New York State Cultural Resources Act

1. The New York State Cultural Resources Act (General Municipal Law, art 13-E) provides support for the continuation of financially troubled cultural institutions and museums in the State, by providing for the creation of cultural trusts, which, when authorized by separate legislation (General Municipal Law, art 13-F), are given the power to assist participating cultural institutions in the construction of combined-use facilities, i.e., institutions where part is devoted to the cultural purpose, and part used for commercial purposes designed to produce financial support for the cultural part, and which are granted a general tax exemption, including an exemption from real estate taxation on the combined-use facility developed by the trust. The immediate effect of these acts was to permit the Museum of Modern Art to realize income through tax equivalency payments that would be made upon the completion and sale or rental of condominium apartments to be constructed above the museum facilities, and although that is the only museum presently meeting the statutory specifications, absent a showing that other institutions could not meet them also, or absent a finding to that effect which could be made as a matter of law, the legislation is not unconstitutional as special legislation applicable only to a single enterprise.

Statutes -- Standard of Review

2. Courts are required to exercise a large measure of restraint when considering highly intricate and imaginative schemes for public financing or for public expenditures designed to be in the public interest, and when a court reviews such a decision, it must operate on the rule that it may not substitute its judgment for that of the body which made the decision.

Statutes -- Presumption of Constitutionality

3. It is presumed that an act of the Legislature is constitutional and this presumption can be upset only by proof persuasive beyond a reasonable doubt.

Statutes -- Presumptions

4. It is presumed that the Legislature has investigated and found facts necessary to support the legislation enacted by it, as well as the existence of a situation showing or indicating its need or desirability.

Constitutional Law -- New York State Cultural Resources Act

5. The New York State Cultural Resources Act (General Municipal Law, art 13-E) provides support for the continuation of financially troubled cultural institutions and museums in the State, by providing for the creation of cultural trusts, which, when authorized by separate legislation (General Municipal Law, art 13-F), are given the power to assist participating cultural institutions in the construction of combined-use facilities, i.e., institutions where part is devoted to the cultural purpose, and part used for commercial purposes designed to produce financial support for the cultural part, and which are granted a general tax exemption, including an exemption from real estate taxation on the combined-use facility developed by the trust. Any argument that the enactments are invalid fails since there is ample precedent to support this sort of financing effort, especially with respect to the device of having a public benefit corporation, i.e., the trust, utilize the power of condemnation, which it is empowered to do under article 13-F of the General Municipal Law, and legitimize the tax exemption for property, a portion of which is used for commercial purposes.

Eminent Domain -- Condemnation by Public Benefit Corporation

6. A public benefit corporation can condemn property for a public use, if authorized by statute.

Legislature -- Preservation of Cultural Institutions

7. If the State Legislature and a responsible unit of local government believe that the preservation of cultural institutions is in the public interest in that visitors will be produced in the city in which they are located, and benefit all who attend, city residents and visitors from throughout the State alike, legislation is legitimate which thus subsidizes the institutions.

Constitutional Law -- New York State Cultural Resources Act -- Approval of General Law at Local Level

8. The New York State Cultural Resources Act (General Municipal Law, art 13-E) provides support for the continuation of financially troubled cultural institutions and museums in the State, by providing for the creation of cultural trusts, which, when authorized by separate legislation (General Municipal Law, art 13-F), are given the power to assist participating cultural institutions in the construction of combined-use facilities, i.e., institutions where part is devoted to the cultural purpose, and part used for commercial purposes designed to produce financial support for the cultural part, and which are granted a general tax exemption, including an exemption from real estate taxation on the combined-use facility developed by the trust. The fact that the provisions of the New York City Cultural Resources Act (General Municipal Law, art 13-F) did not become operative until the Board of Estimate approved them did not render the act a special law, since the Legislature can, without creating a special or local law, provide that a general law will not become effective until approved at the local level, although such approval is not necessary.

Statutes -- Local Laws

9. The traditional and legal test for a local law is whether the statute affects the "property, affairs or government" of a governmental subdivision of the State (NY Const, art IX, § 2) but that terminology does not prohibit the State from legislating with respect to these concerns; if the subject matter of the legislation is of sufficient importance to the State generally, the legislation cannot be deemed a local law even though it deals directly with the affairs of a municipality.

Statutes -- General Laws

10. Where a legislative exemption from taxation is granted to a class, there is no constitutional impairment, since an act need not apply to all persons, places or things in the State to be deemed general; accordingly, where a legislative method of providing aid has an equal impact on all members of a rationally defined class similarly situated, the law is thus a general and not a special one.

 

310 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Mayer v. City Rent Agency


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


46 N.Y.2d 139; 385 N.E.2d 605; 412 N.Y.S.2d 867; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 2387


November 29, 1978, Argued  
December 21, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order modified, with costs to plaintiffs on defendants' and intervenors' appeals and without costs to defendants or intervenors on plaintiffs' appeal, and the case remitted to Supreme Court, New York County, for the entry of judgment and further proceedings, if any, in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, city rent agency, city, and tenants, appealed an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) that affirmed the trial court's judgment, which granted plaintiff landlords' motion for summary judgment, denied the city rent agency's, city's, and tenants' cross-motion for summary judgment, adjudged New York City, N.Y., Local Law No. 76 (1977), null and void, and enjoined its enforcement.


OVERVIEW: The landlords sought a declaration that New York City, N.Y., Local Law No. 76 (1977) was invalid and conflicted with a prior law, which prohibited the enactment of local laws subjecting rent controlled housing to more stringent or restrictive regulation and control than in effect in 1971. The trial court granted the landlords summary judgment and declared New York City, N.Y., Local Law No. 76 (1977) invalid. The appellate court affirmed the judgment of the trial court and the city rent agency, city, and tenants appealed. While the appeal was pending, a commissioner announced approval of the local law, even though it was deemed to impose more restrictive regulatory control. The court held that: (1) the local law did impose more restrictive regulatory control than was previously in effect; (2) the local law was not validated by the commissioner's approval because his approval authority did not extend to local laws or ordinances; and (3) the landlords were entitled to apply for an order directing that previously authorized rent increases by the city rent agency would be effective prior to the date of that order depending upon the particular controlled premises.


OUTCOME: The order of the appellate court affirming the judgment of the trial court was modified without prejudice to the right of any of the landlords. The case was remitted to the trial court for an entry of judgment declaring that the commissioner's approval of the ordinance at issue did not validate the ordinance.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and Tenant -- Rent Regulation

The "Labor Cost Repeal Law" (Local Laws, 1977, No. 76 of City of New York) attempted to clarify the "labor cost pass-along" provision of the city's rent control laws (Local Laws, 1970, No. 30 ["Maximum Base Rent Law"]) which established a maximum base rent (MBR) ceiling for each rent controlled apartment and provided that when the MBR was more than 7 1/2% above the rent in effect on December 31, 1971, with certain exceptions, the collectible rent could be increased by no more than 7 1/2% a year, and that notwithstanding the foregoing limitation, the maximum rent should be increased when labor expenses exceeded payroll expenses. Local Law No. 76, which provided that the labor cost pass-alongs were to be included in and subject to, and not added to, the 7 1/2% annual rent ceiling and that, as to any labor cost pass-along theretofore granted that had resulted in a rent increase in excess of 7 1/2% in one year, such increase was void and was to be refunded to the tenant, is invalid because it violated the statutory prohibition against the enactment of local laws subjecting rent controlled housing accommodations to more stringent or restrictive regulation and control than that in effect on June 1, 1971 (L 1971, ch 372), since under Local Law No. 30 as originally enacted authorized rent increases based on labor cost pass-along were allowed in addition to annual 7 1/2% permitted increases, whereas Local Law No. 76 purported to place labor cost pass-along increases under the general 7 1/2% ceiling, and the law was not validated in consequence of its approval by the State Commissioner of Housing and Community Renewal, since the authority of approval of the commissioner is limited to local rules and regulations and does not extend to local laws or ordinances.

 

311 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Carr v. Carr


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


46 N.Y.2d 270; 385 N.E.2d 1234; 413 N.Y.S.2d 305; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 2418


October 20, 1978, Argued  
December 21, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, without costs, and the action dismissed. Question certified answered in the negative.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York) reversed an order of the trial court granting defendant second wife's motion to dismiss plaintiff first wife's complaint for lack of in rem and personal jurisdiction over the second wife. The second wife appealed.


OVERVIEW: After learning that the second wife had applied for survivor benefits when her husband died, the first wife filed a declaratory action seeking to adjudicate the validity of her earlier marriage to the decedent. The first wife alleged that after she and the decedent were married for several years, the decedent obtained an ex parte Honduran divorce. The court reversed the appellate court's order denying the second wife's motion to dismiss for lack of jurisdiction, holding that in the absence of any minimum contacts between the second wife and the forum state, that state lacked jurisdiction over her. The fiction of the marital res ceased to have any validity or legal substance after the decedent's death, rendering the exercise of jurisdiction in violation of the Due Process Clause.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate order, without costs, and dismissed the action.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- Jurisdiction -- In Personam Jurisdiction

1. In the absence of "minimum contacts" with the State of New York, New York courts have no jurisdiction over the nonresident second wife of a deceased husband, where the first wife seeks to obtain a declaration as to the validity of the first marriage, since the fiction of the marital res ceases to have any vitality or legal substance after the death of either husband or wife, and to uphold personal jurisdiction in such a case would be to ignore the restraints imposed by the due process clause.

Husband and Wife -- Divorce Jurisdiction -- Domicile Requirement

2. Divorce jurisdiction may be exercised only when at least one of the parties to the marriage is domiciled in the State; so fundamental is the domicile requirement that it is a constitutional prerequisite to the recognition of a divorce decree under the full faith and credit clause. (US Const, art IV, § 1.)

 

312 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Thomas


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


46 N.Y.2d 100; 385 N.E.2d 584; 412 N.Y.S.2d 845; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 2383


October 26, 1978, Argued  
December 7, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed and the case remitted to the Appellate Term, First Department, for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed from a judgment of the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Circuit (New York) that reversed defendant's conviction for possession of gambling records and driving while intoxicated.


OVERVIEW: After being injured in a single car accident, defendant was transported to a hospital by police and was asked to submit to a blood test to determine his blood alcohol content. Defendant refused the test, and evidence of his refusal was admitted at trial pursuant to N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 1194(4). Defendant's conviction was reversed on appeal, and the State sought review. The court reversed. The court noted that the United States Supreme Court had recently held that blood test results were not testimonial or communicative evidence and that, therefore, admission of these results did not violate the privilege against self-incrimination. The court concluded that evidence of one's refusal to submit to a blood test also did not violate the self-incrimination privilege so long as there was no compulsion to refuse to take the test. Defendant was under no compulsion to refuse the test; therefore, this evidence was admissible against him.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed because the admission of evidence of defendant's refusal to submit to a blood test was constitutional. Defendant was not compelled to refuse the test.


HEADNOTES

Motor Vehicles -- Chemical Tests -- Refusal to Submit to Blood Test

1. Subdivision 4 of section 1194 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law which states that evidence of a defendant's refusal to submit to a chemical test to determine the alcoholic content of his blood shall be admissible in any trial based on a violation of section 1192 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law, provided he was given sufficient warning, in clear and unequivocal language, of the effect of such refusal, does not violate defendant's privilege against self incrimination under either the Federal or State Constitution. (US Const, 5th Amdt; NY Const, art 1, § 6.) Although the evidence of defendant's refusal to take the test can be classified as communicative or testimonial, it should be admissible so long as the defendant was under no compulsion to refuse the test, and under the circumstances, submission to the test, not its evasion, was the desired result. In being presented with the choice, defendant was confronted with no impairment of any right enjoyed by him, for he had no constitutional privilege not to submit to the chemical test and the statutory right not to take the test was qualified by express provision for admissibility of proof of refusal. (Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 1194, subd 4.) Furthermore, the admissibility of refusal evidence may also be viewed as a permissible condition reasonably attached to the grant of permission to operate a motor vehicle on the highways of the State inasmuch as no physical characteristic or condition could be more closely related to incompetence to operate a motor vehicle than inebriation, and no aspect of motor vehicle regulation can be more important to the welfare of both operators and the public than keeping inebriated drivers off the public highways.

Crimes -- Credibility of Witness -- Hostility as Affecting Credibility

2. It is recognized as a general proposition that as to any witness hostility to the party against whom he testifies may be shown to affect his credibility. However, it should be left to the sound discretion of the trial court whether to admit or to exclude as too remote evidence of hostility; accordingly, it cannot be said that the trial court was erroneous as a matter of law in preventing defense counsel from questioning two arresting officers as to their knowledge, at the time of the arrest of defendant, regarding a civilian complaint which he had filed against a fellow officer, where the nature or quality of the relationship between the witness and the officer against whom the complaint had been lodged, the character or seriousness of the complaint, or whether it was warranted or could reasonably have been viewed as an unjustified attack on a public servant were never established. In most instances, at least where the hostility-provoking circumstance or action involves the witness himself, such factors are properly classified as going to probative worth rather than admissibility.

 

313 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Watergate II Apartments v. Buffalo Sewer Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


46 N.Y.2d 52; 385 N.E.2d 560; 412 N.Y.S.2d 821; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 2378


September 15, 1978, Argued  
December 6, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: On plaintiff's appeal: Order affirmed.

On defendant's cross appeal: Appeal dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant developer sought review of a decision by the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which reversed an order granting summary judgment to the developer in the developer's declarative action to determine whether charges imposed by respondent sewer authority were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The developer entered into a tax abatement agreement with the city under which its apartment property was exempted from taxes over a certain limit. The sewer authority subsequently billed the developer for "sewer rents" based on the property's actual tax value. The developer contended that the "sewer rent" was an unconstitutional tax because it bore no relationship to actual consumption. The court held that: (1) the sewer authority was not a taxing jurisdiction subject to the tax abatement agreement with the city; (2) the "sewer rent" bore a direct relationship to the cost of furnishing the services because the cost went beyond actual consumption and included costs for sewer lines, treatment facilities, water distribution, and other developments; and (3) the sewer authority's criterion for apportioning sewer rents was a reasonable exercise of its power.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order granting summary judgment to the developer.


HEADNOTES

Appeal -- Misdescription in Notice of Appeal

1. Although no appeal can be taken from a decision, if a party is otherwise entitled to invoke the review of an appellate court, such a defect in the notice of appeal will be treated as a mere misdescription.

Administrative Law -- Exhaustion of Remedies

2. One who objects to the act of an administrative agency must exhaust available administrative remedies before being permitted to litigate in a court of law.

Administrative Law -- Exhaustion of Remedies -- Exceptions

3. Although it is the general rule that one who objects to the act of an administrative agency must exhaust available administrative remedies before being permitted to litigate in a court of law, the rule need not be followed when an agency's action is challenged as either unconstitutional or wholly beyond its grant of power, or when resort to an administrative remedy would be futile, or when its pursuit would cause irreparable injury.

Actions -- Type of Action Prescribed by Statute

4. Where a statute expressly designates a CPLR article 78 proceeding as the sole route to relief from its invalid application, resort to another form of judicial scrutiny nevertheless may be had when the statute is alleged to be unconstitutional, by its terms or application, or where it is attacked as wholly inapplicable; there is no reason why this principle should have less force where our jurisprudence requires that one challenging the enforcement of a statute first pursue administrative remedies.

Public Authorities -- Reasonableness of Sewer Rents

5. Inasmuch as a sewer authority is empowered to establish a schedule of sewer rents (Public Authorities Law, § 1180) and the authority can fix charges only for such services as it actually provides its customers (Public Authorities Law, § 1178, subd 11), in determining whether the formulae used by the authority reflect reasonable and nonarbitrary interpretations of the statute, exact congruence between the cost of the services provided and the rates charged to particular customers is not required; where only an approximation of cost or value is possible, discrepancies may have to be endured in the name of administrative flexibility so long as there exists some rational underpinning for the charges levied.

Public Authorities -- Computation of Sewer Rents

6. A city sewer authority has the power to levy sewer rents computed on the basis of assessed valuation of the plaintiff's property in the face of a tax limitation agreement between the plaintiff and the city since the agreement limited plaintiff's liability with respect to taxes imposed by or on behalf of any taxing jurisdiction as defined in section 125 (subd 1, par [d]) of the Private Housing Finance Law and that definition excludes public benefit corporations such as a sewer authority from its reach, since the sewer charges bear a direct relationship to the broader reality of the services and benefits actually rendered to property owners as a whole as required by section 1180 of the Public Authorities Law and thus are rents rather than taxes, and since there is no less reason to apply the sewer charges to the exempt portion of the plaintiff's property than to that which is nonexempt.

 

314 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Mandels v. Liberty Mut. Ins. Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


45 N.Y.2d 455; 382 N.E.2d 762; 410 N.Y.S.2d 62; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 2270


September 13, 1978, Submitted  
October 26, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff insured person sought review of a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which reversed the trial court's order granting a motion by the insured to dismiss an affirmative defense of defendant insurance company and for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The insured was operating his automobile when it as involved in a collision and his wife, a passenger, was injured. The wife commenced an action against the owner and operator of the other vehicle, who impleaded the insured. The insured requested the insurance company to defend and indemnify him in the third-party action. The insurance company disclaimed asserting that amendments to N.Y. Ins. Law § 167(3) applied only to causes of action that arose on or after August 1, 1976. The appellate division denied a motion by the insured to dismiss the insurance company's affirmative defense. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that, although the amendments did not specifically mention or refer to third-party claims, it was clear that they applied only where the accident giving rise to possible liability occurred on or after August 1, 1976; and for accidents occurring before that date, third-party suits were excluded from coverage. Because the accident occurred prior to August 1, 1976, the court held that the appellate division correctly determined that the insurance company had no obligation to defend or to indemnify the insured.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the appellate division, which denied a motion by the insured to dismiss the insurance company's affirmative defense in the insured's request for an order requiring the insurance company to defend and indemnify him in a third-party cause of action.


HEADNOTES

Insurance -- Automobile Liability Insurance -- Injuries to Spouse

Under an amendment to subdivision 3 of section 167 of the Insurance Law, which provided that the exclusion from automobile liability insurance coverage of an insured because injuries to his spouse would apply only where the injured spouse, to be entitled to recover, must prove the culpable conduct of the insured spouse (L 1976, ch 616) and which amendment was to apply to all causes of action arising on or after August 1, 1976 (L 1976, ch 617), an insurer is not obligated to defend or indemnify its insured in a third-party action commenced on November 17, 1976 against the insured who was operating his automobile on May 30, 1976 when it was involved in a collision in which his wife sustained injuries, the wife having commenced an action against the operator of the other vehicle, the third-party plaintiff, in October, 1976. The provision establishing the effective date refers to the main cause of action which arises immediately upon the occurrence of the accident and it necessarily follows that the establishment of that date, insofar as it affects an insurer's obligation to respond to a third-party claim, is likewise limited to those situations where the main claim arises on or after August 1, 1976; for accidents occurring before that date third-party suits are excluded from coverage.

 

315 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Marcus Associates, Inc. v. Huntington


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


45 N.Y.2d 501; 382 N.E.2d 1323; 410 N.Y.S.2d 546; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 2285


September 11, 1978, Submitted  
October 26, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff company appealed a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which held that defendant town's zoning amendment was valid. The zoning amendment provided for a limited number of uses and occupants of premises in a light industrial district. The appellate division found that the amendment was constitutional or did not deprive the company of all reasonable use of its property.


OVERVIEW: The company commenced the action to obtain a declaration of the zoning amendment's invalidity, and contended that enactment of the subject amendment was beyond the town's zoning power and was unconstitutional. The court held rejected the company's argument that population density was not a proper subject of zoning regulation in industrial areas. The court held that the company offered no evidence demonstrating its inability to realize a reasonable return on the regulated land, and that the record revealed an overwhelming majority of the successfully developed parcels in the zone conformed to the strictures of the recent amendment. The court held that the zoning amendment did not deprive the company of all reasonable use of the zoned plots. The court held that legitimate governmental goals included those which promoted public health, safety, morals, or general welfare. The court held that the aim of the town's zoning amendment was to preserve the established character of the area, which was a permissible goal. The court found a reasonable nexus between the town's objective and the zoning ordinance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the appellate division, with costs, and held that the town's challenged zoning ordinance amendment was valid. The court ascertained that the challenged ordinance bore a rational relationship to a legitimate goal of government.


HEADNOTES

Statutes -- Presumption of Constitutionality

1. The strong presumption of constitutionality of legislative enactments applies not only to enactments of the Legislature but to ordinances of municipalities as well; while the presumption is rebuttable, unconstitutionality must be demonstrated beyond a reasonable doubt and only as a last resort should courts strike down legislation on the ground of unconstitutionality.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Requirement of Legislative Delegation

2. In order to withstand judicial scrutiny, a municipality's exercise of its zoning power must be founded upon a legislative delegation to so proceed; such a delegation is found in section 261 of the Town Law.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Population Density

3. Population density is a proper subject of zoning regulation in both residential and industrial areas, within the legislative delegation of section 261 of the Town Law.

Statute -- Construction

4. An unambiguous statute must be applied in accordance with its express terms; courts should not add restrictions or limitations where none exist, nor should they interpret what has no need of interpretation.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning -- Constitutional Challenge

5. A constitutional attack upon a zoning measure will succeed where the property owner establishes that no reasonable return may be had from any permitted use; it is not enough to show that the subject land has merely suffered a significant diminution in value as a result of the zoning regulation. Where the plaintiff, in challenging a zoning ordinance amendment providing for a limited number of uses and occupants of premises in a light industrial district, offers no evidence demonstrating its inability to realize a reasonable return on the regulated land, and many parcels in the zone conform to the amendment, the ordinance does not deprive plaintiff of all reasonable use of the zoned plots.

Statutes -- Relationship to Legitimate Governmental Object

6. A legislative enactment must bear at least a minimal relationship to a legitimate governmental object, which is one which in some way promotes the public health, safety, morals or general welfare; a court may inquire into the relationship of an ordinance to a proper governmental interest, but cannot substitute its judgment for that of the legislative body as to the necessity, wisdom or expediency of the legislative act.

Municipal Corporations -- Zoning

7. Where the express aim of a town board in enacting a zoning ordinance amendment providing for a limited number of uses and occupants of premises in a light industrial district is to preserve the character of the area, a permissible goal of a legislative enactment, there is a reasonable nexus between the town's objective and the zoning ordinance, and the ordinance bears a rational relationship to a legitimate governmental goal.

 

316 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Baden v. Staples


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


45 N.Y.2d 889; 383 N.E.2d 110; 410 N.Y.S.2d 808; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 2312


September 21, 1978, Argued  
October 24, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs. Question certified answered in the affirmative.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, automobile owner and driver, appealed the order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the trial court's order granting a motion to attach an insurance policy. Plaintiffs, passengers in another vehicle, filed the motion in their negligence action.


OVERVIEW: The owner and the driver were residents of New Hampshire, where the accident took place. The passengers were apparently residents of New York. Because personal jurisdiction over the driver and the owner was not available in New York, the passengers instead sought to obtain jurisdiction by attaching the insurance obligation owed the driver and the owner by their liability insurance carrier, a corporation doing business in New York. This procedure was sanctioned in the prior caselaw. The driver and the owner argued that such an attachment procedure was unconstitutional and that sine the holding was premised on another case, which was subsequently overruled, it must also fall. The court held that, although the legislature was free to change the rule, the court was bound by consideration of stare decisis and institutional stability, absent compelling grounds to the contrary, to reject the challenge to the established caselaw rule. Because the driver and the owner had failed to present necessary "cogent reasons" the court declined to abandon a precedent that evidently worked no great injustice. Therefore, the court affirmed the order and affirmatively answered the certified question.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order rendered by the intermediate court of appeals, which had affirmed the trial court's order granting the passengers' motion for an attachment of the insurance policy of the driver and the owner of the other vehicle in the passengers' negligence action.


HEADNOTES

Attachment -- Attachment of Insurance Policy

The Appellate Division properly affirmed an order granting a motion to attach an insurance policy since the Seider v Roth (17 NY2d 111) rule satisfies the jurisdictional standards set forth in Shaffer v Heitner (433 U.S. 186), and considerations of stare decisis and institutional stability and the fact that the application of the rule does not appear to have worked extensive injustice during its 12-year history, require that it be upheld. The primary risks and burdens of defending a Seider-type action rest on the insurer, who does business in New York, and over whom even personal jurisdiction may, of course, be obtained. The burdens of the nonresident insured, although existent, are not sufficient to prevent, constitutionally, the New York courts from adjudicating a dispute principally between a New York plaintiff and an insurance company doing business in New York.

Gold v. Shapiro


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


45 N.Y.2d 849; 382 N.E.2d 767; 410 N.Y.S.2d 68; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 2274


September 13, 1978, Argued  
October 19, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Bail -- Prohibition of Bail -- Constutional Law

In a proceeding to prohibit an individial Justice of the Appellate Division from siging an order effectuating his decision to grant bail and a stay of execution to a defendant pending his appeal to the Appellate Division from a conviction of a class A-III felony, the Appellate Division properly granted the application, and its judgment is affirmed on the opinion thereat which concluded that the statute ( CPL 530.50) providing that bail was not available to a defendant receiving a class A felony sentence pending his appeal, was constitutional and rationally [**3] related to legitimate State interests, and therefore respondent Justice was without power to consider the application for bail and a stay.

 

318 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Sharrock v. Dell Buick-Cadillac, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


45 N.Y.2d 152; 379 N.E.2d 1169; 408 N.Y.S.2d 39; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 2106


April 26, 1978, Argued  
July 11, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff owner challenged the statutory authorization afforded a garageman to foreclose a lien for repair and storage charges, N.Y. Lien Law §184, by means of a public sale of the vehicle in his possession. The Appellate Division modified the lower court's order, granted summary judgment to plaintiff, and remitted to Westchester County for a trial on the issue of damages as raised by plaintiff's complaint and defendants' counterclaim.


OVERVIEW: The question was certified by the Appellate Division whether its order was properly made. The court affirmed the order and answered the question in the affirmative. N.Y. Lien Law §§200, 201, 202 and 204, insofar as they empowered a garageman to conduct an ex parte sale of a bailed vehicle, violated the due process clause, N.Y. Const. art. I, § 6, since they failed to provide the owner of the vehicle with an opportunity to be heard prior to permanent deprivation of a significant property interest. Fundamental notions of procedural due process required that before the state deprived a person of a significant property interest in aid of a creditor, that person be given notice and an opportunity to be heard prior to deprivation of that interest. So tested, the statutes did not pass muster in light of the due process standards articulated in analogous circumstances by the United States Supreme Court. The garageman's right to retain his possessory lien was unaffected by this decision, but he could not sell the vehicle to satisfy his claim unless and until a method was devised, consistent with due process, of affording the owner some opportunity to be heard.


OUTCOME: The order granting summary judgment to plaintiff and declaring that certain sections of the Lien Law were unconstitutional was affirmed, with costs, and the certified question answered in the affirmative.


HEADNOTES

Liens -- Garageman's Lien

1. Sections 200, 201, 202 and 204 of the Lien Law, which authorize a garageman to foreclose his possessory lien for repair and storage charges (Lien Law, § 184) by means of a public sale of the vehicle in his possession, violate the due process clause of the State Constitution (art I, § 6) insofar as they empower a garageman to conduct an ex parte sale of a bailed vehicle, since they deprive the owner of the vehicle of a significant property interest without providing any opportunity to be heard.

Constitutional Law -- Due Process

2. The threshold question in any judicial inquiry into conduct claimed to be violative of the Fourteenth Amendment of the Federal Constitution is whether the State has in some fashion involved itself in what, in another setting, would otherwise be deemed private activity; purely private conduct does not rise to the level of constitutional significance absent a significant nexus between the State and the actors or the conduct; this nexus, which has been denominated "State action" is an essential requisite to any action grounded on violation of equal protection of the laws or deprivation of due process of law; further, where the impetus for the allegedly unconstitutional conduct is private, the State must have significantly involved itself for that action to fall within the ambit of the Fourteenth Amendment.

Constitutional Law -- Due Process

3. The mere fact that an activity might not constitute State action for purposes of the Federal due process clause (US Const, 14th Amdt), which is phrased in terms of State deprivation of life, liberty or property, does not perforce necessitate that the same conclusion be reached when the conduct is claimed to be violative of the State due process clause (NY Const, art I, § 6), which contains no language requiring State action and thus permits application of a more flexible State involvement requirement than is currently being imposed by the Supreme Court with respect to the Federal provision.

Liens -- Garageman's Lien

4. While the possessory nature of the lien which a garageman has on a vehicle until his charges are satisfied (Lien Law, § 184) does not constitute State action sufficient to trigger the protections afforded by the due process clause of the State Constitution (art I, § 6), the State, by authorizing the enforcement of that lien by means of an ex parte sale of the bailed vehicle (Lien Law, §§ 200, 201, 202, 204) without first affording its owner an opportunity to be heard, has entwined itself into the debtor-creditor relationship arising out of otherwise regular consumer transactions, and has delegated to private parties functions traditionally associated with sovereignty, and this does constitute meaningful State participation which triggers the protections afforded by that clause.

Constitutional Law -- Due Process

5. Fundamental notions of procedural due process require that, before the State may deprive a person of a significant property interest in aid of a creditor, that person be given notice and an opportunity to be heard.

Liens -- Garageman's Lien

6. While it is a reasonable exercise of the State's police power to give a garageman a security interest in vehicles which he has enhanced in value (Lien Law, § 184), it is unreasonable and constitutionally impermissible to provide absolutely no safeguards against unauthorized or unnecessary repairs or storage charges which may underlie the whole transaction, especially where an ownership interest hangs in the balance; nor may the statutory provisions for summary sale of a vehicle by a garageman without providing the owner with an opportunity for a hearing (Lien Law, §§ 200, 201, 202, 204) be justified on the ground that the initial retention of the vehicle arose out of a voluntary transaction, since, at most, voluntary delivery of the vehicle creates a bailor-bailee relationship, which could not divest the bailor of his continuing interest and title.

 

319 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Tuscan Dairy Farms, Inc. v. Barber


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


45 N.Y.2d 215; 380 N.E.2d 179; 408 N.Y.S.2d 348; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 2137


June 1, 1978, Argued  
July 11, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant milk dealer sought review of the determination of respondent Commissioner of Agriculture and Markets (commissioner) that denied the dealer's application for an extension of its milk dealer's license. The dealer appealed, on constitutional grounds, from a judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York) that unanimously confirmed the commissioner's determination.


OVERVIEW: The milk dealer contended that that the commissioner's determination was not supported by a preponderance of the evidence before him as required by N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 258-c and that the commissioner's application of the statute to the dealer violated the Commerce Clause of the United States Constitution. The court affirmed the appellate court's confirmation of the commissioner's denial of the dealer's application for an extension of its license and held that the application of § 258-c did not violate the Commerce Clause. The court concluded that the commissioner's denial of the license did not have as its objective the economic protection of the state's milk industry or even that of the county in which the dealer wanted to operate. Rather, the commissioner's concern was addressed to the maintenance of the existing "balanced milk distribution structure" to protect consumers. The court found that on the basis of the evidence introduced by the Department of Agriculture and Markets, taken with the dealer's failure to offer any countervailing evidence, the commissioner's determination was supported by the preponderance of the evidence before him.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court that upheld the commissioner's decision to deny the milk dealer's application to extend its license.


HEADNOTES

Agriculture -- Milk Control -- Interstate Commerce

1. The denial by the Commissioner of Agriculture and Markets of the extension of a milk dealer's license for the delivery of milk on a wholesale basis into the County of Richmond to a New Jersey dealer, which was licensed to make wholesale deliveries of milk and milk products into Orange and Rockland Counties and deliveries of other dairy products into most of the remaining counties of the State, did not violate the commerce clause of the United States Constitution. The denial did not have as its objective the economic protection of the milk industry of New York State or Richmond County, but was addressed to the maintenance of the existing balanced milk distribution structure for the protection of the welfare of the consumers in the public interest and, although the denial would incidentally provide a measure of economic protection to businesses already operating within the existing system, that consequence should not serve to abrogate the ultimate, legitimate objective of preserving a system necessary to serve the varied needs of milk consumers in the county. Section 258-c of the Agriculture and Markets Law, providing for the granting and extension of milk dealers' licenses, on its face does not discriminate with respect to the activities of intrastate and out-of-State suppliers as tending to a destructive competition in a market already adequately served, on the basis of which licensing may be withheld, and the statute does not represent any attempt at extraterritorial control of trade or commerce.

Agriculture -- Milk Control -- Licensing

2. The denial by the Commissioner of Agriculture and Markets of the extension of a milk dealer's license for the delivery of milk on a wholesale basis into the County of Richmond to a New Jersey dealer, which was licensed to make wholesale deliveries of milk and milk products into Orange and Rockland Counties, on the ground that the extension would tend to a destructive competition in a market already adequately served and as such would not be in the public interest, was supported by a preponderance of the evidence. The direct evidence, together with the inferences reasonably to be drawn therefrom, supports the conclusion that the introduction of a new distributor to supermarkets only, with adverse effect on distributors presently serving small retailers and themselves engaged in retail sales, would tend to a competition for milk sales which would be destructive by reason of the potential disturbance of the balanced distribution structure. (See Agriculture and Markets Law, § 258-c.)

Constitutional Law -- Police Power -- Milk Control

3. The milk industry within the State is a proper subject for regulation under the State's police power.

Constitutional Law -- Interstate Commerce

4. Legislation enacted solely for the protection of local economic interests by the restriction of competition from without the State is inherently unconstitutional.

Constitutional Law -- Interstate Commerce

5. Where a statute regulates evenhandedly to effectuate a legitimate local public interest and its effects on interstate commerce are only incidental, it will be upheld unless the burden imposed on such commerce is clearly excessive in relation to the putative local benefits.

 

320 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Morin v. Foster


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


45 N.Y.2d 287; 380 N.E.2d 217; 408 N.Y.S.2d 387; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 2143


June 6, 1978, Argued  
July 11, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county legislators sought review of a judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a special term order granting respondents, county manager and taxpayer, a preliminary injunction and declaring unconstitutional and null and void a local law as passed by the county legislators.


OVERVIEW: The county legislators claimed that the law enhanced rather than curtailed their power and that since the legislature had the power to fix the term of office of any county employee, the exercise of that power could not be deemed a curtailment of any related power. The county manager and taxpayer claimed that the law was an attempt to abolish or curtail a power of the legislature without providing for a permissive referendum as constitutionally and statutorily required. The court held that the law was unconstitutional and void and affirmed the appellate court decision. Any local law which abolished or curtailed any power of an elected county legislature was inoperative unless subject to a permissive referendum. The court found that the appointment of a county manager was a governmental matter and because the local law under attack attempted to curtail the power of the county charter for the appointment of a four-year term for county manager, it was subject to permissive referendum as constitutionally mandated. The law did not provide for permissive referendum on the issue, and therefore, was constitutionally deficient and void.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate division finding the local law unconstitutional was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Counties -- Powers of County Legislatures

1. Unless specifically provided by statute or charter provisions, one county legislature may not bind the hands of its successors in areas relating to governmental matters.

Counties -- Local Laws -- Appointment of County Manager

2. A provision in a county charter that the county manager is to be appointed by the county legislature for a four-year term empowers that legislature to bind its successors, a power which is unique, and a local law whereby the county legislature seeks to amend the charter so as to abolish the county manager's four-year term and provide that he will serve at its pleasure curtails that unique power, and, accordingly, must be subject to a permissive referendum as mandated by the State Constitution (art IX, § 1, subd [h], par [2]), which provides that, in counties operating under a county manager form of government, any charter amendment which abolishes or curtails "any power of an elective county officer" must provide for a permissive referendum.

Counties -- Local Laws -- Curtailment of Power of Elective County Officer

3. Under the provision of the State Constitution (art IX, § 1, subd [h], par [2]) which mandates that, in counties operating under a county manager form of government, any amendment of the county charter that abolishes or curtails "any power of an elective county officer" must provide for a permissive referendum, a permissive referendum must be provided for in any charter amendment which curtails any power of an elective county officer, and a claimed enhancement of some powers does not negate the curtailment of others.

 

321 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Payton


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


45 N.Y.2d 300; 380 N.E.2d 224; 408 N.Y.S.2d 395; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 2144


April 28, 1978, Argued  
July 11, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: In People v Payton: Order affirmed.

In People v Riddick: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed their convictions, which were affirmed by the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York). Defendants argued that their respective convictions of murder and possession of a controlled substance were improper because evidence should have been suppressed where it was seized after a warrantless entry into their homes without the existence of exigent circumstances.


OVERVIEW: One defendant challenged that a shell casing found on his stereo set, which matched those found at a service station were a murder occurred, contributed to identifying him as the killer in the jury's eyes. The other defendant argued that narcotics and hypodermic syringes, which were seized from his home, led to his ultimate conviction. Each defendant asserted that the evidence should have been suppressed as it was seized by the police without a warrant upon an entry in their homes for an arrest. The court found that the entries made by the police in the cases did not violate the defendants' constitutional protections against unreasonable searches and seizures. The court found that the entry made for the purpose of effecting a felony arrest within the homes of defendants to be arrested by a police officer who entered without permission did not violate their constitutional rights to be secure against unreasonable searches and seizures as probable cause existed even though the arresting officer had not obtained a warrant, and there were no exigent circumstances. To hold otherwise, the court concluded, would be unduly restrictive.


OUTCOME: The order in each case was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Arrest -- Entry into Home to Effect Arrest

1. An entry made for the purpose of effecting a felony arrest within the home of the person to be arrested by a police officer who has entered without permission of the owner, if based on probable cause, is not necessarily violative of the constitutional right to be secure against unreasonable searches and seizures even though the arresting officer has not obtained a warrant and there are no exigent circumstances.

Crimes -- Arrest -- Entry into Home to Effect Arrest

2. There is a substantial difference between the intrusion which attends an entry into a home for the purpose of searching the premises and that which results from an entry for the purpose of making an arrest, and a significant difference in the governmental interest in achieving the objective of the intrusion in the two instances; in the case of a search, unless appropriately limited by the terms of a warrant, the incursion on the householder's domain normally will be both more extensive and more intensive and the resulting invasion of his privacy of greater magnitude than what might be expected to occur on an entry made for the purpose of effecting his arrest (as compared with the gross intrusion which attends the arrest itself), and a community's interest in the apprehension of criminal suspects is of a higher order than is its concern for the recovery of contraband or evidence.

Crimes -- Appeal -- Matters Reviewable

3. Where the determination of the true purpose of police officers in entering a defendant's apartment turned on a question of fact, the resolution of which was dependent on the credibility ascribed to the testimony of the entering officer, and that factual issue was resolved in favor of the People by the hearing court, which was affirmed by the Appellate Division, the issue is beyond review by the Court of Appeals.

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Inevitable Discovery

4. In order for evidence derived from an unlawful seizure to be admissible under the so-called "inevitable discovery" doctrine, there must be a very high degree of probability that it would have been obtained independently of the tainted source.

Crimes -- Unlawful Search and Seizure -- Inevitable Discovery

5. Where a gun sale receipt was found during an unlawful search of defendant's apartment, and was subsequently suppressed, but normal police investigative techniques would have uncovered the gun dealer, testimony by the latter, as well as the Federally required gun sale record which he maintained, are admissible under the so-called "inevitable discovery" doctrine.

Crimes -- Right to Self-Representation

6. The exercise by a defendant of his constitutional right to represent himself at trial requires an unequivocal request to proceed pro se.

Crimes -- Arrest -- Entry into Home to Effect Arrest

7. The requirement that police officers give notice of their authority and purpose prior to their entry into premises to effect an arrest ( CPL 120.80) serves the purpose of providing the person within an opportunity to respond to the demand for admittance, thus obviating the need for forcible entry; where, in response to a police officer's knock, defendant's infant son opened the door to his apartment, and, promptly upon entering, the police declared their authority and their purpose to arrest defendant, the purpose of this requirement was accomplished, and no prejudice resulted from the officer's failure to give notice outside the open door.

 

322 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Mayor of Mt. Kisco v. Supervisor of Bedford


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


45 N.Y.2d 335; 380 N.E.2d 243; 408 N.Y.S.2d 414; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 2146


May 31, 1978, Argued  
July 11, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, supervisor and town board, sought review of a judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York) that determined that annexation by respondent village of certain lands located in the town adjacent to the village border was in the over-all public interest pursuant to N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 712(10).


OVERVIEW: The village sought to annex 224.2 acres located on its southeasterly border. The bulk of that land was owned by the intervenor, who sought to have the land annexed by the village pursuant to N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law art. 17. The village board of trustees determined that annexation was in the over-all public interest; the town board found that it was not. The village commenced a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 712 seeking to overturn the decision of the town board. The appellate division designated three referees as required by N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 712(6). Although acknowledging that annexation was in the public interest, the referees reported against annexation solely because of the lack of sewage disposal. After a second hearing, the appellate division adjudged that the annexation was in the over-all public interest. The court of appeals found that the appellate division concluded that in light of the issuance of the permit by the state for the sewer system, whatever uncertainty existed was sufficiently obviated as to warrant a finding that annexation was in the over-all interest of the public. Because the decision was not completely irrational, it was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that the annexation was in the public interest.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Annexation of Territory

1. The annexation by a village of 224.2 acres of land in a town adjacent to the village border upon 40 acres of which land a condominium complex was planned with the rest of the land to be conveyed to a conservation organization in perpetuity as open space was properly found to be in the over-all public interest. The needs of the planned complex are much more compatible with the substantially greater public services available in the village than with those more limited services provided by the town and, in the light of the issuance of a permit to the complex developer by the State of a pollutant discharge elimination system, whatever uncertainty previously might have existed with respect to the adequacy of the sewage disposal plan was sufficiently obviated.

Municipal Corporations -- Annexation of Territory

2. The power of the Court of Appeals to review a determination of the Appellate Division made in a proceeding pursuant to article 17 of the General Municipal Law with respect to the annexation of the territory of one municipality by another municipality is quite circumscribed. The statutory scheme places the responsibility for the determination solely upon the Appellate Division and any issues resolved by the Appellate Division remain invulnerable if there is any rational basis for its findings and conclusions.
