State v. Rutkowski


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


44 N.Y.2d 989; 380 N.E.2d 166; 408 N.Y.S.2d 335; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 2168


June 6, 1978, Argued  
July 6, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order modified, with costs, in accordance with the memorandum herein and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a state and its commissioner of health, sought review of the order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York) that modified, and as modified affirmed a trial court's order and judgment, which declared N.Y. Pub. Health Law §§ 1115, 1116, and N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 17-1505 unconstitutional. The modification resulted in a complete denial of plaintiffs' motions for summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs filed an action against defendant developers seeking to enjoin the sale of any lot, or the erection of a permanent building upon any lot, from a certain parcel of land, until a subdivision plan showing methods for obtaining and furnishing an adequate water supply and sewage disposal facilities was filed with and approved by the department of health. The trial court found in favor of the developers and declared that N.Y. Pub. Health Law §§ 1115, 1116, and N.Y. Envtl. Conserv. Law § 17-1505 was unconstitutional. The appellate court modified the trial court's judgment, reversing that part which granted plaintiffs summary judgment on their fourth cause of action. On further review, the court modified the trial court's order. The court held that the lower courts, in addressing the vagueness issue, improperly relied on hypothetical cases and that the developers failed to demonstrate that the statutes were unconstitutionally vague as applied. Nonetheless, the court held that the state was not entitled to summary judgment on their fourth cause of action because it could not have been decided as a matter of law that the lots were sold for residential purposes.


OUTCOME: The court modified the appellate court's order that modified the trial court's judgment. In so doing, the court held that the statutes at issue were not unconstitutionally vague, but concluded nonetheless that plaintiffs were still not entitled to summary judgment. As modified the court affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Health -- Filing Requirement for Residential Subdivisions

In an action by the State and the State Commissioner of Health to enjoin the sale of any lot, or the erection of a permanent building upon any lot, from a certain parcel of land, until a subdivision plan showing methods for obtaining and furnishing an adequate water supply and sewage disposal facilities was filed with and approved by the Department of Health, as required by section 1116 of the Public Health Law and section 17-1505 of the Environmental Conservation Law for residential subdivisions, an order of the Appellate Division which affirmed a declaration that those statutes were unconstitutional is modified; defendants have not met their burden of demonstrating, as alleged by them, that the statutes are unconstitutionally vague as applied to them; however, the State is not entitled to summary judgment since defendants deny that the lots were sold for residential purposes and it cannot be decided as a matter of law that they were sold for residential purposes.

Pomeroy v. Whalen


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


44 N.Y.2d 992; 380 N.E.2d 168; 408 N.Y.S.2d 336; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 2169


June 6, 1978, Argued  
July 6, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, with costs, and the matter remitted to the Appellate Division, Third Department, for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant commissioner sought review of a judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York) that modified and confirmed a determination of the commissioner. The modifications annulled the commissioner's findings of violations for respondent landowner's sale of six lots and for failure to submit final plans for water supply plans and annulled the fines for those seven violations.


OVERVIEW: The landowner purchased a tract of land in 1956 under a single deed. Two years later, having decided to develop a portion of the tract, the landowner followed the requirements set forth in N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 1116 by applying to the Department of Health for approval of sanitary facilities and water systems contemplated for the first 16-lot section of a residential subdivision. A certificate of approval was issued. In 1972, the Department discovered that the landowner had sold additional residential lots from the same tract outside the boundaries of the approved 16-acre subdivision. No new application had been submitted and no approval granted for the additional six lots in the subdivision. A hearing officer found that the landowner had six Public Health Law violations and recommended penalties. The commissioner adopted the hearing officer's findings. The lower appellate court modified. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded. Because the landowner knew that she was subject to § 1116 and that it required approval for further segmentation of the tract, she could not be heard to complain of sanctions for her conduct.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower appellate court and remanded for the lower appellate court to determine whether there was substantial evidence to sustain the commissioner's determination that the landowner violated statutory law by the sale of six residential lots from an unapproved portion of her realty subdivision.


HEADNOTES

Health -- Filing Requirement for Residential Subdivisions

In a proceeding to annul a determination of the State Commissioner of Health that petitioner had violated sections of the Public Health Law and State Sanitary Code by selling six lots from an unapproved realty subdivision, by extending an unapproved water supply without plan approval and by failing to submit final plans for water supply purposes, the judgment of the Appellate Division, which modified by annulling the determination insofar as it found violations for selling six lots and failing to submit final plans for water supply purposes, is reversed and the matter remitted to the Appellate Division to determine whether there is substantial evidence to support the determination. Section 1115 of the Public Health Law as applied to petitioner was not unconstitutional for vagueness.

 

325 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Smith


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


44 N.Y.2d 613; 378 N.E.2d 1032; 407 N.Y.S.2d 462; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 2053


April 25, 1978, Argued  
June 15, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Appellate Term of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which, after making a finding that N.Y. Penal Law § 240.37 was constitutional, reversed the trial court's dismissal of the State's complaint and reinstated the loitering for the purpose of engaging in a prostitution charges against defendant.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with violating N.Y. Penal Law § 240.37, which prohibited loitering for the purposes of prostitution. The lower court dismissed the complaint and declared the statute unconstitutional. The appellate division reversed and reinstated the case. On appeal, defendant contended that the statute was unconstitutional because it granted unfettered discretion to the police in making arrests based solely on circumstantial evidence. The court held that the statute was not unconstitutional for vagueness since it detailed the prohibited conduct and limited itself to one crime. Since the statute, by its terms, was limited to such conduct that had never been a form of constitutionally protected free speech; the statute did not have a chilling effect on First Amendment freedoms. Accordingly, the charges against defendant were reinstated.


OUTCOME: Finding that the challenged statute detailed the prohibited conduct and limited itself to one crime, the court affirmed the order upholding the statute's constitutionality and reinstating the prostitution charges against defendant.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Prostitution -- Loitering for Purpose of Engaging in Prostitution

1. Section 240.37 of the Penal Law, which prohibits loitering for the purpose of engaging in a prostitution offense, is not unconstitutional for vagueness since it details the prohibited conduct and limits itself to one crime and, as a consequence, the police are precluded from speculating or groping for violations; the section does not authorize an arrest or conviction based on simple loitering of a known prostitute or anyone else, but, rather, requires loitering plus additional objective conduct evincing that the observed activities are for the purpose of prostitution.

Crimes -- Appeal -- Absence of Defendant

2. While ordinarily the Court of Appeals will not entertain the appeal of an absconding defendant, in a criminal action involving the constitutionality of section 240.37 of the Penal Law the appeal will be heard where all sides seek a determination; there are two pending civil actions in the United States District Court involving such constitutionality; defendant's attack is upon the accusatory instrument, she has not been tried and, in these circumstances, an authorized attorney may appear for the defendant in absentia; and the statute is still being enforced. The issue is imbued with substantial public interest, is surrounded with some uncertainty and is likely to surface again.

Constitutional Law -- Criminal Statutes

3. A criminal statute must be sufficiently definite to give a person of ordinary intelligence fair notice that his contemplated conduct is forbidden by the statute and it must provide explicit standards for those who apply them to avoid resolution on an ad hoc and subjective basis, with attendant dangers of arbitrary and discriminatory application.

Crimes -- Prostitution -- Loitering for Purpose of Engaging in Prostitution

4. Regardless of the possibilities of arrests under section 240.37 of the Penal Law, prohibiting loitering for the purpose of engaging in a prostitution offense, based on mere suspicion rather than probable cause, a conviction is not contemplated since the standard of proof is proof beyond a reasonable doubt of every element of the offense and where circumstantial evidence is relied on exclusively, the facts must be inconsistent with innocence and exclude to a moral certainty every other reasonable hypothesis. Furthermore, based on particulars obvious to and discernible by a trained police officer, it would be a simple task to differentiate between casual street encounters and a series of acts of solicitation for prostitution.

Crimes -- Prostitution -- Loitering for Purpose of Engaging in Prostitution

5. Section 240.37 of the Penal Law, which prohibits loitering for the purpose of engaging in a prostitution offense, does not violate the exercise of First Amendment freedoms for it is limited to behavior which has never been a form of constitutionally protected free speech.

 

326 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Argibay


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


45 N.Y.2d 45; 379 N.E.2d 191; 407 N.Y.S.2d 664; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 2088


March 29, 1978, Argued  
June 15, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Orders affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed orders from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department, New York, which affirmed judgments convicting defendants of the criminal sale of a controlled substance in the second degree in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 220.41.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted for the criminal sale of a controlled substance in the second degree in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 220.41. The appellate division affirmed. Defendants appealed again, arguing that the jury was not charged on agency for one defendant, which instruction was erroneously given for second defendant, and the trial court erred by not declaring a mistrial after a juror told counsel that she hated him. The court found that first defendant was not entitled to a jury instruction on agency since he was acting at the very least as a middleman or broker of narcotics for his supplier. Though the agency charge was erroneous, second defendant failed to object and preserve the error at trial. Finally, defense counsel's refusal of the trial court's offer to conduct a voir dire examination of a potentially prejudicial juror worked a waiver of any error.


OUTCOME: The orders of the intermediate court affirming judgments that convicted defendants of criminal sale of controlled substance were affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Controlled Substances -- Agency

1. In a case involving criminal sale of narcotics, no charge on agency is required, or appropriate, when the testimony essential to a verdict in favor of the People leads to the inevitable conclusion that the defendant was not merely accommodating the buyer, but was acting, if not as a principal seller, then at the very least as a middleman or a broker for his supplier.

Appeal -- Preservation of Issue for Appellate Review

2. Where, in a case involving criminal sale of narcotics, no objection was taken to a charge on agency, error in that charge was not preserved for appellate review.

Crimes -- Misconduct of Juror

3. Where, during trial, a defense counsel informed the trial court that a juror had told counsel she hated him, failure to take action on that report would have been error, but defendants were not entitled, automatically, to a mistrial; the trial court acted appropriately in offering to conduct a voir dire examination of the juror, and a refusal of that offer by defense counsel worked a waiver of any error.

Crimes -- Controlled Substances -- Agency

4. In a case involving criminal sale of narcotics, the mere fact that the defendant was a middleman or a broker, rather than the supplier of the narcotics, is not enough to warrant a charge on agency; a middleman who acts as a broker between a seller and buyer, aiming to satisfy both, but largely for his own benefit, cannot properly be termed an agent of either, but is a trader in narcotics, a narcotics merchant.

Crimes -- Controlled Substances -- Agency

5. To be an agent of his buyer, a narcotics merchant must be a mere extension of the buyer, acting to procure what the buyer wants because the buyer has asked him to do so, but not out of any independent desire or inclination to promote the transaction.

Crimes -- Controlled Substances -- Agency

6. A defendant charged with selling narcotics to undercover police officers was not entitled to a charge on agency, or on facilitation, where there was testimony, uncontroverted by proof or cross-examination, that the officers had never met him until the narcotics were actually delivered and paid for; that, in fact, he had used others as intermediaries to shield him from all contact with them until the transaction was to be completed; that, when they finally did meet, he would not allow them to see the supplier of the narcotics, and a dispute over the quantity of narcotics arose between them, and that he subsequently admitted to one of the officers that he was leaving the narcotics trade because it had ceased to be financially profitable; this testimony is not reconcilable with the theory that the defendant was acting merely as an agent of the officers.

Crimes -- Controlled Substances -- Agency

7. An agency charge is not required simply because it appears that a seller of narcotics did not personally grow, import, or manufacture the illicit substance; it should be reserved for cases where there is at least some evidence, however slight, to support the inference that the supposed agent was acting, in effect, as an extension of the buyer.

Peninsula General Nursing Home v. Sugarman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


44 N.Y.2d 909; 379 N.E.2d 223; 407 N.Y.S.2d 697; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 2097

DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


HEADNOTES

Health -- Medicaid Reimbursement -- Right of Provider of Services to Fair Hearing

Where petitioner nursing home rendered services to a named individual and sought to be reimbursed under the Medicaid program and where coverage was denied on grounds the individual was ineligible since he had transferred property to his children prior to applying for benefits, an order of the Appellate Division which affirmed a judgment declaring section 366 of the Social Services Law and regulations thereunder unconstitutional to the extent they denied petitioner a fair hearing, enjoining enforcement of said section and directing respondents to afford petitioner a fair hearing, is reversed and the petition dismissed on the dissenting opinion at the Appellate Division, which concluded that petitioner, as a provider of services, did not come within the purview of the statute or the regulations and that the administrative agency properly declined to afford petitioner a hearing in view of the [***4] remedy available to it in the form of a plenary suit.

People v. Vaccaro


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


44 N.Y.2d 885; 379 N.E.2d 159; 407 N.Y.S.2d 631; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 2076


April 24, 1978, Submitted  
June 6, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed in a memorandum.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Criminal Possession of Weapon -- Felony or Misdemeanor

Where defendant shot and wounded a police officer and was charged, inter alia, with criminal possession of a weapon in the second degree, a felony, and criminal possession of a weapon in the fourth degree, a misdemeanor, and where the trial court dismissed the misdemeanor charge and only submitted the felony charge to the jury, the Appellate Division properly affirmed a judgment convicting defendant of assault in the second degree and criminal possession of a weapon in the second degree, because, although the elements of criminal possession of a weapon in the second and fourth degrees are identical in circumstances where the culpable act is possession of a loaded firearm, the overlapping of elements does not render the statutes unconstitutional. Since by statutory definition criminal possession of a weapon in the fourth degree is a lesser included offense of criminal possession of a weapon in the second degree, the court correctly dismissed the lesser charge because there was no "reasonable view of the evidence which would support a finding that the defendant committed such lesser offense but did not commit the greater." ( CPL 300.50, subd 1.)

 

329 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Mundy v. Nassau County Civil Service Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


44 N.Y.2d 352; 376 N.E.2d 1305; 405 N.Y.S.2d 660; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 1946


March 29, 1978, Argued  
May 11, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, government agencies, appealed an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which modified an order by denying the government agencies' motions to dismiss the complaint filed by plaintiff provisional employees. The provisional employees commenced an action for a declaratory judgment that the civil service tests used by the government agencies were illegal and invalid.


OVERVIEW: The provisional employees took a civil service test for permanent employment with the government agencies and were subsequently notified that they had failed or received low grades. A few days later, because of an unrelated suit, certification of the list of test takers was withdrawn and held in abeyance until the unrelated suit was resolved. The list was later recertified, and the provisional employees were then either demoted or discharged because of their test grade and position on the list. The government agencies alleged that the claim was time-barred. The court determined that the limitations period did not begin to run until the list was recertified. The court reasoned that the government agencies created the ambiguity and uncertainty by declaring that certification was withdrawn and held in abeyance. The court stated that the provisional employees should not have to risk dismissal for prematurity or untimeliness by guessing when a final and binding determination had or had not been made. The court concluded that in order to not deny a party his day in court, the statute of limitations did not begin until the ambiguity was resolved.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower court, with costs.


HEADNOTES

Actions -- Form of Action

1. A complaint which seeks a declaration that civil service examinations are illegal and invalid because they are not sufficiently job-related should be considered a petition commencing an article 78 proceeding.

Limitation of Actions -- Proceeding Against Body or Officer -- Civil Service Examinations

2. The establishment and certification of an eligible list based on grades from a contested examination is usually the type of final and binding determination from which the four-month period of limitations applicable to an article 78 proceeding ( CPLR 217) is measured.

Limitation of Actions -- Proceeding Against Body or Officer -- Civil Service Examinations

3. Public employees aggrieved by the questionable propriety of a civil service examination should not be allowed to press stale claims or disrupt the appointments of those otherwise secure in their tenure merely by framing a lawsuit as a declaratory judgment action or casting their grievance as a continuing wrong, and only a short Statute of Limitations can effectively contain this type of challenge within fair as well as feasible bounds.

Limitation of Actions -- Proceeding Against Body or Officer

4. Any ambiguity created by an administrative body as to whether it has made a final and binding determination, from which the four-month period of limitations applicable to an article 78 proceeding is measured ( CPLR 217), should be resolved against it in determining whether such a proceeding has been timely commenced.

Limitation of Actions -- Proceeding Against Body or Officer -- Civil Service Examinations

5. Where, within a few days of the initial certification of an eligible list based on grades from civil service examinations, the civil service commission withdrew certification of the list and notified all eligibles that certification would be withdrawn and held in abeyance pending a final determination of a suit challenging a transfer of control of the department for which the examination was held, employees challenging the legality of the examinations did not become aggrieved, and the four-month period of limitations applicable to an article 78 proceeding did not begin to run against them, until the recertification of the list following final resolution of the other suit.

 

330 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Ryder Truck Lines, Inc. v. Maiorano


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


44 N.Y.2d 364; 376 N.E.2d 1311; 405 N.Y.S.2d 666; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 1947


March 31, 1978, Argued  
May 11, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employer sought review of a judgment from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the lower court's denial of its motion to stay the arbitration proceedings commenced by respondent employee to resolve an issue regarding damages that resulted from a work-related injury.


OVERVIEW: The employee, while operating a motor vehicle in the course of his employment, was injured in a one-vehicle accident. The employer paid the employee workmen's compensation benefits. The employee also sought payment from his employer, in its capacity as owner of the motor vehicle, of first-party benefits under N.Y. Ins. Law art. 18, the "no fault" law. The employee commenced arbitration proceedings. The employer filed a motion to stay the arbitration, which was denied, and the denial was affirmed on appeal. The court also affirmed. The court held that there was nothing in the state constitution or statute mandating exclusion of an employee's claims against employers for first-party benefits under N.Y. Ins. Law art. 18. Therefore, the court found that the parties' controversy fell within the applicable arbitration provision of their contract.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the finding of the appellate court which upheld the lower court's decision to deny the employer's attempt to stay the arbitration proceedings brought by the employee regarding issues surrounding a work-related injury.


HEADNOTES

Arbitration -- No-Fault Automobile Insurance -- Employee Claims Against Employers

1. There is neither constitutional nor statutory bar to submission to arbitration by an employee of a claim against his employer for first-party benefits under the no-fault insurance law (Insurance Law, art 18) with respect to injuries suffered by him in the course of his employment while operating a motor vehicle owned by his employer.

Arbitration -- Matters Arbitrable

2. If parties be deemed to have agreed to refer a dispute to arbitration, then, unless barred by considerations of public policy, disposition of their differences on the merits must be left to the arbitrator.

Insurance -- No-Fault Automobile Insurance -- Scope of Arbitration

3. Subdivision 2 of section 675 of the Insurance Law, which provides that "Every insurer shall provide a claimant with the option of submitting any dispute involving the insurer's liability to pay first party benefits * * * to binding arbitration" is a broad arbitration clause, embracing all disputes with respect to entitlement to first-party benefits arising under the no-fault insurance law (Insurance Law, art 18).

Insurance -- No-Fault Automobile Insurance -- Scope of Arbitration

4. Where an employee has sought arbitration of a claim against his employer for first-party benefits under the no-fault insurance law (Insurance Law, art 18), and the employer argues that there is nothing to submit to arbitration inasmuch as the employee has no claim whatsoever against his employer under the no-fault statute, the controversy is not as to the merits of the employee's particular asserted entitlement to first-party benefits, which would be a matter for the arbitrator, but as to the scope of the agreement to arbitrate, a matter for the court.

Constitutional Law -- Workmen's Compensation -- No-Fault First-Party Benefits

5. Section 18 of article I of the New York State Constitution, which provides that "Nothing contained in this constitution shall be construed to limit the power of the legislature to enact laws * * * for the payment * * * of compensation for injuries to employees * * * without regard to fault as a cause thereof * * * or to provide that the right of such compensation, and the remedy therefor shall be exclusive of all other rights and remedies for injuries to employees", does not itself enact a workmen's compensation program, but merely removes all constitutional constraints on the enactment of such a program by the Legislature, and, accordingly, cannot be construed as prohibiting the adoption of a law which would permit an employee, in addition to his workmen's compensation remedy, to make a claim against his employer for first-party benefits under the no-fault insurance law (Insurance Law, art 18) with respect to injuries suffered by him in the course of his employment while operating a motor vehicle owned by his employer.

Insurance -- No-Fault Automobile Insurance -- Workmen's Compensation

6. Nothing in the no-fault insurance law (Insurance Law, art 18), or in the legislative circumstances surrounding its adoption, suggests that the Legislature intended that the benefits of the no-fault program should not be available to an employee who is injured in the course of his employment while operating a motor vehicle owned by his employer; whether or not a third-party tort-feasor is involved, benefits under the Workmen's Compensation Law, which purports to limit the employer's liability to such benefits (§ 11), and no-fault first-party benefits for the employee's basic economic loss, which is expressly defined to exclude workmen's compensation benefits (Insurance Law, § 671, subd 2, par [b]), are independently available to him.

Constitutional Law -- Workmen's Compensation -- No-Fault First-Party Benefits

7. To accord to employees injured in the course of their employment in motor vehicle accidents the right to make claims against their employers for first-party benefits under the no-fault insurance law (Insurance Law, art 18), in addition to claiming workmen's compensation benefits, while employees injured in the course of their employment from other causes may claim only workmen's compensation benefits, does not constitute a denial of equal protection of the laws.

Insurance -- No-Fault Automobile Insurance -- Employer's Liability to Employee

8. The no-fault law (Insurance Law, art 18) places responsibility on an employer for payment of first-party benefits to an employee injured in the course of his employment while operating a motor vehicle owned by his employer, and it is of no legal significance whether the employer is self-insured or carries insurance coverage.

 

331 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Mena v. D'Ambrose


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


44 N.Y.2d 428; 377 N.E.2d 466; 406 N.Y.S.2d 22; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 1962


March 30, 1978, Argued  
May 11, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: On respondents' appeal: Order modified, without costs, in accordance with the memorandum herein and, as so modified, affirmed.

On petitioners' cross appeal: Cross appeal dismissed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Civil Service Commission of the City of New York (commission) and petitioner candidates sought review of an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which granted the candidate's motion for reargument or renewal of their petition which was previously dismissed and granted the petition to the extent of assuring the candidates certification to the next occurring vacancies.


OVERVIEW: The candidates were placed on an eligible list for a promotion. One officer had his test rescored and the list was adjusted with regard to his position on the list. Another officer received the promotion. If the all of the tests had been rescored and the entire list adjusted, one of the candidates would have received the promotion rather than the other officer. The test was then rescored for everyone and the list readjusted. However, the commission claimed that the eligibility of the candidates ran from the date of their initial placement. The candidates sought immediate appointment. On appeal, the court determined that the candidates' scores were improperly computed resulting in an inaccurate eligible list as to them and that they were aggrieved thereby. The court found that the candidates were entitled to the continuation of a special eligible list for a period consistent with N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 56 commencing at the time when the list was corrected. The court modified the order with regard to the length of time the candidates would remain on the list. The court dismissed the candidate's cross appeal because they were not aggrieved by the appellate court's modification.


OUTCOME: The court modified the order of the appellate court by changing the length of time the candidates would remain on the eligibility list, affirmed the order as modified, and dismissed the candidate's cross appeal.


HEADNOTES

Civil Service -- Eligible Lists -- Effect of Expiration During Litigation

1. Where litigation concerning errors in an eligible list was commenced during the active life of the list, the fact that the list expired during the course of the litigation does not preclude relief.

Civil Service -- Eligible Lists -- Duration

2. Section 56 of the Civil Service Law, which provides that an eligible list shall last no longer than four years, was not intended to exonerate known and continued wrongs of the testing agency by the mere passage of time, and, where it is demonstrated that errors have rendered a list in derogation of the merit and fitness standards (NY Const, art V, § 6), and candidates have demonstrated that they were aggrieved by those errors, the statutory durational period does not begin to run until the list is corrected.

Civil Service -- Eligible Lists -- Duration

3. Candidates whose scores were improperly computed, resulting in an inaccurate eligible list as to them, and who were aggrieved thereby, in that, but for the error, one of them would have received the last appointment made from the list, are entitled to the continuation of a special eligible list, consisting of their names, for a period consistent with the statutory durational period of four years (Civil Service Law, § 56) commencing at the time when the list was corrected, which special list will be subject to the applicable Civil Service Law provisions, such as section 61, providing for the selection of one-out-of-three certified persons, and shall continue until such time as one of them is appointed or until it expires by operation of law, whichever occurs first.

 

332 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People by Whalen v. Woman's Christian Asso.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


44 N.Y.2d 466; 377 N.E.2d 725; 406 N.Y.S.2d 272; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 1993


March 21, 1978, Argued  
May 9, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state challenged a decision from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a decision that that declared that respondent hospital was not limited to rates established by the hospital service organization for the provision of services.


OVERVIEW: After the hospital ended its affiliation with the hospital service organization it set its own fee schedule. The state initiated an action against the hospital for a declaration that the hospital could not charge the organization's member patients more than the fees set by the organization. The hospital sought a declaration that it was not subject to the established rates. The trial court entered a judgment that the hospital was not bound by the organization price schedules and the intermediate appellate court affirmed the declaration. The state asserted that as to hospital services rendered to the organization's subscribers, N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 2807 sought to accomplish cost limitations not merely by instituting controls on the amounts which an organization-affiliated hospital could receive, but by imposing a widespread limitation on the amounts hospitals, whether or not they were member hospitals, could charge patients who subscribed to the organization. The court affirmed, determining that the N.Y. Pub. Health Law did not impose price controls but only created indirect price control by encouraging use of the organization's price controlled reimbursement system.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the intermediate appellate court that declared that the hospital was not limited to rates established by the hospital service organization for the provision of services.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Regulation of Hospital Costs

1. The legislative purpose of article 28 of the Public Health Law, as amended by the Hospital Cost Control Law (L 1969, ch 957), was to control spiraling hospital costs, and the regulation of those costs contemplated by the provisions thereof advanced the legitimate legislative purposes of promoting public health, welfare and safety.

Health -- Hospital Reimbursement Rates -- Direct Patient Charges

2. Article 28 of the Public Health Law, as amended by the Hospital Cost Control Law (L 1969, ch 957), which was enacted to control spiraling hospital costs, does not impose direct controls on hospital prices per se, but, instead, provides for an indirect method of control, which regulates only reimbursement payments by Blue Cross, government agencies and health maintenance organizations (§ 2807), and puts no ceiling on the charges which hospitals may make to individual patients.

Health -- Hospital Reimbursement Rates -- Direct Patient Charges

3. Under the indirect method of controlling hospital costs provided for in article 28 of the Public Health Law, as amended by the Hospital Cost Control Law (L 1969, ch 957), which regulates only reimbursement payments by Blue Cross, government agencies and health maintenance organizations, and puts no ceiling on the charges that hospitals may make to individual patients, a hospital which is not affiliated with Blue Cross is free to apply its own schedule of fees to patients who are Blue Cross subscribers.

 

333 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Little v. Ciuros


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


44 N.Y.2d 825; 377 N.E.2d 980; 406 N.Y.S.2d 449; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 2016


May 3, 1978, Argued  
May 9, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, without costs. The stay previously granted by this court is continued until May 19, 1978, the date on which the remittitur of this court shall issue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Relator appealed an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), that affirmed the dismissal of a writ of habeas corpus seeking to prevent relator's extradition to another state.


OVERVIEW: Relator commenced a habeas corpus proceeding to resist her extradition to North Carolina. Relator argued that if forced to return to North Carolina she would have been subjected to cruel and inhuman treatment by correctional officials of that state. The court ruled that interstate extradition was mandated by U.S. Const. art. IV, § 2, cl. 2, which provided that a person charged in any state with treason, felony or other crime, who fled from justice and was found in another state, shall on demand of the executive authority of the state from which he fled, be delivered up, to be removed to the state having jurisdiction of the crime. Further, the court held that no contention was made that any of the applicable provisions had not been complied with. Also, the court found that it was not within the competency of the court to determine whether the prison officials of a sister state would, prospectively, deprive relator of her constitutional rights, and that one alleging present or future improper activity in another state was under the necessity of seeking redress in accordance with law and in the forum in which the alleged improprieties would occur.


OUTCOME: The order of the appellate division affirming dismissal of the writ of habeas corpus was affirmed where the requisite jurisdictional requirements were adequately demonstrated, and relator did not provide compelling cause for the court to give her sanctuary from the state demanding her extradition.


HEADNOTES

Extradition -- Where Claims Resisting Extradition are to be Made

Claims by a relator resisting extradition to the demanding State that she will be subjected to unconstitutional treatment there may not, in all but the most unusual circumstances, be invoked in the courts of the asylum State. Under the scheme of extradition, as set forth in both the Federal Constitution and the statutes implementing it, she must seek redress in accordance with law and in the forum in which the alleged improprieties will occur.

Bethlehem Steel Corp. v. Board of Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


44 N.Y.2d 831; 378 N.E.2d 115; 406 N.Y.S.2d 752; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 2027


May 2, 1978, Argued  
May 9, 1978, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Remittiturs Signed June 8, 1978.



DISPOSITION: In Bethlehem Steel Corp. v Board of Educ. of City School Dist. of Lackawanna: Order affirmed, with costs, proposed remittitur to be settled on 20 days' notice.

In Jones v City School Dist. of City of Geneva: Order modified, with costs to plaintiffs, in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed. Proposed remittitur to be settled on 20 days' notice.

In Waldert v City of Rochester: Order modified, with costs to plaintiff, in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed. Proposed remittitur to be settled on 20 days' notice.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant school boards appealed from various orders of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York) declaring 1976 N.Y. Laws ch. 349, 484 unconstitutional, and denied the repayment of portions of taxes paid by plaintiff taxpayers under a tax levy.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to 1976 N.Y. Laws ch. 349, 484, the school boards levied taxes on the taxpayers, excluding amounts appropriated for retirement, Social Security costs, health and dental insurance from the computation of the total amount of revenue that might be raised by real property tax levy under the limitations of N.Y. Const. art. VIII, § 10. Three taxpayers successfully challenged the imposition of the taxes, with the intermediate appellate court declaring 1976 N.Y. Laws ch. 349, 484, unconstitutional. Agreeing with this holding, the court affirmed, but it modified the orders, directing repayment of portions of the taxes paid under protest. The laws presented nothing more than an attempt to circumvent the constitutional limitation on the amount of revenue that could be raised by local subdivisions through taxing real property. Further, the school boards' financial crisis was not an emergency justifying the suspension of constitutional limitations pursuant to the emergency clause in the N.Y. Const. N.Y. Const. art. II, § 25.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgments by permitting two taxpayers to repayment of the taxes they paid in excess of the constitutional tax limitation, and by permitting the third taxpayer to establish its rights to repayment of excess taxes paid if those taxes were paid under protest. As modified, the court affirmed, with costs.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Constitutional Limitation on Real Property Taxes

Chapters 349 and 484 of the Laws of 1976 are declared unconstitutional in their entirety, for the reasons expressed in the opinion at the Appellate Division, which held that the subject legislation unconstitutionally permitted certain municipalities and school districts to exclude amounts appropriated for retirement and Social Security costs from the computation of the total amount of revenue that might be raised by real property tax levy under the limitations established by section 10 of article VIII of the New York Constitution (L 1976, ch 349, § 2 [Emergency City and School District Relief Act]), unconstitutionally provided, as an alternative, for a tax for such purposes (L 1976, ch 349, § 3 [State Real Property Tax Act]), and unconstitutionally permitted certain school districts to exclude amounts appropriated for health and dental insurance from the constitutional tax limitations (L 1976, ch 484). Contrary to a conclusion of the Appellate Division, certain plaintiffs in actions challenging the legislation are entitled to repayment of the taxes paid in excess of the constitutional tax limitation, and another is entitled to establish its rights to such repayment if the taxes were paid under appropriate protest. Per Curiam opinion.

 

335 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Isaacson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


44 N.Y.2d 511; 378 N.E.2d 78; 406 N.Y.S.2d 714; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 2019


February 16, 1978, Argued  
May 9, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York) affirmed the conviction of defendant by the trial court of the criminal sale of a controlled substance in the first degree. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: An acquaintance of defendant was arrested for possession of a controlled substance, beaten, and offered leniency if he would become a police informant. Testing of the substance revealed that it was not an illegal controlled substance, but police did not inform the accused of that fact. The informant contacted defendant and attempted to induce defendant to sell him two ounces of cocaine. Defendant had made some minor sales of controlled substances, but had no contacts for the quantity requested by the informant. After seven calls, defendant agreed to make the sale, but insisted that the transaction take place in Pennsylvania. The informant established a meeting location that he alleged was in Pennsylvania, but in reality was in New York. Defendant was indicted and sentenced to 15 years at Attica. The court held that the conduct of the police required dismissal of the indictment on the basis of due process. The court found the police manufactured and created the crime. Even though defendant failed to prove an affirmative defense of entrapment, the police conduct, when tested by due process standards, was egregious and deprivative as to bar prosecution.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the conviction of defendant for the illegal sale of cocaine and dismissed the indictment. The court held that defendant's constitutional rights to due process were violated.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Due Process

1. Under the State due process clause (NY Const, art I, § 6), a court may impose higher standards than those held to be necessary by the United States Supreme Court under the corresponding Federal constitutional provision.

Crimes -- Dismissal on Due Process Grounds

2. Where the police obtain evidence by brutalizing a defendant, a conviction resulting from such methods offends due process.

Crimes -- Entrapment -- Dismissal on Due Process Grounds

3. Where a defense of entrapment is not made out because of the predisposition of the defendant to commit the crime, police misconduct may warrant dismissal on due process grounds.

Crimes -- Dismissal on Due Process Grounds

4. The type of conduct which mandates the barring of prosecution on due process grounds should not be limited to situations involving police brutality.

Crimes -- Dismissal on Due Process Grounds

5. Factors to be considered in determining whether due process principles have been transgressed include whether the police manufactured a crime which otherwise would not likely have occurred, whether the police themselves engaged in criminal or improper conduct repugnant to a sense of justice, whether the defendant's reluctance to commit the crime is overcome by appeals to humanitarian instincts, by temptation of exorbitant gain, or by persistent solicitation in the face of unwillingness, and, whether the record reveals simply an overriding police desire to obtain a conviction of any individual.

Crimes -- Dismissal on Due Process Grounds

6. Where, as a result of police brutalization and trickery resulting in the misconception that he was facing a stiff prison sentence, a third party became an informant and at the behest of the police proceeded to contact various individuals, including defendant, a Pennsylvania resident with no prior record who, although initially unwilling, following persistent solicitation finally agreed to sell the informant drugs in a quantity set by the police, and was subsequently and deceitfully lured into New York where the sale was made in accordance with specifications detailed by the police, even though the defense of entrapment was not made out because of defendant's predisposition to commit the crime, the police conduct warrants dismissal on due process grounds.

 

336 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Hynes v. Moskowitz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


44 N.Y.2d 383; 377 N.E.2d 446; 406 N.Y.S.2d 1; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 1959


March 29, 1978, Argued  
May 4, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated proceedings, appellant nursing homes and their principals challenged orders of the Supreme Courts of Rockland County and Queens County (New York), which issued a subpoena duces tecum and which denied motions to quash grand jury subpoenas duces tecum, on the ground that N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 610.25 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellants challenged orders that directed the production of certain books and records pursuant to a subpoena duces tecum and that denied motions to quash grand jury subpoenas duces tecum on the ground that N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 610.25 was unconstitutional. The court affirmed the orders, holding that the statute was not unconstitutionally vague because the legislature granted the attorney general the power to issue subpoenas and to retain custody of subpoenaed materials. The court held that the statute clearly provided that the retention of the subpoenaed documents must be supported by good cause and must be limited to a reasonable period of time. In addition, the statute provided that the needs of the subpoenaed party be considered to assure that the possession of the subpoenaed records by the attorney general would not prove unduly onerous to the subpoenaed party.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial courts' orders denying motions to quash subpoenas since the statute was not unconstitutionally vague because the legislature granted power to issue subpoenas and to retain custody of subpoenaed materials to the attorney general within reasonable bounds.


HEADNOTES

Witnesses -- Subpoena Duces Tecum -- Constitutionality of Retention of Documents

1. Statutes providing for limited retention of subpoenaed documents upon "good cause shown by the issuer" are not unconstitutionally vague, requiring no court order in advance of the issuance of a subpoena but, rather, the showing of "good cause" only upon challenge to the possession of the subpoenaed documents. ( CPL 610.25; Executive Law, § 63, subd 8; CPLR 2305, subd [c].)

Witnesses -- Subpoena Duces Tecum

2. A person subject to a nonjudicial subpoena duces tecum may challenge it in court if the subpoena requires the production of irrelevant or immaterial documents or is used as an instrument of harassment; likewise, where the subpoenaed person fails to comply with the subpoena, the issuer may move to compel compliance upon a showing that the items sought have some relevancy and materiality to the matter under investigation.

Witnesses -- Subpoena Duces Tecum

3. While a showing of probable cause or probative evidence to suspect illicit activity need not be shown to support a subpoena, evidence so scant that it does not legitimately raise an issue of impropriety is not enough.

Witnesses -- Subpoena Duces Tecum -- Constitutionality of Retention of Documents

4. Statutes providing for limited retention of subpoenaed documents upon "good cause shown by the issuer" are not violative of the Fourth Amendment proscription against unreasonable searches and seizures. ( CPL 610.25; Executive Law, § 63, subd 8; CPLR 2305, subd [c].)

Witnesses -- Subpoena Duces Tecum

5. CPL 610.25, providing for the limited possession and retention by the Grand Jury of subpoenaed records, meets with constitutional standards by providing adequate safeguards to insure that the retention of the subpoenaed documents serves a legitimate public purpose and is reasonable in its scope and duration.

Witnesses -- Subpoena Duces Tecum

6. Statutes which provide for the retention of documents acquired by an office subpoena duces tecum issued by the Attorney-General, which statutes also promote the public interest and prescribe safeguards to insure that the terms of retention will not unduly burden the subpoenaed party, are constitutional. (Executive Law, § 63, subd 8; CPLR 2305, subd [c].)

 

337 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Health Ins. Asso. v. Harnett


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


44 N.Y.2d 302; 376 N.E.2d 1280; 405 N.Y.S.2d 634; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 1940


March 21, 1978, Argued  
May 2, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: On plaintiffs' appeal: Order, insofar as appealed from, affirmed, without costs.

On defendant's cross appeal: Cross appeal, from so much of the Appellate Division order as affirmed the denial of defendant's motion for summary judgment with respect to plaintiffs' third cause of action, dismissed upon the ground that such portion of the order appealed from does not finally determine the action within the meaning of the Constitution; order otherwise affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The parties cross-appealed from an Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) order summarily adjudicating that the Mandatory Maternity Care Coverage Law, 1976 N.Y. Laws 843, did not violate plaintiffs' due process rights and that application of N.Y. Ins. Law §§ 162-a, 164-a, and 253(1-a) (1976) to guaranteed renewable policies issued before statutory enactment would unconstitutionally impair contracts.


OVERVIEW: The lower court entered an order granting summary judgment in favor of defendant after holding that N.Y. Ins. Law §§ 162-a, 164-a, and 253(1-a) (1976), which required health insurance policies written, altered, or amended after the statutes' effective date to include maternity care coverage to the extent that such indemnity was provided under illness and death policies, were constitutional. The lower court also granted summary judgment in favor of plaintiffs after finding that application of the statutes to guaranteed renewable policies would be an unconstitutional impairment. On appeal, the court affirmed. Sections 162-a, 164-a, and 253(1-a) did not violate plaintiffs' due process rights because the burden that was placed on them was justified by a rational relation to a valid public purpose. However, §§ 162-a, 164-a, and 253(1-a) could not constitutionally be used to modify guaranteed renewable policies issued by plaintiffs because such modification would violate the constitutional restrictions on the impairment of contractual obligations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, holding that the Mandatory Maternity Care Coverage Law (Law) did not violate due process because any burden it placed on plaintiffs was justifiably related to a valid public purpose but that the Law could not be applied to guaranteed renewable policies issued before the Law was enacted.


HEADNOTES

Insurance -- Health Insurance -- Mandatory Maternity Care Coverage

1. Chapter 843 of the Laws of 1976, which mandates the inclusion of maternity care coverage in health and accident insurance policies issued after January 1, 1977, is not unconstitutional as to its substantive provisions, but may not constitutionally require the addition of such coverage to policies in existence before that date and thereafter renewed, if the renewal is at the option of the insured alone without the consent of the insurer.

Constitutional Law -- Due Process

2. What constitutes required due process varies with the status of the party asserting that there has been a denial thereof as well as with the governmental function involved; the test for determining whether due process has been accorded may be said to be constant, i.e., whether there has been protection of the individual against arbitrary action, but the specifics required to satisfy that standard may differ.

Constitutional Law -- Due Process -- Police Power

3. A reasonable exercise of the State's police power is not constitutionally infirm as violative of the due process clause.

Constitutional Law -- Due Process -- Police Power

4. When a statute is challenged on nonprocedural grounds as violative of due process, the question is whether there is some fair, just and reasonable connection between it and the promotion of the health, comfort, safety and welfare of society.

Statutes -- Presumption of Constitutionality

5. As a matter of substantive law, every legislative enactment is deemed to be constitutional until its challengers have satisfied the court to the contrary; the Legislature is presumed to have investigated and found the existence of a situation indicating the need for and the desirability of the statute.

Insurance -- Health Insurance -- Mandatory Maternity Care Coverage

6. Insurance carriers and their trade association, challenging the constitutionality of chapter 843 of the Laws of 1976, which mandates the inclusion of maternity care coverage in health and accident insurance policies issued after January 1, 1977, and which was enacted to remedy the absence of adequate insurance coverage for rapidly rising maternity care costs falling on young families usually of moderate income, have failed to establish that the chapter is unfair, unjust or arbitrary and thus a deprivation of due process; the choice of means to meet the objective of making maternity care coverage widely available was within the legislative prerogative, and, absent an unconstitutional selection, the wisdom of the Legislature in exercising its discriminating authority is beyond the scope of judicial review.

Insurance -- Health Insurance -- Mandatory Maternity Care Coverage

7. Chapter 843 of the Laws of 1976, which mandates the inclusion of maternity care coverage in health and accident insurance policies written, altered, amended or renewed after January 1, 1977, may not constitutionally be applied to policies issued before its effective date as "guaranteed renewable policies", i.e., policies renewable at the sole option of the insured, because of the constitutional restrictions on impairment of obligations of contracts.

Constitutional Law -- Impairment of Contract Rights

8. Only on rare occasions and in extreme circumstances do rights fixed by the terms of a contract willingly entered into give way to a greater public need.

Appeal -- Court of Appeals -- Finality of Judgments and Orders

9. An order denying a motion for summary judgment is not a final order.

People v. Rolle


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


44 N.Y.2d 743; 376 N.E.2d 1322; 405 N.Y.S.2d 677; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 1950


March 23, 1978, Argued  
April 25, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed and judgment of City Court, City of Syracuse, reinstated in memorandum.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff People of New York appealed the order of the Onondaga County Court (New York), which reversed and vacated a judgment of the trial court convicting defendant, upon his plea of guilty, of a violation of N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law § 96(1), and dismissed the information.


OVERVIEW: During an investigation of illegal sale and storage of alcoholic beverages, a search warrant was executed for defendant's residence, which was found to contain bottles of wine, containers of beer and bottles of assorted liquor. Defendant was then arraigned on charges of illegal storage of an alcoholic beverage in violation of N.Y. Alco. Bev. Cont. Law § 96(1). The trial court found defendant guilty. The appellate court determined that the statute was unconstitutionally vague, because it did not give a reasonable man sufficient notice of what was prohibited and what was permitted. The court reversed the order of the appellate court. The court held that the statute was not unconstitutionally vague. The statute prohibited the unlicensed storage of alcoholic beverages. The affidavit on which the search warrant was obtained detailed ample facts of storing of alcoholic beverages for sale, and actual sales, by defendant. The criminal information charged defendant with storing wine, beer, and liquor in unlicensed premises for purposes of sale.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court and reinstated the decision of the trial court.


HEADNOTES

Intoxicating Liquors -- Storing Alcoholic Beverages Without License -- Constitutionality of Statute

An order of the County Court which reversed a judgment convicting defendant of violating section 96 (subd 1) of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law, which provided that "No alcoholic beverage shall be stored in or upon any premises which shall not be duly licensed", on the grounds that the statute was unconstitutionally vague, is reversed and the judgment of conviction is reinstated, since the purpose of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Law indicates that the regulatory law governs only the sale and distribution of such beverages and not their noncommercial possession, and defendant's conduct came within the scope of section 96.

 

339 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Estate of Fay


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


44 N.Y.2d 137; 375 N.E.2d 735; 404 N.Y.S.2d 554; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 1858


February 9, 1978, Argued  
March 29, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant half-sister sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which sustained respondent distributees' objections to the half-sister's claim to part of the decedent's estate.


OVERVIEW: The decedent's paternal half-sister filed a claim for part of the decedent's estate. The distributees, who were the decedent's maternal siblings, objected to the half-sister's claim. The trial court determined that the decedent had been born illegitimate and that the half-sister was precluded by N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law § 4-1.2 from receiving any inheritance. The lower court affirmed the trial court's decision, and the court affirmed the lower court. The court held that there was no question of law presented because the conclusion that the decedent was illegitimate was a question of fact decided by the trial court. The court found that the trial court placed greater weight upon the decedent's birth certificate, which stated that he was illegitimate, than upon his baptismal certificate, which implied a lawful marital status of his parents by including the word "conjugium." The court held that the preclusion of inheritance by the paternal kindred of an illegitimate, at least where there had been no order of filiation, was not violative of equal protection. The court found that § 4-1.2 was designed to permit only true members of the decedent's family to share in his estate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's decision that sustained the distributees' objections to the half-sister's claim to part of the decedent's estate.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child -- Presumption of Legitimacy

1. There is an established legal presumption that every person is born legitimate, but this presumption is not conclusive; it may be rebutted where to do otherwise would outrage common sense and reason.

Appeal -- Court of Appeals -- Matters Reviewable

2. Where a Surrogate found, upon the evidence presented at trial, that a decedent was not a legitimate child, and this factual determination was affirmed by the Appellate Division, it is not reviewable by the Court of Appeals.

Descent and Distribution -- Illegitimate Child -- Exclusion of Paternal Kindred from Class of Illegitimate's Distributees -- Constitutional Law

3. EPTL 4-1.2 (subd [b]), which precludes inheritance by the paternal kindred of an illegitimate child, except that the child's father may inherit if an order of filiation has been made, is not violative of equal protection.

Descent and Distribution -- Illegitimate Child -- Exclusion of Paternal Kindred from Class of Illegitimate's Distributees -- Constitutional Law

4. The statutory exclusion of paternal kindred, other than the father, from the class of an illegitimate's distributees ( EPTL 4-1.2, subd [b]) is not directed at the illegitimate and does not deny inheritance to individuals related to the illegitimate on the basis of their sex; it is merely a legislative judgment designed to ensure that only the true members of an illegitimate's family are permitted to share in his or her estate, and it serves a legitimate purpose in providing for the orderly settlement of estates and the dependability of title to property passing under the State's laws of intestate distribution.

 

340 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Rapp v. Carey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


44 N.Y.2d 157; 375 N.E.2d 745; 404 N.Y.S.2d 565; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 1860


January 12, 1978, Argued  
March 29, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Governor issued Exec. Order No. 10.1, N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 9, § 3.10, requiring appellees state employees to file financial statements and to abstain from various political and business activities. The state employees obtained an order declaring that the order was unconstitutional and enjoining its enforcement. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York) affirmed this order and the Governor sought review.


OVERVIEW: The Governor sought to justify the executive order as an implementation of N.Y. Pub. Off. Law § 74. The lower courts found that the order was an unconstitutional exercise of legislative power. The court found that § 74 established broad standards of conduct for the covered state employees and left the development of specific rules to advisory committees. The court found that implicit in the legislature's enactment of § 74 was its determination that the existence of conflicts of interest for these state employees was to be determined on a case-by-case basis, not by use of blanket prohibitions. The court found that the executive order conflicted with § 74 and exceeded the Governor's power of appointment and reached employees who could be neither directly appointed nor summarily dismissed by him. The court also found that the restriction on political activities involved a broad question of policy that was properly resolved by the legislature and not by the Governor. The court held that the Governor had only those powers delegated to him by the Constitution and the statutes and that his exercise of legislative power in enacting this executive order could not be sustained.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the Appellate Division.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Financial Disclosure by Public Employees -- Regulation of Outside Activities of Public Employees

1. Neither in the Constitution nor in the statutes is there express or implied authority for the Governor to exact compliance with Executive Order No. 10.1 (9 NYCRR 3.10), which requires a wide range of State employees within the executive branch, many of whom are not subject to removal by the Governor, to file financial disclosure statements and to abstain from various political and business activities, nor does that order merely implement existing legislation relating to conflicts of interest, but reaches beyond that and assumes the power of the Legislature to set State policy in the conflict of interest area.

Governor -- Powers of Governor

2. While the executive power of the State, vested in the Governor, is broad, and he has the power to enforce legislation and is accorded great flexibility in determining methods of enforcement, he may not go beyond stated legislative policy and prescribe a remedial device not embraced by that policy.

Public Officers -- Regulation of Outside Activities of Public Employees

3. An executive order of the Governor which presumes to prohibit service in political party office by a wide range of State employees within the executive branch, many of whom are not subject to removal by the Governor, and to prohibit, except on permission of the Board of Public Disclosure, all types of privately compensated employment by them, nullifies, rather than implements, section 74 of the Public Officers Law, the code of ethics for State officers and employees, which provides broad guidelines designed to eliminate substantial conflicts of interest between State duties and private interests, and which, together with section 73 of that law, proscribing specified transactions peculiarly vulnerable to conflicts of interest, constitutes the legislative policy of the State in the conflict of interest area; implicit in the enactment of the code of ethics is a determination by the Legislature that the existence of conflicts in those areas not specifically covered by section 73 is to be determined on a case-by-case basis, not by the use of blanket prohibitions.

Governor -- Powers of Governor

4. While the Governor is free to regulate the business activities of employees serving at his pleasure, he cannot impose restrictions on the business activities of employees not subject to summary dismissal by him, and a restriction on their political activities involves a broad question of policy, hardly resolvable by other than the Legislature.

 

341 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Subway-Surface Supervisors Asso. v. New York City Transit Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


44 N.Y.2d 101; 375 N.E.2d 384; 404 N.Y.S.2d 323; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 1900


February 6, 1978, Argued  
March 28, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order modified, without costs, and the matter remitted to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner bargaining representative appealed the order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the trial court's order that upheld the constitutionality of New York State Financial Emergency Act for the City of New York (FEA), 1975 N.Y. Law ch. 868-870, but modified the order in that FEA, as applied to pensions, was unconstitutional. Intervenor Attorney General cross-appealed.


OVERVIEW: New York City Transit Authority employee wages were frozen under FEA, due to a financial emergency. The trial court held that the wage freezes were constitutional and directed that a wage deferral agreement be made available. The appellate court modified the order by declaring § 10(1) of FEA in violation of N.Y. Const. art. V, § 7 as it affected pension calculations, and made the deferral agreement subject to § 10(2) of FEA. The court affirmed the appellate court's judgment, but modified it so as to require dismissal of the cross-appeal because the Attorney-General no longer had standing to contest the constitutional invalidity insofar as it affected pension computations where a wage deferral agreement was entered into that negated the issue. Also, FEA was not unconstitutional in that it precluded payment of the collective bargaining agreement's wage increases because the wage freeze was not in violation of the contract clause of the Federal Constitution where the protection of the integrity of contracts was not unlimited, and the State's police power could override the provisions of a contract when the impairment was reasonable and necessary to serve an important public purpose.


OUTCOME: The court modified the order of the appellate, without costs, to the extent of remitting the case to the trial court with directions to dismiss the petition insofar as it sought a declaration that the FEA violated the New York Constitution, and, as so modified, the order was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Wage Freeze Legislation

1. The New York State Financial Emergency Act for the City of New York (L 1975, chs 868-870), imposing a wage freeze on employees of the city and of covered organizations, is not unconstitutional insofar as it precluded payment of wage increases provided in an existing collective bargaining agreement.

Parties -- Standing

2. The collective bargaining representative for employees of the New York City Transit Authority has no standing to challenge, as an impairment of the right to pension benefits protected by section 7 of article V of the New York State Constitution, a provision of the New York State Financial Emergency Act for the City of New York (L 1975, chs 868-870) that, for the purpose of computing the pension base of retirement allowances, wage increases provided for in collective bargaining agreements but suspended by that act should not be considered as part of compensation or of annual salary earned or earnable, since it and the Transit Authority have entered into a wage deferment agreement, as provided for in other provisions of the act, under which pension calculations have been made as though the wages provided for in its collective bargaining agreement had in fact been paid.

Constitutional Law -- Wage Freeze Legislation

3. While the provisions of the New York State Financial Emergency Act for the City of New York (L 1975, chs 868-870), imposing a wage freeze on employees of the city and of covered organizations, worked an impairment of contract rights under collective bargaining agreements which provided for wage increases for those employees, such impairment does not violate the contract clause of the Federal Constitution (art I, § 10).

 Constitutional Law -- Impairment of Contract Rights

4. In the exercise of its police power, the State may override the provisions of a contract when the impairment is reasonable and necessary to serve an important public purpose.

Civil Service -- Wage Freeze Legislation

5. There is no basis on which to disturb a legislative finding that imposition of the wage freeze provisions of the New York State Financial Emergency Act for the City of New York (L 1975, chs 868-870) on employees of the New York City Transit Authority, which is performing governmental functions of the city and receives a substantial portion of its financial support from city funds, was necessary to the alleviation of the financial crisis confronting the city.

Civil Service -- Wage Freeze Legislation

6. The New York State Financial Emergency Act for the City of New York (L 1975, chs 868-870), which suspended increases in the wages of employees of the city and of covered organizations, provided for in existing collective bargaining agreements, was enacted at a time when the services for which compensation at the increased rates provided for by the agreements had not yet been rendered; confronted with the necessity to lighten the financial burden under which the city was laboring, the Legislature's choice of prospective impairment of executory aspects of wage contracts was reasonable.

Constitutional Law -- Wage Freeze Legislation

7. Even if the New York State Financial Emergency Act for the City of New York (L 1975, chs 868-870), imposing a wage freeze on employees of the city and of covered organizations, deprived those employees of future wage increases while the rights of city bond and note holders were unaffected there are rational bases for such differentiated treatment, and, accordingly, it would involve no denial of equal protection to the employees.

Civil Service -- Wage Freeze Legislation

8. The provisions of the New York State Financial Emergency Act for the City of New York (L 1975, chs 868-870) imposing a wage freeze on employees of the city and of covered organizations do not constitute a plan of composition in violation of Federal bankruptcy law or an intrusion into an area vested exclusively in the Federal judicial system.

Carl A. Morse, Inc. v. Rentar Industrial Development Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


43 N.Y.2d 952; 375 N.E.2d 409; 404 N.Y.S.2d 343; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 1854


February 6, 1978, Argued  
March 21, 1978, Decided


HEADNOTES

Liens -- Mechanics' Liens -- Constitutionality

In an action for foreclosure of mechanics' liens, wherein, in support of a motion [**3] for partial summary judgment, defendants alleged that plaintiff was acting as an agent for the owner when it performed the work and, therefore, was not a valid lienor, and that the provisions of the Lien Law with respect to mechanics' liens (art 2) unconstitutionally deprived an owner of his property without due process of law, an order of the Appellate Division which affirmed the denial of their motion is affirmed on the opinion at the Appellate Division. In addition, it is observed that the dissenting opinion at the Appellate Division confuses the impact of a lien on property which does not dispossess the owner with the line of cases in the United States Supreme Court involving precisely such dispossession by seizure without preliminary judicial determination.

In the Matter of Fuchsberg


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


STATE OF NEW YORK COURT ON THE JUDICIARY
