344 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


McGirr v. Division of Veterans Affairs, Executive Dep't


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


43 N.Y.2d 635; 374 N.E.2d 123; 403 N.Y.S.2d 212; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 1781


January 11, 1978, Argued  
February 22, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner ex-employee sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which dismissed the ex-employee's action for judicial review of his termination by respondents New York Division of Veterans Affairs.


OVERVIEW: The division fired the ex-employee for being absent without sufficient explanation for more than 10 working days, in violation of N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 4, § 5.3(d). The division denied the ex-employee's request for reconsideration, and upheld his termination. The ex-employee appealed to the New York State Civil Service Commission, which dismissed his appeal, holding that no appeal could be taken from a "voluntary" termination. The ex-employee then sought judicial review. The trial court held in favor of the ex-employee. The appellate court reversed, and dismissed the ex-employee's action. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the ex-employee's administrative appeal to the commission was time barred by N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 76(1) because it was not brought within 20 days of his termination, and could not be resurrected in a petition for judicial review brought under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78. The court further held that the ex-employee's petition for judicial review was also time barred, because it was not brought within four months of the division's denial of reconsideration, as required by N.Y. C.P.L.R. 217.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's order which reversed the trial court's judgment ordering the division to reinstate the ex-employee, and dismissed the ex-employee's action for judicial review of his termination.


HEADNOTES

Civil Service -- Reinstatement -- Limitation of Actions

1. An article 78 proceeding brought by a civil service employee seeking reinstatement to his position is time-barred as against the agency which employed him, where it was brought more than four months after that agency denied a request by him for reconsideration; his invocation of reconsideration served as a demand for reinstatement, and the agency's denial of reconsideration constituted a rejection of that demand, and set in motion the four-month period within which judicial review had to be requested ( CPLR 217).

Civil Service -- Appeal to Civil Service Commission -- Judicial Review

2. Where a civil service employee takes an administrative appeal to the Civil Service Commission from a determination of his employing agency, as provided for in section 76 of the Civil Service Law, but fails to take it within the 20-day period of limitation prescribed by that section, administrative review of the merits of the agency's determination by the commission is time-barred, and the employee may not resurrect the same question by means of an article 78 proceeding for judicial review of the commission's dismissal of his belated appeal.

Civil Service -- Dismissal for Unexplained Absence without Leave

3. The decision in Matter of Johnson v Director, Downstate Med. Center (41 NY2d 1061), invalidating a rule of the State Department of Civil Service (4 NYCRR 5.3 [d]), which provided that an employee was deemed to have resigned his position when he was absent without leave and without an explanation for a period in excess of 10 working days, does not hold that an unexplained absence may not be grounds for dismissal of an employee; the thrust of that decision is that the full panoply of procedural due process required by section 75 of the Civil Service Law may not be circumvented by an administrative rule creating an irrebuttable presumption of resignation.

 

345 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Holly S. Clarendon Trust v. State Tax Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


43 N.Y.2d 933; 374 N.E.2d 1242; 403 N.Y.S.2d 891; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 1831


January 13, 1978, Argued  
February 22, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state tax commission challenged a judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term in Monroe County (New York), which found in favor of respondent taxpayer in his challenge to an increased tax assessment and which declared N.Y. Tax Law § 618(4) (as amended by 1973 N.Y. Laws ch. 718, § 2) unconstitutional to the extent that it was applied retrospectively.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer argued that the tax commission's retrospective application of the amended version of § 618(4), which limited the amount of deduction that could be taken on long-term net capital gains, was improper. On review, the court affirmed the trial court's judgment in favor of the taxpayer. In reaching its conclusion, the court held that the apparent absence of a persuasive reason for the statute's retroactive application, combined with such an application's potentially harsh effects, offended constitutional limits.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment declaring the state tax law unconstitutional to the extent that it was applied retrospectively and finding in favor of the tax payer in its challenge to an increased tax assessment.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Retroactive Application of Income Tax Statute

Supreme Court properly declared section 618 (subd [4]) of the Tax Law (as amd by L 1973, ch 718, § 2) unconstitutional insofar as it applied retroactively to the 1972 income tax liability of plaintiff, an inter vivos New York trust, since, although retroactivity provisions in tax statutes, if for a short period, are generally valid, the apparent absence of a persuasive reason for retroactivity, with its potentially harsh effects, offends constitutional limits, especially when the tax imposed is one which might exert significant influence on personal or business transactions.

Association of Personnel Agencies, Inc. v. Ross


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York

347 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Martin v. Julius Dierck Equip. Co.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


43 N.Y.2d 583; 374 N.E.2d 97; 403 N.Y.S.2d 185; 1978 N.Y. LEXIS 1776


November 17, 1977, Submitted  
January 18, 1978, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff individual challenged a judgment from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed the trial court's decision and granted summary judgment in favor of defendants, distributor and manufacturer, in plaintiff's negligence and strict products liability action, finding that plaintiff's claims were barred by the statute of limitations.


OVERVIEW: Although plaintiff individual was injured in Virginia, he brought a negligence and strict products liability action against defendants, manufacturer and distributor, in New York, where defendants had principal places of business. The trial court denied defendants' motions for summary judgment, but the lower appellate court reversed the trial court and granted the motions. The court affirmed. The court rejected as illogical plaintiff's argument that his cause of action for strict products liability accrued in New York when defendant manufacturer tendered delivery to defendant distributor. The court held that for purposes of the borrowing statute, N.Y. C.P.L.R. 202, plaintiff's causes of action for negligence and strict products liability accrued in Virginia, the place of injury. Because Virginia applied a two-year statute of limitations, Va. Code Ann. § 8-24 (1950), the court held that plaintiff's action was untimely. The court also held that Virginia's tolling statute, Va. Code Ann. § 8-33 (1950), was inapplicable because defendants were not residents of Virginia before plaintiff's cause of action accrued.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower appellate court's order, which granted summary judgment in favor of defendants, manufacturer and distributor. Plaintiff individual's negligence and strict product liability causes of action accrued when he was injured in Virginia, and Virginia's two-year statute of limitations barred his action. The court also held that Virginia's tolling statute was inapplicable.


HEADNOTES

Limitation of Actions -- Foreign Limitations Statute

1. Causes of action in negligence and in what is denominated as breach of warranty but is, in reality, strict products liability, asserted against the New York manufacturer and distributor of a forklift truck which allegedly malfunctioned while plaintiff, a resident of the District of Columbia, was operating it on his employer's premises in Virginia, accrued in Virginia, as the place of the injury, for purposes of New York's borrowing statute ( CPLR 202), which provides that an action based upon a cause of action accruing outside of New York in favor of a nonresident cannot be commenced after the expiration of the time limited by the laws of either New York or the place where the cause of action accrued.

Contracts -- Breach of Warranty

2. A cause of action for breach of warranty is a contractual remedy, designed to enforce the agreement, express or implied, of the parties and to place them, should one of the parties fail to perform in accordance with the agreement, in the same position in which they would have been had the agreement been performed.

Products Liability -- Nature of Remedy

3. A cause of action for negligence or for strict products liability seeks to provide a remedy for an individual injured because of another's violation of an obligation imposed not by contract, but by law, and endeavors to place him in the position he occupied prior to his injury, or to make him "whole".

Products Liability -- Nature of Remedy

4. A plaintiff who is not in privity with the seller of a product which is alleged to have caused his injury possesses a cause of action in negligence or strict products liability, as opposed to breach of warranty.

Products Liability -- Nature of Remedy

5. Strict products liability sounds in tort exclusively and not at all in contract.

 

348 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Bernstein v. Toia


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


43 N.Y.2d 437; 373 N.E.2d 238; 402 N.Y.S.2d 342; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2562


November 9, 1977, Argued  
December 21, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, without costs, and the petition dismissed. Question certified answered in the negative.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant New York Department of Social Services sought review of the decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which unanimously affirmed an order denying motions to dismiss the petition of respondent public assistance recipients. Respondents sought a declaration that a regulation of appellant was invalid.


OVERVIEW: Respondent public assistance recipients sought a declaration that a regulation of appellant New York Department of Social Services (department), which fixed a maximum shelter allowance in each district, was invalid because of its failure to make provision for exceptions due to special circumstances in individual cases. The courts below invalidated the regulation. The department had issued the regulation pursuant to N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 131-a, which contained the basic legislative authorization for monthly grants and allowances of public assistance. The court stated that the statute itself was not explicit as to how grants for shelter should be fixed and that it was the department's responsibility to establish rules, regulations and policies to carry out its duties. Further, the regulation reflecting the department's choice was beyond the court's power to disturb unless it was so lacking in reason for its promulgation that it was arbitrary. The court reversed the lower court's decision because it found no sufficient basis to disturb the regulation because the flat grant concept in the administration of shelter allowances was neither unconstitutional nor in conflict with § 131-a.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the appellate division without costs, and the petition was dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Social Services -- Shelter Allowances

1. A regulation of the Department of Social Services (18 NYCRR 352.3 [a]), which fixes a maximum shelter allowance for recipients of public assistance in each social services district, with variations from district to district and for family size within each district, making no provision for exceptions due to special circumstances in individual cases, is not violative of the constitutional guarantees of equal protection and due process or of the constitutional mandate that the State provide for the aid, care and support of the needy, nor is it in conflict with section 131-a of the Social Services Law, which contains the basic legislative authorization for monthly grants and allowances of public assistance.

Social Services -- Flat Grant Concept of Public Assistance

2. The flat grant concept of public assistance, under which, after statistical and qualitative analysis of a fair sampling of individual grants, a uniform figure is fixed that is determined to be adequate in general to meet the needs in question, has a rational relationship to the legitimate State interest of seeking to assure optimum realized benefits from available public assistance moneys, and is not violative of equal protection and due process.

Social Services -- Shelter Allowances

3. Section 131-a of the Social Services Law, which provides for monthly grants and allowances of public assistance, sets forth maximum grants for basic needs, and requires the inclusion of additional amounts for, inter alia, shelter, is not explicit as to whether grants for shelter shall be fixed by the employment of flat grants, which subject the allowances payable to fixed ceilings, or by special grants, the entitlement to and the amount of which would be determined on an individual basis taking into account the particular circumstances of each recipient, and, therefore, it is the responsibility of the Department of Social Services to implement its provisions and make the choice between flat grants and special grants for shelter allowances.

Statutes -- Construction Given Statutes by Administrative Agency

4. The construction given statutes and regulations by the agency responsible for their administration, if not irrational or unreasonable, should be upheld.

Constitutional Law -- Obligation of State to Aid Needy

5. Article XVII of the State Constitution, which requires the State to provide for the aid, care and support of the needy, does not mandate that public assistance be granted on an individual basis in every instance, nor does it command that the State always meet in full measure all the legitimate needs of each public assistance recipient.

Lee v. Smith


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


43 N.Y.2d 453; 373 N.E.2d 247; 402 N.Y.S.2d 351; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2563


November 9, 1977, Argued  
December 21, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant government agency sought review of a ruling from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which unanimously affirmed a judgment of the trial court. The appellate court and the trial court both held that NY. Soc. Serv. Law. § 158 violated appellee disabled persons' equal protection guarantees found in the state and federal constitutions, as well as in the N.Y. Const. art XVII.


OVERVIEW: The disabled persons contended that the government's refusal to grant their petitions for state aid simply because they received SSI from the federal government was wrong and deprived them of their guarantee of equal protection under both the state and federal constitutions. They further contended that the statute in question violated N.Y. Const. art XVII, which imposed an affirmative duty on the government to aid the needy. The government argued that the statute was constitutional because the recipients in both categories were treated alike in that they were all granted amounts, which satisfied their standard of monthly need. The court rejected the government's argument and affirmed the appellate court's decision. The court noted that the government was constitutionally charged with aiding the needy. The court then found that classification of the aged, disabled, and blind into a separate category, which provided less benefits than those needy persons not suffering from the disabilities. The court concluded that the government's law bore no rational relationship to the objective of ensuring that the federal government continued to bear the cost of administering the SSI program.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's decision.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Obligation of State to Aid Needy

1. The State's constitutional obligation to provide for the aid, care and support of the needy (NY Const, art XVII) imposes upon the State an affirmative duty to aid the needy.

Constitutional Law -- Obligation of State to Aid Needy

2. Under the equal protection guarantees of the State and Federal Constitutions, any legislative classification which denies to one class of needy persons public assistance which is available to all others cannot be justified unless it is rationally related to a legitimate State interest.

Social Services -- Eligibility for Home Relief

3. While the State may have a legitimate interest in seeing that the Federal Government continues to bear the costs of administering the Supplemental Security Income (SSI) program (US Code, tit 42, § 1381 et seq.), section 158 (subd [a]) of the Social Services Law, which provides that those aged, disabled and blind who are SSI recipients are ineligible for home relief, bears no rational relationship to that objective.

Social Services -- Eligibility for Home Relief

4. While the State may have a legitimate interest in reducing the costs of administering its home relief program, it may not accomplish that result by arbitrarily denying home relief to those who receive income under the Federal Supplemental Security Income program when it does not deny home relief to other needy individuals who receive income from other sources.

Social Services -- Eligibility for Home Relief

5. Section 158 (subd [a]) of the Social Services Law, insofar as it provides that a "person who is receiving federal supplemental security income payments and/or additional state payments shall not be eligible for home relief", is unconstitutional as violative of the equal protection guarantees of the State and Federal Constitutions as well as article XVII of the State Constitution, which requires the State to provide for the aid, care and support of the needy.

 

350 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Suffolk Outdoor Advertising Co. v. Hulse


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


43 N.Y.2d 483; 373 N.E.2d 263; 402 N.Y.S.2d 368; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2565; 8 ELR 20185


October 11, 1977, Argued  
December 21, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order modified, with costs to defendants, in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed. Question certified answered in the negative.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York) held that the only viable claim of plaintiff owners of nonconforming billboards with respect to the alleged unconstitutionality of an ordinance passed by defendant town was that it was not reasonably related to the public safety and welfare, and that a triable issue of fact had been created as to that issue. The owners appealed.


OVERVIEW: Southampton, N.Y., Building Zone Ordinance No. 26 prohibited the erection of all nonaccessory billboards. The ordinance further provided for the removal of all nonconforming billboards. However, the owners of nonconforming billboards (owners) were given the opportunity to apply for an extension of the amortization period. The owners sought a declaration that the ordinance was unconstitutional in that it was not reasonably related to public safety and welfare. The court held that: (1) the ordinance was reasonably related to public safety and welfare, and, as such, was a valid exercise of the police power; (2) that the ordinance did not infringe upon the right to free speech; (3) aesthetics alone was a valid basis for the exercise of the police power, and the ordinance was substantially related to the effectuation of that objective; and (4) the owners had failed to apply for an extension, as the ordinance gave them an opportunity to do, and thus had failed to exhaust the administrative remedy available to them. Therefore, it was premature to pass upon the reasonableness of the amortization period as applied.


OUTCOME: The order of the appellate court was modified, and as so modified, was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Regulation of Billboards

1. A town ordinance which prohibits the erection of all nonaccessory billboards in all districts throughout the town; provides for the removal of all nonconforming billboards on or before a certain date, approximately three years after the adoption of the ordinance; gives the owners of nonconforming billboards an opportunity to apply for an extension of the three-year amortization period, and makes an exception for the town to establish public information centers where business signs may be located, is reasonably related to public safety and welfare, and, as such, is a valid exercise of the police power.

Constitutional Law -- Protection of Commercial Speech

2. While commercial speech falls within the protection of the First and Fourteenth Amendments, a State and its political subdivisions may regulate the time, place and manner of it, as opposed to its contents, to effectuate a significant governmental interest, and the regulation of aesthetics constitutes such an interest.

Municipal Corporations -- Regulation of Billboards

3. An ordinance which makes no attempt to regulate the content of the commercial speech appearing on billboards, but regulates only the place and manner in which they may be maintained, does not infringe upon the right to free speech.

Constitutional Law -- Police Power

4. Once it is established that a regulation enacted pursuant to the police power has a valid basis, it need only be shown to sustain its constitutionality that it is reasonably related to the objective for which it was enacted.

Municipal Corporations -- Regulation of Billboards

5. Aesthetics alone constitutes a valid basis for the exercise of the police power and an ordinance prohibiting nonaccessory billboards is substantially related to the effectuation of this objective.

Municipal Corporations -- Regulation of Billboards

6. Where an ordinance prohibits nonaccessory billboards and gives the owners three years to remove them, but also gives them an opportunity to obtain an extension of that amortization period if it can be established that, as to a particular sign, the three-year period is unreasonable, and the owners fail to apply for such an extension, and thus fail to exhaust the administrative remedy available to them under the ordinance before instituting an action challenging its validity, it would be premature for a court to pass upon the reasonableness of the amortization period as applied.

Municipal Corporations -- Regulation of Billboards

7. The Federal Highway Beautification Act of 1965 (US Code, tit 23, § 131) and section 88 of the Highway Law do not preclude a municipality from directing the removal of nonconforming billboards without making provision for compensation.

 

351 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Modjeska Sign Studios, Inc. v. Berle


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


43 N.Y.2d 468; 373 N.E.2d 255; 402 N.Y.S.2d 359; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2564; 8 ELR 20183; 11 ERC (BNA) 1107


October 11, 1977, Argued  
December 21, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs, and the case remitted to Supreme Court, Albany County, for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein. Appeal by "Proposed Intervenor" Dale dismissed, with costs, upon the ground that it does not lie.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property lessee sought review of the decision by the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the summary judgement granted for the defendant State upon the lessee's claim that requirements to remove billboards were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: A property lessee and several landowners entered into agreements to allow for billboards to be erected for advertising purposes along the roadway. The State created regulations that required the removal of the billboards but allowed an amortization period for compliance. Right before the amortization period was to expire, the property lessee sought a declaration that the regulations were unconstitutional as a taking without just compensation. The trial court granted summary judgment to the State, and the decision was affirmed on appeal. The property lessee sought further review, and the court reversed the lower courts' decisions. The court found that the aesthetic goal of the billboard removal was a proper exercise of police power but that the need for immediate removal of the billboards was to be determined by the reasonableness of the amortization period. The court remanded the matter for a determination of the reasonableness of the proscribed period.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the summary judgment for the State and ordered a hearing to determine if the statutory amortization period of six and one-half years was unreasonable as applied to the billboards.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional Law -- Police Power

1. Aesthetics alone is a valid basis for the exercise of the police power.

Constitutional Law -- Police Power

2. The requirement that an exercise of the police power be reasonable mandates not only that the regulation relate to the purpose for which it was enacted, but also that it does not unreasonably deprive an owner of all beneficial use of his property.

Environmental Conservation -- Regulation of Advertising Signs

3. The restrictions placed upon the use of property by ECL 9-0305, which provides that, to ensure the natural beauty of the Catskill and Adirondack Parks, advertising signs and structures, for which a permit is not obtained, are prohibited, except accessory signs and signs located within the limits of an incorporated village, and which required the removal by January 1, 1976 of signs erected as of May 26, 1969 which were not in conformance with regulations promulgated to implement that section, do not rise to the level of a "taking" or confiscation without due process of law under the guise of the police power.

Environmental Conservation -- Regulation of Advertising Signs

4. The prohibition of billboards does not deprive landowners or their lessees of all reasonable use of their property, and thus does not unreasonably deprive them of property without due process of law.

Environmental Conservation -- Regulation for Aesthetic Purposes

5. A regulation enacted solely for aesthetic purposes, in contrast to a safety-motivated regulation, generally does not provide a compelling reason for immediate implementation with respect to existing structures or uses.

Environmental Conservation -- Reasonableness of Amortization Period

6. Whether an amortization period, a period during which an existing nonconforming use may be continued, is reasonable depends upon the facts of each particular case, but, if an owner can show that the loss he suffers as a result of the removal of a nonconforming use at the expiration of an amortization period is so substantial that it outweighs the public benefit gained by the legislation, then the amortization period must be held unreasonable.

Environmental Conservation -- Regulation of Advertising Signs

7. An owner of signs situated within the Catskill Park, who, pursuant to ECL 9-0305, was given six and one-half years to remove those signs, is entitled to an opportunity to establish, if it can, that the statutory amortization period is unreasonable as applied to it.

Environmental Conservation -- Regulation of Advertising Signs

8. By choosing to provide compensation for billboards removed to beautify Federally aided highways (Highway Law, § 88), the Legislature did not relinquish its authority to require the removal of billboards pursuant to the police power without compensating those owners adversely affected.

Environmental Conservation -- Regulation of Advertising Signs

9. ECL 9-0305, prohibiting the erection of advertising signs and structures within the Catskill and Adirondack Parks, without permit, and requiring the removal of nonconforming signs within six and one-half years, is not violative of the constitutional guarantee of free speech.

 

352 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Lublin v. Central Islip Psychiatric Center


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


43 N.Y.2d 341; 372 N.E.2d 307; 401 N.Y.S.2d 466; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2468


November 16, 1977, Argued  
December 19, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, without costs, and the matter remitted to the Appellate Division, Second Department, for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner appealed an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed and remanded an order from the trial court denying petitioner's application for a conditional release under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law 330.20(5) from respondent center holding that the burden of proof should have been placed on respondent commissioner.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was found not guilty of murder by reason of mental disease or defect, and was committed pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law 330.20(1). Petitioner brought suit to seek release pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law 330.20(5). The trial court denied the petition on the grounds that petitioner failed to prove by a fair preponderance of the evidence that he could be released without danger to himself or others. The appellate court reversed, holding that the burden of proof should have been placed on respondent commissioner. On appeal, the supreme court held that trial court properly placed upon petitioner the burden of proving by a fair preponderance of the evidence that he could be released without danger. The court found that it was beyond doubt that petitioner was both insane and dangerous at the time of the murder and it was only because of his insanity that he was relieved of criminal liability. Given the clear existence of this condition, it was appropriate that the condition be presumed to continue until the contrary was proven.


OUTCOME: The order of the appellate court was reversed because the trial court properly placed upon petitioner the burden of showing by a fair preponderance of the credible evidence that he could be discharged or released without danger to himself or others.


HEADNOTES

Insane and Other Incompetent Persons -- Release From Confinement After Acquittal By Reason of Insanity -- Burden of Proof

1. A person who has been validly committed to an institution as a result of an acquittal of a crime by reason of mental defect or disease, and who at a later time seeks release on the claimed ground that he is no longer dangerous to either himself or others, is required to prove by a fair preponderance of the evidence that he may safely be released.

Insane and Other Incompetent Persons -- Release From Confinement After Acquittal By Reason of Insanity -- Burden of Proof

2. A person who was both insane and dangerous at the time he murdered his wife and attempted to take his own life and was relieved of criminal liability only by his clearly insane condition, as evidenced by the admitted commission of a violent act, is presumed to continue in that condition until the contrary is proven. The burden placed on him is neither excessive nor novel, for the burden of proof is normally placed upon the party seeking affirmative relief. Moreover, the burden of proof becomes significant only when the evidence is so delicately balanced that to reach a solution it is necessary to give one side or the other the benefit of the doubt, and, upon a balancing of the conflicting interests implicated, the petitioner's very strong and basic interest in liberty is outweighed by the assertion by the State of its parens patriae interest in the mentally incompetent, with its concomitant obligation to protect them from harm to either themselves or others, and its responsibility to exercise its police powers to protect its citizenry from an individual who has given proof positive that he is in fact dangerous to both society and himself.

Insane and Other Incompetent Persons -- Release From Confinement After Acquittal By Reason of Insanity -- Burden of Proof

3. An individual who is seeking release from commitment after a finding of not guilty by reason of insanity is not required to prove lack of dangerousness beyond a reasonable doubt. Aside from the clear constitutional objections to the imposition of such a harsh burden, CPL 330.20 (subd 3) characterizes such a hearing as a civil proceeding and the normally applicable standard of proof in such a proceeding is that petitioner need only show by a fair preponderance of the credible evidence that he may be discharged or released without danger to himself or others.

Sonmax, Inc. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


43 N.Y.2d 253; 372 N.E.2d 9; 401 N.Y.S.2d 173; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2458


November 14, 1977, Argued  
December 15, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners sought review from a judgment of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which declared that local municipal laws were validly enacted by the city council and were constitutional. The property owners contended that the local law that reduced from three years to one year the period of time required before the commencement of in rem tax foreclosure proceedings was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The property owners filed their declaratory judgment action challenging the constitutionality of the local law that reduced the waiting period preceding the institution of tax foreclosure proceedings. The court noted that rather than seek amendment of the state law by the legislature the municipality had adopted its own local enactment to reduce the waiting period to one year. The lower court granted summary judgment to the municipality and the property owners sought appellate review. The court held that whether the state law was a general law or not was inconsequential because the local law was not inconsistent with such law, even if it were deemed general. The court noted that the state procedure expressly provided that it was optional and that local tax districts were entitled to take advantage of its provisions but were not required to do so. The court concluded that there was nothing in the state statute that foreclosed the establishment of local in rem foreclosure procedures by the adoption of special acts. Accordingly, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, which held that the enactment of local laws that reduced the waiting period for the institution of in rem tax foreclosure proceedings was constitutional and not inconsistent with state law.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Tax Liens, Tax Sales and Tax Titles -- In Rem Foreclosure Proceedings

1. The enactment by the City of New York of Local Law No. 45 of 1976, by the terms of which the period of time required to elapse before in rem tax foreclosure proceedings may be instituted was reduced from three years to one year, was not violative of any provision of the New York State Constitution.

Constitutional Law -- Delegation by Legislature of Authority to Assess and Collect Taxes

2. While all taxing power in the State of New York is vested in the Legislature pursuant to article III (§ 1) and article XVI (§ 1) of the New York State Constitution, the Legislature can delegate authority to assess and collect taxes to a city.

Taxation -- Tax Lien, Tax Sales and Tax Titles -- In Rem Foreclosure Proceedings

3. Even assuming that title 3 of article 11 of the Real Property Tax Law, the successor to the so-called Stagg Act, the State's first in rem foreclosure procedure legislation (L 1939, ch 692), is a general law within the contemplation of the constitutional and statutory provisions barring the enactment of local laws inconsistent with general laws (NY Const, art IX, § 2, subd [c]; Municipal Home Rule Law, § 10), Local Law No. 45 of the Local Laws of the City of New York of 1976, which reduced from three years to one year the period of time required to elapse before in rem tax foreclosure proceedings may be instituted, is not inconsistent with that title, which establishes an optional procedure and does not foreclose the establishment of local in rem foreclosure procedures by the adoption of special acts.

Constitutional Law -- In Rem Foreclosure Proceedings

4. Local Law No. 45 of the Local Laws of the City of New York of 1976, as amended by Local Law No. 6 of 1977, which reduced from three years to one year the period of time required to elapse before in rem tax foreclosure proceedings may be instituted, except for owner-occupied one-family and two-family dwellings taxed at not more than $ 2,000 per year, does not deny equal protection of the laws.

 

354 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Wein v. Beame


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


43 N.Y.2d 326; 372 N.E.2d 300; 401 N.Y.S.2d 458; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2466


November 10, 1977, Argued  
December 15, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff sought review of the order by the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which granted summary judgment to defendant, declaring that a plan formulated by a private developer and the bankrupt owner of a deteriorated hotel, in which the city agreed to waive its right under the Urban Development Act (Act), N.Y. Act ch. 174, § 22 (1968), was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff contended the project was not a New York Urban Development Corporation project (project), an erroneous standard; that it would have been a judicial usurpation to have intervened in the transaction and the resolution and agreements to surrender, suspend, and contract away the power of taxation in violation of the N.Y. Const. art. XVI, § 1. Defendant contended there was no surrender, suspension, or contracting away of the city's sovereign taxing powers since the renovation of the hotel was an industrial project undertaken by the project, which by statute, was exempt from city imposed real estate taxes. The court affirmed, finding there was a strong presumption of constitutionality of the legislative act, especially in the area of municipal financing. The court held the purchase of the hotel by the project was a valid tax exemption by operation law. Since the only tax exemption was the explicit the explicit statutory exemption granted by the project, the validity which was unchallenged by plaintiff, there was no violation of the constitutional prohibition against special exemptions, and no contracting away of the city' power to tax.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that in favor of defendant that affirmed the constitutionality of the Act.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Constitutionality of Plan for Renovation of Hotel

1. A plan formulated by the City of New York, the Urban Development Corporation (UDC), a private developer, and the bankrupt owner of a badly deteriorated hotel in the heart of the city, on which the tax arrears amount to over 10 million dollars, which is intended to result in the renovation of the hotel by the private developer, and which provides for the purchase of it by the private developer for 10 million dollars; the sale of it by the private developer to UDC for one dollar; a waiver by the city of its right to reimbursement from the State of an amount equal to the tax revenues it would have derived absent the tax exemption which will be created by the UDC purchase (UDC Act, §§ 22, 26 [L 1968, ch 174, as amd by L 1970, ch 1041]), and a UDC lease-back of the hotel to the private developer under a 99-year lease, with the rental payable to the city, in amounts that, for the first 40 years, will be less than the taxes which could otherwise be assessed, and, for the remainder of the lease, will be equal to the taxes, and with title to the property to vest in the city at the termination of the lease, does not violate the constitutional proscriptions against special tax exemptions and the contracting away of the power to tax (NY Const, art XVI, § 1).

Constitutional Law -- Presumption of Constitutionality of Legislative Actions

2. A strong presumption of constitutionality accompanies legislative actions, especially in the area of municipal financing, and such actions will be sustained absent a clear showing of unconstitutionality.

 Constitutional Law -- Prohibition Against Special Tax Exemptions

3. Where, under a plan designed to combat otherwise inevitable urban blight, a property in New York City will obtain tax exempt status, not by any legislation enacted by the city council, but by virtue of the purchase of it by the Urban Development Corporation (UDC), to which an explicit statutory exemption has been granted (UDC Act, § 22 [L 1968, ch 174]), there is no violation of the constitutional prohibition against special tax exemptions and no contracting away of the power to tax by the city (NY Const, art XVI, § 1), regardless of whether the plan was originally developed by the city, UDC or the private developer to whom UDC will lease it back, and regardless of the extent of actual participation in the project by UDC.

Constitutional Law -- Prohibition Against Special Tax Exemptions

4. The constitutional prohibition against special tax exemptions (NY Const, art XVI, § 1) operates only to prevent a city in its sovereign role as tax gatherer from directly granting an exemption to a taxpayer, and is not to be automatically applied whenever a municipality is engaged in a business transaction which will of necessity have tax consequences upon another party to the transaction.

Constitutional Law -- Prohibition Against Special Tax Exemptions

5. Payments which the State has elected to make to reimburse municipalities for loss in tax revenues which would otherwise result from a purchase of previously taxable property by the Urban Development Corporation (UDC Act, § 26 [L 1968, ch 174, as amd by L 1970, ch 1041]) cannot be classified as taxes, and thus a waiver by a municipality of the right to receive such funds is not the equivalent of granting a special tax exemption in violation of the constitutional prohibition against such special exemptions (NY Const, art XVI, § 1).

 

355 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Bianca v. Frank


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


43 N.Y.2d 168; 371 N.E.2d 792; 401 N.Y.S.2d 29; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2449


October 13, 1977, Argued  
November 22, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, without costs, because of excessive length of respondent's brief.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant police commissioner challenged an order of the Appellate Division in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which modified an order of the trial court to, in effect, reinstate respondent police officer's N.Y. C.P.L.R. article 78 proceeding to annul the police commissioner's determination in a disciplinary hearing and to seek reinstatement. The trial court had dismissed the proceeding as time-barred.


OVERVIEW: The police officer was charged with violation of the police department's rules and regulations. A disciplinary hearing was held by the police commissioner. Thereafter, the police officer was served with a notice advising him of his dismissal from the force, but his counsel was never served with the notice of disposition. More than 90 days after the police officer was served with notice of his dismissal, he commenced an article 78 proceeding to annul the determination and seek reinstatement. The trial court dismissed the action as time-barred under Nassau County Administrative Code § 8-13.0(d), which required that a petition to review be filed within 30 days of service upon the member of the force. The appellate division effectively reversed and reinstated the police officer's petition. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that "upon the member of the force" had to read in the accepted sense to require, at least, that notice be served upon the police officer and that, without service on such attorney, the time limitations set forth in the code did not begin to run.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the appellate division reinstating the police officer's case.


HEADNOTES

Proceeding Against Body or Officer -- Limitations of Time -- Notice of Disposition

1. An attorney who represented a police officer at a disciplinary proceeding is required to be served with a copy of the determination of the Police Commissioner in order to commence the running of the applicable 30-day Statute of Limitations for review.

Limitations of Actions -- Notice of Disposition to Counsel

2. Basic procedural dictates and fundamental policy considerations require that once counsel has appeared in a matter a Statute of Limitations or time requirement cannot begin to run unless that counsel is served with the determination or the order or judgment sought to be reviewed.

Attorney and Client -- Service of Papers Upon Attorney

3. Once a party chooses to be represented by counsel in an action or proceeding, whether administrative or judicial, the attorney is deemed to act as his agent in all respects relevant to the proceeding. Thus any documents, particularly those purporting to have legal effect on the proceeding, should be served on the attorney the party has chosen to handle the matter on his behalf.

Attorney and Client -- Service of Papers Upon Attorney

4. Unless a legislative enactment specifically excludes the necessity of serving counsel and the intention to so depart from standard practice is clearly established and stated in unmistakable terms, any general requirement that notice must be served upon a party must be read in the accepted sense to require, at least, that notice be served upon the attorney the party has chosen to represent him.

 

356 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Parker v. Stage


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


43 N.Y.2d 128; 371 N.E.2d 513; 400 N.Y.S.2d 794; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2444; 98 A.L.R.3d 328


October 14, 1977, Argued  
November 21, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant commissioner appealed an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the dismissal of his petition to compel respondent father to support his daughter who was receiving public assistance.


OVERVIEW: The commissioner of the county department of social services commenced a proceeding to compel a father to contribute toward the support of his 18-year old daughter who was receiving public assistance. The family court dismissed the petition because the daughter emancipated herself by voluntarily leaving the father's house to live with her paramour and have his child out of wedlock. The appellate court affirmed. The Commissioner appealed. The court held that the appellate court, in the interest of preserving family unity and justice, had the discretion to refuse to compel the father to support a daughter who chose to leave home. The court found that the abandoned father continuously supported the daughter from birth and urged her to remain at home and to continue schooling or obtain employment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the commissioner's petition and finding that the father was not obligated to support his daughter.


HEADNOTES

Parent and Child -- Support of Children

1. Although the statutory obligation of a father to support a child (Family Ct Act, § 413; Social Services Law, § 101) is stated in mandatory terms, a child who voluntarily and without good cause abandons the parent's home forfeits her right to support.

Social Services -- Liability of Relatives to Support Needy Individuals

2. While it was once the policy of New York State to place the financial burden of supporting needy individuals upon designated relatives, rather than the public, in order to reduce the amount of welfare expenditures, it now recognizes the need to preserve family unity even though the consequence is to place needy relatives on the public welfare rolls.

Parent and Child -- Support of Children

3. The courts have discretionary powers to refuse to enforce a father's obligation to support his children until they are 21 years of age when enforcement of that obligation might lead to an injustice.

Social Services -- Liability of Parent to Support Child

4. The duty of a parent to pay for the support of a child who chooses to leave home, and applies for public assistance, is not absolute; the courts must consider the impact on the family relationship and the possibility of injustice in the particular case.

Social Services -- Liability of Parent to Support Child

5. Where a father continuously supported his 18-year-old daughter from birth, urged her to remain at home and continue her schooling, was a forgiving parent who always accepted her back after her absences, and made efforts to obtain employment for her, but she chose to leave home to live with her paramour, the father should not be obligated to pay for her support even though she is receiving public assistance.

 

357 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Riley v. County of Monroe


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


43 N.Y.2d 144; 371 N.E.2d 520; 400 N.Y.S.2d 801; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2446


October 11, 1977, Argued  
November 21, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, class of taxpayers, challenged the order from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York) granting summary judgment in favor of respondent county and held that the use of countywide revenues to build and operate a sanitary landfill for the exclusive use of the residents of the northeast quadrant of the county was permitted under N.Y. County Law § 226-b, and was not a denial of equal protection of the law.


OVERVIEW: A class of county taxpayers and a town commenced an action in the trial court to challenge a county's use of county-wide real property tax revenues to build and operate a sanitary landfill for the exclusive use of the residents of the northeast quadrant of the county. The complaint alleged that under N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 5, the county was wasting public funds by not establishing special districts, and that the county resolution was an unconstitutional violation of the equal protection of the laws guaranteed by N.Y. Const. art. I, § 11 as well as the U.S. Const. amend. XIV to the United States Constitution. On appeal, the court found that the resolution and appropriation were a valid exercise of government power under N.Y. County Law § 226-b. The court reasoned that § 226-b had provided the authority to spend tax revenues for solid waste disposal that was not subject to attack under N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 5. Furthermore, no violation of a constitutional right had been alleged and the statute was rationally related to a legitimate governmental purpose.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court granting summary judgment in favor of the county was affirmed as a valid exercise of governmental authority.


HEADNOTES

Municipal Corporations -- Use of General County Funds to Finance Limited Access Landfill

1. The use of county-wide real property tax revenues to build and operate a sanitary landfill for the exclusive use of the residents of one quadrant of the county is authorized by section 226-b of the County Law, which authorizes a county to "appropriate and expend such sums as it may deem proper to provide for the collection and disposition of solid wastes in such county".

Municipal Corporations -- Use of General County Funds to Finance Limited Access Landfill

2. Section 226-b of the County Law, which authorizes a county to "appropriate and expend such sums as it may deem proper to provide for the collection and disposition of solid wastes in such county" does not contain, nor is it confined by, the limitations imposed upon a facility authorized only by article 5-A of the County Law, which can be funded only by the imposition of a special tax on the area that is in fact served by the facility, and, absent such limitations, there is no requirement that a county establish the special districts envisioned by that article in order to finance solid waste disposal facilities which are not county-wide.

Constitutional Law -- Validity of Legislative Actions

3. Legislative actions carry with them a strong presumption of validity and, where no cogent reason is produced for applying a strict scrutiny test, which is applicable where a fundamental right or suspect classification is involved, or for applying the middle tier scrutiny test developed in Alevy v Downstate Med. Center (39 NY2d 326), the traditional rational basis test is the appropriate standard to apply in determining whether the actions violate the equal protection guarantee.

 Municipal Corporations -- Use of General County Funds to Finance Limited Access Landfill

4. The activities of a county legislature, which, faced with a near crisis situation in one sector of the county, determined to take "emergency" action and construct a limited capacity, limited access landfill in that sector, using general county funds as authorized by section 226-b of the County Law, and which plans to construct similar facilities in the rest of the county, did not deny equal protection of the laws to a town outside that sector which was allegedly refused the use of county funds to finance the operations of its own town landfill.

 

358 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


In re Estate of Lalli


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


43 N.Y.2d 65; 371 N.E.2d 481; 400 N.Y.S.2d 761; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2437


October 13, 1977, Argued  
November 17, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Upon reargument: Prior determination of this court affirming the decree of the Surrogate's Court adhered to, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state supreme court affirmed the trial court's order granting respondent administratrix of the decedent's estate's motion to dismiss appellant child's application for a compulsory accounting on the ground that that the child was not a distributee pursuant to N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law § 4-1.2. The matter was before the court on remand from the U.S. Supreme Court for further consideration.


OVERVIEW: The child filed an application for a compulsory accounting. The administratrix of the decedent's estate filed a motion to dismiss the application on the ground that the child, who was born out of wedlock and who had been formally acknowledged by the decedent as his son, even though there was never any order of filiation, was not a distributee under N.Y. Est. Powers & Trusts Law § 4-1.2(a)(2). The trial court rejected the child's contention that § 4-1.2(a)(2) was unconstitutional. The state supreme court affirmed that decision. The U.S. Supreme Court remanded the case to the state supreme court for further consideration after deciding that an Illinois statute involving an illegitimate child's right to inherit was unconstitutional. On remand, the court held that the Illinois statute was distinguishable because there was a manifested and impermissible hostility to illegitimacy. According to the court, § 4-1.2(a)(2) served the legitimate state purpose of making provision for the orderly settlement of estates and the dependability of titles to property passing under intestacy laws. Thus, the court again affirmed the trial court's decision.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's decision granting the motion to dismiss filed by the administratrix of the decedent's estate.


HEADNOTES

Descent and Distribution -- Illegitimate Child -- Constitutional Law

1. The Illinois statutory scheme found unconstitutional in Trimble v Gordon (430 U.S. 762), wherein the right of an illegitimate child to inherit from his father depended not only on proof, by way of the father's acknowledgment, of the fact of paternity, but on proof as well that the parents had intermarried, differs significantly from the New York statute pertaining to inheritance by illegitimate persons ( EPTL 4-1.2, subd [a], par [2]), wherein the right of an illegitimate to inherit from his father depends only on proof that a court of competent jurisdiction has made an order of filiation declaring paternity during the lifetime of the father.

Children Born Out of Wedlock -- Order of Filiation -- Constitutional Law

2. A State may constitutionally require as proof of paternity a judicial determination made during the lifetime of the father.

Descent and Distribution -- Illegitimate Child -- Constitutional Law

3. Even in the absence of specific legislative materials as evidence of intent, a statute pertaining to the right of an illegitimate to inherit from his father, which has as its only prerequisite proof that a court of competent jurisdiction has made an order of filiation declaring paternity during the lifetime of the father, serves the legitimate State purpose of making provision for the orderly settlement of estates and the dependability of titles to property passing under intestacy laws. ( EPTL 4-1.2, subd [a], par [2].)

Descent and Distribution -- Illegitimate Child -- Constitutional Law

4. EPTL 4-1.2 (subd [a], par [2]), insofar as it provides that an illegitimate child is the legitimate child of his father if an order of filiation is issued in a proceeding brought during the lifetime of the father, meets the constitutional guidelines articulated in Trimble v Gordon (430 U.S. 762).

Comiskey v. Arlen


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


43 N.Y.2d 696; 372 N.E.2d 34; 401 N.Y.S.2d 200; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2506


October 11, 1977, Argued  
November 17, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs, in a memorandum. Question certified answered in the affirmative.


HEADNOTES

Physicians and Surgeons -- Malpractice -- Medical Mediation Panel -- Constitutionality

In a medical malpractice action where the case was referred to a medical malpractice mediation panel under section 148-a of the Judiciary Law, the Appellate Division reversed an order of Trial Term granting those branches of a motion by plaintiff to suppress the panel's recommendation and to declare section 148-a (subd 8) of the Judiciary Law unconstitutional, and denied those branches of the motion. The order of the Appellate Division is affirmed. The formal written recommendation of the panel should not be suppressed on pretrial application, as it was premature prior to trial, which might never take place and which if it does, might not give rise to considering the issue; moreover, issues of constitutionality should not be reached unnecessarily, since the issue might no longer matter if plaintiff recovers a judgment after trial.

 

360 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Davis


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


43 N.Y.2d 17; 371 N.E.2d 456; 400 N.Y.S.2d 735; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2434


September 7, 1977, Argued  
November 15, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: In People v Davis: Judgment modified and the case remitted to Supreme Court, Westchester County, for resentencing in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed.

In People v James: Judgment modified and the case remitted to Supreme Court, Kings County, for resentencing in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated cases, two defendants, both convicted of first degree murder, one by the Supreme Court, Westchester County (New York), and the other by the Supreme Court, Kings County (New York), appealed their convictions and challenged the constitutionality of the New York death penalty statute, New York Penal Laws §60.06.


OVERVIEW: One defendant shot and killed a police officer in civilian clothes on the way to work while defendant was robbing a convenience store. Another defendant shot and killed a corrections officer during an escape while incarcerated on another murder charge. Both defendants were convicted of first degree murder pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law §125.27. First defendant conviction was reversed on nonconstitutional grounds because it was not proved beyond a reasonable doubt that the police officer was in the line of duty. The N.Y. death penalty statute, N.Y. Penal Law §60.06, mandated the death sentence for killing a police officer in the line of duty without consideration of mitigating factors. The court held § 60.06 unconstitutional for that reason. It was essential that the capital sentencing decision allow for consideration of whatever mitigating circumstances may be relevant to either the particular offender or the particular offense. The second conviction was, therefore, reversed also. Both judgments were modified, vacating the death sentences, and the cases were remanded for resentencing as murder in the second degree.


OUTCOME: The judgments were modified by vacating the sentences of death and the cases remitted for resentencing and, as so modified, the judgments were affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- Identification of Defendant

1. Under the circumstances that a witness identified an exhibit as "supposed to be the photograph of the robber" and said that the photo and another were distorted from pictures he had seen on a viewing screen, that "[these] are not the guys I picked" and that "these are the photographs they handed me as being the robber here and the shooter here, and I disagreed * * * [because] of the facial features, that they're wrong", it cannot be said that the rejection of the exhibit was error.

Crimes -- Evidence -- Admissibility

2. A party's attempt to procure false testimony or to corrupt a witness, though collateral to the issues, is competent as an admission by acts and conduct that the party's case is weak and its evidence dishonest, and the fact that evidence was fabricated is admissible even though the evidence itself was not used.

District and Prosecuting Attorneys -- Prosecutorial Misconduct

3. There is no indication, much less proof, of falsification on the part of the police or misconduct such as bribery of a witness nor any "fastening" of such wrongdoing to the prosecution based upon a witness' testimony that a statement was taken from him under duress, where the signed statement was not offered or received in evidence.

Evidence -- Relevance

4. Relevant evidence is evidence having any tendency in reason to prove any material fact and tending to convince that the fact sought to be established is so.

Evidence -- Probative Value

5. Even if evidence is proximately relevant, it may be rejected if its probative value is outweighed by the danger that its admission would prolong the trial to an unreasonable extent without any corresponding advantage; or would confuse the main issue and mislead the jury; or unfairly surprise a party; or create substantial danger of undue prejudice to one of the parties.

Crimes -- Collateral Issues -- Prejudice

6. Since an attempt by the defense to introduce a witness' testimony that he was coerced to make a statement, not offered in evidence by the People, was for the obvious purpose of creating the impression that a substantial portion of the prosecution's proof was tainted in a fashion similar to the proffered account, the probative value of the testimony could be outweighed by dangers that the main issue would be obscured by prolongation of trial and by the solid possibility of undue prejudice to the prosecution, and, accordingly, the discretion of the trial court was not abused.

Crimes -- Evidence -- Newly Discovered Evidence

7. There is no merit to defendant's assertion of error because of the denial of a new trial, since the newly discovered evidence which formed the basis of the motion would be relevant only if one accepted the alibi testimony of defendant, his relatives and friends, which proof was not accepted by the jury, and since defense counsel was aware of the evidence during trial.

Crimes -- Murder -- Killing of Police Officer in Line of Duty

8. While the facts support a conviction of the crime of murder in the second degree, proof of guilt beyond a reasonable doubt as to all of the elements of the crime of murder in the first degree is not made out by a showing that defendant killed a policeman who asserted that he was an officer when restrained by defendant from leaving a supermarket on his way to report for duty, since there continues to be doubt concerning whether the victim was acting in the line of duty when he was killed, which is one of the elements of murder in the first degree as specified by the Legislature.

Crimes -- Murder -- Death Penalty

9. There is proof beyond a reasonable doubt of guilt of murder in the first degree of a defendant who, while attempting to escape custody, shot a corrections officer, and defendant, accordingly, comes within New York's death penalty statute which provides a mandatory death sentence for all persons over 18 years of age found to have intentionally caused the death of a police officer or a State or local corrections facility officer in the line of duty, where defendant knew or had reason to know the victim was such an officer or employee, or caused the death of anyone if defendant was confined or in custody for a life term or upon an indeterminate sentence with a maximum of life and a minimum of at least 15 years or if defendant had escaped from such confinement or custody and had not yet been returned.

Statutes -- Presumption of Constitutionality

10. State statutes imposing the death penalty for first degree murder (Penal Law, §§ 60.06, 125.27) carry with them a strong presumption of constitutionality and should be stricken as unconstitutional only as a last resort without substitution of the judgment of the courts for that of the Legislature as to the wisdom and expendiency of the legislation.

Courts -- Supreme Court Rulings

11. The court is bound by rulings of the United States Supreme Court as to the validity of State statutes under the United States Constitution.

 Constitutional Law -- Mandatory Death Penalty -- Circumstances

12. Unconstitutionality of New York's death penalty statute for murder of a corrections officer (Penal Law, §§ 60.06, 125.27, subd 1, par [a], cl [ii]) is indicated by the fact that the statute provides neither for the furnishing of information, without which there can be no consideration of the individual offender and the circumstances of a particular offense, nor standards to guide the sentencing authority in the use of that information had it been furnished.

Constitutional Law -- Mandatory Death Penalty -- Mitigating Factors

13. Since the New York death penalty statute for murder of a corrections officer (Penal Law, §§ 60.06, 125.27, subd 1, par [a], cl [ii]) "does not allow consideration of particularized mitigating factors" for purposes of "the capital sentencing decision" as to "the particular offender", it is unconstitutional.

Constitutional Law -- Mandatory Death Penalty -- Mitigating Factors

14. Although the statutory framework of New York's death penalty statute for murder of a corrections officer (Penal Law, §§ 60.06, 125.27, subd 1, par [a], cl [ii]) may in some manner reflect various mitigating factors as defenses, that is not enough to save New York's death penalty statute from constitutional infirmity, since similar defenses are found in other jurisdictions, including those whose death penalty statute has been held unconstitutional by the Supreme Court, and such limitations do not afford individualized consideration of the offender because defenses relate to guilt or innocence, whereas a mitigating factor may be of no significance to a determination of criminal culpability and will not rise to the level of a defense.

Crimes -- Mandatory Death Penalty -- Mitigating Factors

15. The defenses under New York's death penalty statute for murder of a corrections officer (Penal Law, §§ 60.06, 125.27, subd 1, par [a], cl [ii]) do not allow an individualized consideration of the character, propensity, record or attributes of the individual offender as required by the Supreme Court, since the defenses simply do not present the required information and do not permit a jury which has rejected these defenses and has found a defendant guilty of murder in the first degree to then mitigate the punishment by resurrecting the defenses.

Crimes -- Capital Cases -- Scope of Review

16. Under the State Constitution, in capital cases in which the sentence of death has been imposed, the Court of Appeals is vested with the power to and must review the facts (NY Const, art VI, §§ 3, 5) to determine their sufficiency to make out a case of murder beyond a reasonable doubt and determine that the evidence is of such weight and credibility that the jury was justified in finding the defendant guilty beyond a reasonable doubt.

Constitutional Law -- Mandatory Death Penalty -- Constitutionality

17. Under the United States Constitution, the death penalty is not per se unconstitutional.

 

361 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Tucker v. Toia


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


43 N.Y.2d 1; 371 N.E.2d 449; 400 N.Y.S.2d 728; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2432


October 5, 1977, Argued  
November 10, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant commissioner challenged a judgment from the Supreme Court in Monroe County (New York), which found in favor of respondent applicants in their action that challenged N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 158(a), amended by 1976 N.Y. Laws ch. 76, § 15. The law was determined to be unconstitutional and implementation and enforcement were enjoined.


OVERVIEW: The applicants were needy New York residents who were not living at home and were under age 21. They were not eligible for relief under the Aid to Families with Dependent Children Program, N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law §§343-362. N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law §158(a), amended by 1976 N.Y. Laws ch. 76, § 15 would not provide Home Relief Program, N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law §§ 157-166, services to applicants under age 21 and not living with a parent unless the applicant commenced a support proceeding against his parent and received an order of disposition. The applicants brought an action challenging N.Y. Soc. Serv. Law § 158(a) as unconstitutional. When the trial court entered judgment in favor of the applicants, the commissioner sought review. On appeal, the court affirmed and found that N.Y. Const. art. XVII, § 1, mandated that the aid, care, and support of the needy be provided by the State in a manner determined by the legislature. The court found that the applicants were unable to find their parents or it would have taken a long period of time to secure a disposition against their natural parents. The court noted that the legislature could not refuse aid to those who were classified as needy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court, which determined that a statute that denied aid to needy persons under age 21 was unconstitutional and enjoined its enforcement and implementation.


HEADNOTES

Social Services -- Home Relief -- Constitutionality of Eligibility Requirement

1. Section 15 of chapter 76 of the Laws of 1976, which amended subdivision (a) of section 158 of the Social Services Law to provide that assistance under the State's Home Relief Program is not to be provided to a person under the age of 21 who does not live with a parent or legally responsible relative, unless and until the applicant commences a support proceeding against any such parent or relative and an order of disposition is obtained in that support proceeding, constitutes a substantive violation of section 1 of article XVII of the New York State Constitution, which mandates the State to provide for the aid, care and support of the needy.

Constitutional Law -- Obligation of State to Aid Needy

2. Section 1 of article XVII of the New York State Constitution, which declares that the aid, care and support of the needy are public concerns and shall be provided by the State and by such of its subdivisions, and in such manner and by such means, as the Legislature may determine, imposes upon the State an affirmative duty to aid the needy, and, while it provides the Legislature with discretion in determining the means by which this objective is to be effectuated, in determining the amount of aid, and in classifying recipients and defining the term "needy", it unequivocally prevents the Legislature from simply refusing to aid those whom it has classified as needy.

Constitutional Law -- Denial of Aid to Needy

3. The Legislature may not constitutionally deny all aid to individuals who are admittedly needy on the basis of criteria having nothing to do with need.

 

362 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


New York Public Interest Research Group, Inc. v. Carey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


42 N.Y.2d 527; 369 N.E.2d 1155; 399 N.Y.S.2d 621; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2403


November 1, 1977, Argued  
November 2, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order insofar as appealed from reversed, without costs, and the case remitted to Supreme Court, Albany County, with directions to dismiss the complaint in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The trial court declared that 1977 N.Y. Laws ch. 455 was unconstitutional and awarded costs and attorney fees to respondent taxpayers. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York) modified the trial court's judgment by striking the award of attorney fees and otherwise affirmed. Appellant governor sought further review.


OVERVIEW: After the Board of Elections certified a proposition for the ballot, the taxpayers sought to have the proposed statute declared unconstitutional. The trial court found for the taxpayers, and the appellate court agreed that the statute was unconstitutional. On further review, the court reversed the appellate court's decision and remanded the case to the trial court with instructions to dismiss the action. The taxpayers had sought an advisory opinion, and no genuine controversy existed. Although the court might consider the validity of proposed legislation to resolve a dispute as to whether it should be placed on the ballot, the taxpayers had not demanded that the proposition be removed from the ballot. Judicial expression on the proposed statute would therefore be premature.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's decision and remanded the case to the trial court with instructions to dismiss the taxpayers' complaint.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- advisory opinions.

It would be improper for the courts to express any view regarding a challenge to the constitutionality of chapter 455 of the Laws of 1977 prior to the outcome of an impending referendum thereon. Although a declaratory judgment may be rendered if there is a genuine dispute prior to a breach of duty by a party, a determination would be premature and advisory if the contemplated future event is beyond the control of the parties and may not occur. Thus it has been held proper for the courts to consider the validity of proposed legislation when the immediate and practical effect is to determine whether the proposition will be submitted to the voters, but the challenged proposition herein is irrevocably on the ballot and plaintiffs are asking the court for an advisory opinion concerning the constitutionality of the statute in the event it is adopted. Accordingly, judicial intervention would be premature, and the complaint should be dismissed.

Jensen v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


42 N.Y.2d 1079; 369 N.E.2d 1179; 399 N.Y.S.2d 645; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2419


August 29, 1977, Argued

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city appealed a decision of the New York Supreme Court, Appellate Division, which granted plaintiff resident's motion for summary judgment in an action for a declaration that a resolution and map adopted by the New York City Board of Estimate were unconstitutional and for damages for the taking of her property.

OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's decision, but modified its order to the extent that, since there had been no actual taking of the resident's property, there were no compensable damages.
