Municipal corporations -- damage resulting from mapping of streets. 

 

364 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Wein v. Levitt


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


42 N.Y.2d 300; 366 N.E.2d 847; 397 N.Y.S.2d 758; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2208


June 9, 1977, Argued  
July 14, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed from a decision of the Supreme Court of New York at Special Term that granted summary judgment to defendant state comptroller, declaring that the provisions of chapter 893 of the Laws of 1975 did not constitute or create a debt of the state incurred without a referendum in violation of N.Y. Const. art. VII, § 11.


OVERVIEW: In plaintiff's declaratory judgment action, the lower court rendered summary judgment to defendant state comptroller, declaring that the provisions of chapter 893 of the Laws of 1975 did not constitute or create a debt of the state incurred without a referendum in violation of N.Y. Const. art. VII, § 11. Plaintiff appealed, arguing that a statutory obligation to indemnify state officers and employees for losses sustained as the result of liability arising out of their employment activities amounted to the kind of debt requiring a referendum. Upon review, the court affirmed, finding there was no indication that there would be any borrowing at all, or that there ever had been any borrowing for purposes of fulfilling existing indemnity statutes. Further, plaintiff made no showing that indicated that indemnity payments would not be a contingent cost of doing state business payable routinely out of the general fund, should the obligation arise. Consequently, there was no basis for the assertion that this indemnification was a debt within the meaning of N.Y. Const. art. VII, § 11.


OUTCOME: The decision of the lower court that rendered summary judgment to defendant state comptroller was affirmed because the provisions of the statute at issue did not constitute or create a debt of the state incurred without a referendum in violation of the New York Constitution.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- indemnification of investment officials by State.

1. The indemnity provision contained in section 87-c of the Workmen's Compensation Law, which directs the State Insurance Fund to invest in obligations of certain public benefit corporations, and provides that the State Insurance Fund and the State "shall save harmless and indemnify each and every state officer with responsibility for the custody or investment of such fund", is not violative of section 11 of article VII of the New York State Constitution, which prohibits the State from contracting a debt unless the debt shall be authorized for some single work or purpose by the people at a general election. That constitutional prohibition is concerned with debts such as those created by long-term borrowing and bond obligations, and not with debts which are necessary for the ongoing business of State government and are paid out of current appropriations in the State's general fund. Here, there is no borrowing for a long term, no indication that there would be any borrowing at all, or that there ever has been any borrowing for purposes of fulfilling indemnity statutes already on the books, and no indication that the indemnity payments would not be a contingent cost of doing State business payable routinely out of the general fund, should the obligation arise. Consequently, there is no basis for the assertion that this indemnification is a debt within the meaning of section 11 of article VII.

2. In this action challenging the constitutionality of the indemnity provision, the sole issue raised and considered below was whether it created a legal debt of the State in violation of section 11 of article VII of the State Constitution. The issue of whether it creates, in effect, a loan of the State's credit to the public corporations involved, in violation of section 8 of article VII of the State Constitution, should not be entertained or passed upon. However, this indemnification of State officials is not even colorably a loan to a public corporation, and does not run afoul of the section 8 prohibition.

 

365 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Moritt v. Governor of New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


42 N.Y.2d 347; 366 N.E.2d 1285; 397 N.Y.S.2d 929; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2212


June 1, 1977, Argued  
July 7, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner candidate brought a proceeding pursuant to N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 challenging the constitutionality of N.Y. Elec. Law §§ 131(2), 136(5). He appealed from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which reversed a judgment directing respondent Governor of the State of New York (governor) to convene the legislature for the purpose of enacting election laws not repugnant to the Constitutions of the United States.


OVERVIEW: The candidate claimed that his unsuccessful candidacies for nomination for state-wide office were stymied by N.Y. Elec. Law §§ 131(2), 136(5), which he alleged were unconstitutional. There were 39 congressional districts in New York State, and N.Y. Elec. Law § 136(5) required a candidate to have 100 signatures from each of one-half of those districts. The trial court granted the candidate's petition, directing the governor to convene the legislature at special session to enacting election laws not repugnant to the United States Constitution and declared N.Y. Elec. Law §§ 131(2), 136(5) unconstitutional. The lower court reversed and granted the governor's cross-motion to dismiss. The court affirmed. There was an important state interest in requiring some preliminary showing of a significant modicum of support before printing the name of a political organization's candidate on a ballot. The states were allowed to fix reasonable standards to assure that a candidate had some level of popular support. N.Y. Elec. Law 136(5) did not impose an unconstitutionally onerous burden on a democratic party candidate, and N.Y. Elec. Law § 131(2) did not violate the one man-one vote principle.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, which granted the governor's cross-motion to dismiss the candidate's petition to declare provisions of the New York Election Law unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- nomination of candidates for State-wide offices.

1. Subdivision 5 of section 136 of the Election Law, which provides that petitions for State-wide offices must be signed by not less than 20,000 or 5%, whichever is less, of the then enrolled voters of a party, of whom not less than 100 or 5%, whichever is less, shall reside in each of one half of the Congressional districts of the State, is constitutional. While 2,000 of the required signatures must be distributed geographically among 20 of the 39 Congressional districts, the remaining 18,000 may come from one district or any combination of districts and that is not the type of restriction on access to the primary ballot which is invidiously discriminatory and violative of the equal protection clause.

2. Although, because the number of enrolled party members varies widely from one Congressional district to another, the strict application of the one man-one vote principle would strike down subdivision 5 of section 136 of the Election Law, that principle is not to be applied mechanically or automatically. Accordingly, because sound reasons support the exaction of a broad geographical representation and because of the near practical impossibility of implementing a precise one man-one vote rule, the statute is not violative of that rule.

3. Consideration of the challenge under the Federal Constitution to subdivision 2 of section 131 of the Election Law, which provides for the selection of State-wide candidates by the State committee of a political party, is precluded by res judicata (see Moritt v Rockefeller, 346 F Supp 34, affd 409 US 1020). However, on the merits, that statute does not violate the one man-one vote principle or the New York State Constitution.

 Moritt v Governor of State of N. Y., 53 AD2d 857, affirmed.

 

366 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Penn Cent. Transp. Co. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


42 N.Y.2d 324; 366 N.E.2d 1271; 397 N.Y.S.2d 914; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2210; 10 ERC (BNA) 1389; 7 ELR 20579


April 27, 1977, Argued  
June 23, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs transportation company, holders of fee interest in train station, and lessee of development rights in the station challenged the decision of the Appellate Division (New York), which reversed the trial court's order enjoining defendant city from enforcing landmark preservation provisions of the city administrative code by denying the company's applications to erect a substantial office building on top of the station.


OVERVIEW: The transportation company and lessee sought judicial invalidation of the administrative code landmark preservation provisions as applied to the station, and also sought damages for a temporary taking of property from the time of original designation as a landmark to the time of the requested judicial invalidation. The court maintained that a plaintiff seeking to show that an otherwise reasonable land use regulation constituted a deprivation of due process of law was required to demonstrate affirmatively that the regulation eliminated all reasonable return. The court held that the company and lessee failed to meet their burden as none of their analyses included the benefits provided to the station's varied real estate holdings by the terminal's operation. The court noted that development rights above the station were made transferable, and could be transferred to several sites owned by the station that were suitable for office building construction. The court found that the substitute rights were valuable, and provided significant and fair, compensation for the loss of rights above the terminal itself. Therefore the court held that no constitutional violation was established.


OUTCOME: The order of the appellate division was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- preservation of landmarks.

1. The landmark preservation provisions of the Administrative Code of the City of New York, as applied to Grand Central Terminal to prohibit the building of an office tower over the existing building, do not violate the due process clause of the Constitution since the plaintiff property owners are not deprived of all reasonable return. Unlike zoning and historic district regulation, in which the burdened owners benefit to some extent, and, unlike a taking in eminent domain, in which the owner is paid, landmark regulation imposes upon a single owner a burden from which there may be no benefit other than to that owner's neighbors, and the landowner must be allowed a reasonable return. There is no constitutional imperative that the return embrace all factors derived from the social complex in which the property rests, and, in view of the massive social and governmental investment afforded a favored private enterprise such as a public utility, it is enough that the privately created ingredient receive a reasonable return under a landmarking statute, since the remainder is society's contribution. The railroads have been given monopolies for their rights of way, grants for their land, assistance for grade crossing eliminations, franchises to use city streets without charge, and real estate tax exemptions, while the terminal has been made the center of the city transit system. Absent such heavy governmental investment in the terminal and connecting transportation, the terminal would be worth a fraction of its current value.

2. In computing the reasonable return upon the privately created component of the terminal's value, the capability of the property to yield a reasonable return if efficiently managed is of greater significance than its present return, since the latter criterion could allow the inadequacy of the managers of property to frustrate land use regulations.

3. It is not necessary that the property be operated at a profit, since some portion of the value of the owner's more profitable real estate holdings surrounding the terminal must be attributed to the terminal itself.

4. The plaintiffs have not been wholly deprived of the development rights above the terminal, since those rights are transferable and may be divided among other parcels of land in the vicinity, some owned by plaintiffs, and the right to transfer is of value notwithstanding that some of the parcels are encumbered by long-term leases or are presently improved with suitable buildings.

5. Plaintiffs have failed to meet their burden of showing that the regulation eliminates all reasonable return, and they are not entitled to be relieved of its restrictions.

 

367 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Easley


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


42 N.Y.2d 50; 364 N.E.2d 1328; 396 N.Y.S.2d 635; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2098


May 3, 1977, Argued  
June 16, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which entered a verdict convicting defendant of rape in the third degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had sexual intercourse with a mentally retarded woman who was older than 17. He was convicted of rape in the third degree. On appeal, the court affirmed his conviction. The court concluded that, by the standards the charge articulated, there was sufficient proof on the basis of which the jury had a right to find, as it did, that the victim was a mentally defective person under N.Y. Penal Law § 130.25(1) and 130.25(3)(b) beyond a reasonable doubt. N.Y. Penal Law § 130.10 was not unconstitutional when it placed the burden on defendant to prove that he was unaware of the victim's mental defect at the time he committed the sex act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for rape in the third degree.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- rape in third degree -- capacity to consent to sexual intercourse.

1. In a prosecution of defendant for rape in the third degree based upon his sexual intercourse with a woman incapable of consenting to the act by reason of mental defect, the Trial Judge did not err in charging the jury that the ability to consent to sexual intercourse requires "the exercise of intelligence based upon knowledge of its significance and moral quality". The Penal Law ( § 130.00, subd 5) defines a mentally defective person as "incapable of appraising the nature of his conduct". Such appraisal is qualitative, encompassing more than a knowledge of the physiological nature of sexual intercourse and including an appreciation of the prevailing societal environment to which the person is exposed. Accordingly, the moral quality of the act, as distinguished from the participating woman's personal sense of morality, should not be ignored. The Trial Judge properly requested the jury to consider the capacity to appreciate the moral quality of the act in conjunction with her knowledge of the significance and possible consequences thereof, without suggesting that the determination could turn on an appreciation of the moral quality of the act alone or upon a personal sense of values.

2. There was sufficient proof from which the jury could find that defendant had intercourse with a mentally defective person. Two psychological tests indicated that the woman was moderately retarded and the testimony of a school psychologist, as confirmed by the woman's grandmother, indicated that her understanding was limited to concrete rather than abstract matters, such that she was incapable of comprehending the consequences of sexual intercourse.

3. The court may not review the defendant's contention that the affirmative defense of lack of knowledge of incapacity unconstitutionally places on defendant the burden of proof, since defendant indicated at trial that no such defense would be made.

 

368 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Donawitz v. Danek


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


42 N.Y.2d 138; 366 N.E.2d 253; 397 N.Y.S.2d 592; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2163


March 30, 1977, Argued  
June 14, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs, and the motion for the order of attachment denied. Question certified answered in the negative.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff non-resident infant filed an action against defendant hospital for negligence and defendant physicians, one of whom was a non-resident, for malpractice. The trial court granted the infant's motion directing the attachment of the non-resident physician's liability insurer's contractual obligation to defend and indemnify him. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York) affirmed.


OVERVIEW: The infant, a resident of New Jersey, sustained a leg fracture in a skiing accident in Pennsylvania. She was treated in the emergency room of a hospital in New York by a resident physician. Two days later, the infant was treated by another physician who was a resident of New Jersey. The lower court extended the Seider-Simpson doctrine in extending jurisdiction to New York over the other defendants in the case. The lower court reasoned it would be inconvenient to the infant in having to prosecute multiple actions if jurisdiction as to the New Jersey physician was lacking. Upon review, the court held that a court should not depart from its prior holdings unless impelled by the most cogent freasons and could not fail to take into account considerations of the mandates of stare decisis. As such, the court held that the doctrine of Seider-Simpson should not be extended here.


OUTCOME: The order reversed, with costs, and the motion for the order of attachment was denied. The certified question was answered in the negative.


HEADNOTES

Attachment -- attachment of Insurance policy.

1. In an action by a resident of New Jersey against a physician who practices medicine in New Jersey to recover for injuries sustained in a skiing accident in Pennsylvania, a motion to permit attachment of defendant's medical malpractice insurance policy, the carrier being licensed to transact business in New York, should be denied. The doctrine explicated in Seider v Roth (17 NY2d 111) and Simpson v Loehmann (21 NY2d 305), allowing attachment of a defendant's liability insurer's obligation to defend and indemnify, should not be extended to a case involving a nonresident plaintiff and a nonresident defendant arising out of alleged acts in another State, but should be confined to the limits of stare decisis. Considerations of institutional stability and the mandates of stare decisis should be taken into account. A court should not depart from its prior holdings unless impelled by the most cogent reasons.

2. Although stare decisis dictates that the cited precedents not be overruled, it does not require expansion of the scope of the doctrine. While the insurer's duty to defend and indemnity has been found to be an attachable debt where the plaintiff is a resident, that special type of contract duty is not of suffient substance to support quasi in rem jurisdiction where the plaintiff is a nonresident.

Dworsky v. Farano


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


41 N.Y.2d 780; 364 N.E.2d 827; 396 N.Y.S.2d 163; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2054


June 6, 1977, Argued  
June 9, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, without costs, and section 2.00 of the Troy City Charter declared to be constitutional.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city judge sought review of a decision from the Supreme Court at Special Term of Rensselaer County (New York), which found a section of a city charter to be unconstitutional and which enjoined the city judge from casting a tie breaking vote to replace a deceased member of the city council pursuant to the city charter.


OVERVIEW: The mayor of a city died creating a vacancy in the city council. The city's charter provided that in such cases the city council was to select a replacement and the city judge was to cast the tie breaking vote should it be necessary. A group of individuals challenged the validity of that provision of the city charter and obtained a temporary restraining order prohibiting the city judge from exercising the authority conferred upon him by the provision at issue. The trial court found the provision to be unconstitutional. The city judge appealed. The court determined that the provision was not unconstitutional because the state constitution empowered local governments to appoint other officers of local government. Although N.Y. Const. art. VI, § 20 precluded certain judges from holding any other public office or trust, no such restrictions were placed on city judges. The provision at issue was not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order which found the provision of the city's charter at issue unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- charter provision giving City Court Judge power to resolve deadlock in city council.

Section 2.00 of the Troy City Charter, which provides that, in the event of the death of any city councilman, the vacancy thereby created shall be filled by the vote of the remaining members of the council and, in the event of a tie vote, the City Judge of the City of Troy shall cast the deciding vote, is constitutional. Article IX (§ 1, subd [b]) of the New York State Constitution vests local government with the right to confer upon officers thereof the authority to appoint other officers of local government, and the City Judge, being an officer of local government, thus may be given the power provided for in the Constitution. That constitutional provision is not abrogated or superseded by article VI (§ 20) of the Constitution, prohibiting certain judicial officers from holding any other public office or trust, since the prohibition does not apply to City Court Judges outside of the City of New York. Nor is there an attempt here to transfer the City Court Judge into an investigative or administrative arm of the executive or thrust upon him burdensome duties which would interfere with the performance of his judicial functions under applicable statutes, or would violate any constitutional prohibition as with Judges of higher courts.

371 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Board of Education v. Licata


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


42 N.Y.2d 815; 364 N.E.2d 1337; 396 N.Y.S.2d 644; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2102


March 30, 1977, Argued  
May 12, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed and the order of Supreme Court, Niagara County, reinstated, with costs to abide the event in a memorandum.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant teacher sought review of an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which reversed an order of the lower court and granted a summary judgment to plaintiff board of education, which had filed a declaratory action pursuant to N.Y. Mil. Law § 242 against the teacher who was in the military and intended to take military leave during the school year.


OVERVIEW: The board of education sought a declaration as to a teacher's right to take military leave during the school year when he had requested the military orders despite a board regulation providing that a teacher who was an active member of the reserves would be granted time off for reserve duty if it could not be arranged during the period that the schools were closed. The trial court granted a summary judgment for the teacher. The appellate court reversed the order. On appeal, the court reversed the appellate court's order and reinstated the trial court's order. The court held that N.Y. Mil. Law § 242 did not preclude a public employer from adopting reasonable regulations to coordinate requirements of the military and that the board regulation did not conflict with § 242. The court concluded that, in the absence of any showing that the teacher contrived to arrange for the fixing of dates for his military duty at a time inconvenient to the needs of his employer, he did not violate the regulation, and that evidence of the teacher's practice of requesting June military duty was not sufficient to demonstrate as a matter of law that he had or had not violated the regulation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the appellate court and reinstated the order of the trial court, which granted the teacher a summary judgment in the suit filed by the board of education seeking a declaration as to the teacher's right to take military leave during the school year.


HEADNOTES

 Schools -- teachers -- military leaves. 

 

372 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Patterson v. Carey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


41 N.Y.2d 714; 363 N.E.2d 1146; 395 N.Y.S.2d 411; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2027


February 17, 1977, Argued  
May 10, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order modified, with costs to plaintiffs, in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Parkway Authority filed an action against defendant Governor and Attorney General that sought a judgment to declare N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 153-c unconstitutional. The Parkway Authority's summary judgment motion was granted to rescind the current toll increase, but denied as to future toll increases. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department affirmed. The parties cross-appealed.


OVERVIEW: When the Parkway Authority instituted an increase in the toll charged to motorists for use of the Southern State Parkway, the Legislature enacted § 153-c to rescind the increase and allow future increases only upon compliance with a review process by the State Comptroller. The court held that the statute was unconstitutional in its entirety. It violated the due process clause of N.Y. Const., art. I, § 6 because it deprived the parkway's bondholders of property by jeopardizing their investment without due process of law. The statute also violated the Contracts Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, because it impinged on the State's own contract with the bondholders. Although the statute was enacted to relieve a traffic emergency caused by the increased toll, this was not a sufficiently important public purpose to warrant interference with contractual rights and there was no evidence that it would correct the traffic problems at all. Finally, the statute impermissibly infringed upon the State Comptroller's discretionary power to supervise the financial accounts of public authorities as set out in N.Y. Const., art. X, § 5.


OUTCOME: The court modified the order of the Appellate Division to declare the statute unconstitutional in its entirety.


HEADNOTES

Public authorities -- State reduction of highway toll rate -- due process -- impairment of contractual rights -- supervision of accounts by State Comptroller.

1. Section 153-c of the Public Authorities Law, which rescinds an increase imposed by the Jones Beach State Parkway Authority in the toll charged motorists for the use of the Southern State Parkway, violates the due process clause of the State Constitution. The authority is a public benefit corporation entrusted with the reconstruction, maintenance and improvement of the Parkway, which was financed by bonds secured by toll receipts. In conjunction with the bond issue, the State pledged not to limit or alter the rights of the authority to the detriment of bondholders, and the authority was empowered to charge tolls, and to discretionarily impose increases (Public Authorities Law, §§ 153-a, 153-b). The provisions of section 153-c, rescinding an increase in tolls intended to cover capital expenses and reduced revenues and providing that tolls could be raised only if a report is made to the State Comptroller 120 days prior to a proposed increase, deprives the bondholders of their property, since the toll is the sole source of funds for bond repayment and, therefore, an essential attribute of their contract with the authority. The deprivation of contractual rights was achieved without due process of law, since there was no reasonable relation to the public health, comfort, welfare and, particularly, to the asserted goal of reducing traffic congestion on local streets occasioned by motorists dodging the toll booths. The statute would not have affected toll dodging, since the increase could be instituted after 120 days without any program to prevent toll dodging.

2. Section 153-c of the Public Authorities Law violates the contract clause of the Federal Constitution. In its covenant with holders of authority bonds, the State pledged to vest the authority with power to raise tolls in its sole discretion if revenues became insufficient, and, since tolls are the sole source of funds for bond repayment, the provision was important. It is not necessary that the impairment totally destroy the obligation in order to violate the clause, but the extent of impairment is relevant. Although the contract clause is not an absolute bar to modification of State contractual obligations where there is an appropriate exercise of police power, complete deference to a legislative assessment of reasonableness and necessity is inappropriate, since the State's self-interest is at stake. The asserted traffic emergency was not a sufficiently important public purpose to warrant interference with contractual rights, since there is no indication of extreme difficulties of ingress and egress to the affected areas and no means provided by the statute to achieve the public purpose.

3. The State Comptroller's discretionary power to supervise the financial accounts of public authorities is infringed by the requirements of section 153-c of the Public Authorities Law that he review proposed toll increases and make public his findings. Article X of the State Constitution provides that the accounts of public corporations are subject to supervision by the Comptroller and, unlike article V of the Constitution regarding supervision of the accounts of political subdivisions, article X gives the Legislature no power to control the Comptroller's exercise of discretion. Section 153-c impermissibly purports to mandate that the Comptroller exercise his supervision over a particular topic in a particular manner and imposes the duty to conduct a study and review not merely of accounts but of administrative and operational matters in the management of the authority.

4. Accordingly, a section 153-c of the Public Authorites Law should be declared unconstitutional in its entirety.

Zimmerman v. Murray


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York

374 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Johnson v. Director, Downstate Medical Center, State University of New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


41 N.Y.2d 1061; 364 N.E.2d 837; 396 N.Y.S.2d 172; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 2060


March 29, 1977, Argued  
May 3, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent employer appealed an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed a judgment dismissing petitioner employee's motion and directing the employee's reinstatement to his civil service position.


OVERVIEW: The prior court reversed the dismissal of an employee's petition for a judgment directing the employee's reinstatement to his civil service position. The employer appealed, contending that its termination was authorized by N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 4, § 5.3(d), which provided that when an employee was absent without leave and without an explanation for a period of 10 work days, the absence was deemed to constitute a resignation. The court noted that the provision offered the employer a means for summary, involuntary termination of employment based on nonperformance of duty and that, to the extent that its application resulted in a discharge of the employee, it was parallel to N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 75. However, as it occurred, the employer was not required to give any notice to the employee before invoking the rule, which was contrary to § 75. Upon appeal, the court affirmed the prior court's order and held that the employer's termination as it occurred was invalid because it resorted to use of a statute that did not require that the employee was to be given notice of the termination prior to having invoked the rule.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the prior court that granted the employee's petition directing that his position was to be reinstated. The court held that the employer's resort to a rule that allowed for summary, involuntary termination of civil employment based on the employee's absence was unconstitutionally applied without having given the employee notice.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- termination of employment -- unexplained absence without leave. 

 

375 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Quirk v. Municipal Assistance Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


41 N.Y.2d 644; 363 N.E.2d 549; 394 N.Y.S.2d 842; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 1935


March 31, 1977, Argued  
April 26, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff city bondholders sought review of a decision from Supreme Court of Kings County and the Supreme Court of New York County (New York), which granted defendant, Municipal Assistance Corporation for the City of New York's, motion for summary judgment that declared 1975 N.Y. Laws ch. 168, 169 valid and constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The bondholders asserted that the diversion of the proceeds of the city sales tax and the tax on stock transfers from the general revenues of the city to the city corporation unconstitutionally impaired their rights. The trial courts granted the municipal corporation's motion for summary judgment. On appeal, the court sustained the summary judgments. The court stated that U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, was not violated because there was never an agreement that the revenues of those taxes were to be used to repay city bonds thus, there was no impairment of obligation of contract under § 10. The court stated that in no way was the city ever committed to maintain sales tax revenues or stock transfer tax revenues for the benefit of its bondholders. The bondholders were protected by N.Y. Const. art. VII, §§ 2, 10, 12, which obligated the city to appropriate moneys for repayment of city bonds.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of the city's motion for summary judgment.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- New York State Municipal Assistance Corporation Act -- diversion of tax revenues from New York City.

1. The "diversion" by the State of the proceeds of the New York City sales tax and the stock transfer tax from the general revenues of the city to the Municipal Assistance Corporation is constitutional (US Const, art I, § 10). There is no requirement in the State Constitution or in any undertaking in the city's long-term bonds that such taxes continue to be paid directly to the city. There is no agreement, express or implied, that the revenues of those taxes be used to repay city bonds. Accordingly, such diversion of revenues does not result in the impairment of any contractual rights of the holders of the city's long-term bonds.

2. The city bondholders are protected by the guarantees of the State Constitution which obligate the city to appropriate moneys for the repayment of bonds and to exceed normal real estate tax limitations to raise the necessary moneys (NY Const, art VIII, §§ 2, 10, 12). The "first lien" on the city's revenues (art VIII, § 2) does not give bondholders the right to insist that any particular existing tax be maintained or new ones imposed to protect those revenues, but only assures that, out of the revenues collected, money must be provided to satisfy obligations to bondholders.

 

376 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Clark


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


41 N.Y.2d 612; 363 N.E.2d 319; 394 N.Y.S.2d 593; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 1970


February 11, 1977, Argued  
April 7, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal defendant appealed an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a judgment of the Nassau County Court, rendered upon a verdict convicting defendant of robbery in the first degree.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was indicted for a robbery in which a coin collection was stolen in Nassau County. Police in New York City were investigating the sale of stolen property in the city and, while out on bond, defendant contacted an undercover officer in attempt to fence the stolen coins. After meeting with defendant, the officers received a call regarding the coins from an unknown third person, who put defendant on the telephone. A second conversation was recorded on tape. During this operation, neither of the officers had any knowledge of the outstanding indictment against defendant. Following a trial by jury, defendant was convicted of robbery, and he sought a reversal on the ground that a taped telephone conversation was introduced into evidence in violation of his right to counsel. The intermediate court upheld the conviction, and the court affirmed. The court held that incriminating statements made to the police in a noncustodial separable setting were admissible, despite the attachment of counsel, if the statements were elicited in the course of a good faith police investigation.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction by the trial court for robbery, affirmed by the appellate division, was upheld because his right to counsel was not violated by introduction into evidence of a telephone conversation taped by police pursuing a good faith investigation.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- confession.

1. In a trial for robbery in the first degree, the trial court properly admitted into evidence tapes of conversations between defendant and two undercover police officers in which defendant attempted to dispose of the stolen property, which was the subject of the charge, while he was free on bail after indictment and arraignment on the robbery charge. The officers were independently and in good faith investigating criminal activity relating to the unlawful possession of stolen property. The rule that incriminating statements taken from a defendant after a lawyer has entered the proceeding are inadmissible if procured in the absence of counsel or a waiver, with such waiver to be made only in the presence of counsel if a lawyer has entered the proceeding, is not absolute. Representation by counsel in a proceeding unrelated to the investigation is insufficient to invoke the protections, and voluntary, spontaneous statements are outside the purview of the rule. Incriminating statements made to police in a noncustodial separable setting are admissible despite the attachment of counsel if elicited in the course of a good faith police investigation. The rule is aimed at protecting defendant from deliberate efforts to elicit incriminating statements in disregard of constitutional protections, and there is no purpose in applying it to a good faith investigation.

2. Defendant did not properly preserve for review his claim that he was not given adequate notice pursuant to CPL 710.30 that statements made to a public servant were to be used. Defendant objected on other grounds and was three times overruled and did not further object until after the recorded statements were introduced and a police officer was called to testify about the conversations.

3. Defendant does not present sufficient reason for the court to disturb decisions upholding the constitutionality of the affirmative defense to robbery in the first degree under subdivision 4 of section 160.15 of the Penal Law that a firearm used could not discharge a shot capable of producing death or serious physical injury.

 

377 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Anonymous Attorneys v. Bar Asso. of Erie County


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


41 N.Y.2d 506; 362 N.E.2d 592; 393 N.Y.S.2d 961; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 1912


February 15, 1977, Argued  
April 5, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, without costs. Question certified answered in the affirmative.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorneys challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), denying their motions to dismiss disciplinary proceedings brought against them by respondent, Bar Association of Erie County, and certifying a question on appeal whether incriminating testimony given by an attorney, following a grant of immunity, may be used as evidence against him in a disciplinary proceeding.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, attorneys admitted to practice in New York, were called to testify before a grand jury investigating alleged fixing of traffic tickets. The grand jury then voted them full immunity pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 50.10. The grand jury probe resulted in indictment against certain officials and, subsequently, appellants testified at the trial of these officials. Thereafter, they were served with a petition instituted by respondent bar association seeking to have them disciplined for their involvement in the ticket fixing. Appellants moved to dismiss on the ground that they had been granted immunity from any penalties or forfeitures arising out of the transactions concerning which they had testified. Motion was denied, and they were granted leave to appeal on a certified question of whether incriminating testimony given by an attorney, following a grant of immunity, may be used as evidence against him in a disciplinary proceeding. The court affirmed the order and answered the certified question in the affirmative, holding that the possible sanctions flowing from the disciplinary proceeding did not constitute a penalty or forfeiture within the meaning of § 50.10.


OUTCOME: The court answered the certified question in the affirmative and affirmed the intermediate court's order denying dismissal because incriminating testimony given by appellant attorneys, following grants of immunity, could be used as evidence against them by respondent bar association in attorney disciplinary proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Attorney and client -- disciplinary proceedings -- immunity from prosecution.

1. The possible sanctions flowing from a disciplinary proceeding against an attorney do not constitute a "penalty or forfeiture" within the meaning of CPL 50.10 and, accordingly, a grant of immunity thereunder, while protecting appellant attorneys from use of their Grand Jury testimony in any criminal proceeding, is ineffective to bar a disciplinary proceeding based upon that testimony. CPL 50.10 encompassess penalties imposed upon conviction for a criminal offense committed in violation of State statute and does not protect against all private consequences of facts revealed by testimony given under its shelter. Disciplinary proceedings are civil in nature and serve to protect the court and society and are not criminal proceedings.

2. While a grant of immunity under CPL 50.10 must be coextensive with the privilege against self incrimination which it replaces, that privilege provides only that no person shall be compelled to be a witness against himself in any criminal proceeding. Since the privilege does not extend to use in noncriminal proceedings, immunity does not protect against such use where the testimony cannot be later used in a criminal proceeding.

 

378 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Board of Education v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


41 N.Y.2d 535; 362 N.E.2d 948; 394 N.Y.S.2d 148; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 1961


February 8, 1977, Argued  
April 5, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff school board brought a N.Y.C.P.L.R. art. 78 petition to compel defendant city to make appropriations to the school board as required by N.Y. Educ. Law § 2576. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) affirmed the trial court's decision that the override of the governor's veto by the senate was a nullity because it was a second reconsideration not permitted under the constitution.


OVERVIEW: The state senate had originally failed to override the governor's veto on legislation concerning funding of city school. Subsequently the senate reconsidered and overrode the veto. The court determined that it had the authority to determine the effectiveness of legislative action of the senate. The court found that the constitutional mandate of "reconsideration" in N.Y. Const. art. IV, § 7 was that the legislature could address the vetoed bill a second time. It was apparent, according to the court, that the use of the verb "reconsider" was not intended to refer to the familiar parliamentary procedure, as embodied in Senate Rule V, §8, was not the same as to reconsider in parliamentary usage. Thus, the court found that there was nothing to suggest that the senate be restricted to a single vote in a truncated process quite foreign to normal parliamentary procedure. The court found that the legislation did not rule afoul of the home rule provisions of the N.Y. Const. art. IX, §3(a)(1) because education was expressly made a state responsibility under N.Y. Const. art. XI, § 1. The court converted the article 78 proceeding into an action for a declaratory judgment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision, with costs, and remitted to the trial court for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

State -- legislative override of Governor's veto -- reconsideration of matter before Legislature.

1. Chapter 132 of the Laws of 1976 was duly enacted over the veto of the Governor. The veto was overridden in the Assembly and, following an initial failure to secure sufficient votes to override in the Senate, a motion to reconsider passed and a second vote was successful. Section 7 of article IV of the State Constitution provides that a veto may be overridden after both legislative houses "proceed to reconsider" the bill and pass it by a two-thirds majority. The verb "reconsider" as used therein refers to a new evaluation of a bill nullified by a veto, constituting a discrete procedure from the original enactment and does not refer to the parliamentary procedure by which an action previously effected is again taken up. The vetoed bill thus came before the Senate as any other motion introducing an item of substantive legislative business. Section 9 of article III of the Constitution vests each house with the power to determine the rules of its own proceedings and, pursuant thereto, the Senate has ruled that all substantive questions addressed during the 1976 session were a subject to a single motion to reconsider. Nothing in the Constitution forecloses recourse to normal parliamentary procedures upon return of a vetoed bill, and, accordingly, the initial legislative address after return of the bill included the possibility of a motion to reconsider.

2. The judiciary may properly inquire into the legality of the internal procedures by which chapter 132 of the Laws of 1976 was enacted, although the courts will generally not interfere with internal legislative procedures, since judicial review may be undertaken to determine if the Legislature has complied with constitutional prescriptions as to its procedures.

3. Chapter 132 of the Laws of 1976, requiring the City of New York to annually appropriate for public elementary and secondary education an amount equal to the average proportion of the total city expense budget, as amended, which was appropriated for school purposes over the previous three years, does not violate the home rule provisions of the State Constitution. Education is explicitly exempt from home rule restrictions (NY Const, art XI, § 1) and, as a matter of State concern, may be the subject of State legislation even though of localized application and effect.

4. While the appropriation required by the statute is based in part on the preceding year's budget, as amended, and that budget remains subject to amendment at the time the figures are required to be submitted, any ambiguity may be resolved and the statute may be implemented by utilizing the city's practice of relying on amendments through March 15. This construction is reinforced by the attempted amendment of the statute to require use of that date.

5. The failing of the Board of Education to file its estimated budget on the date required by law does not preclude application of chapter 132 of the Laws of 1976, since the practice has been to file late and, with respect to the fiscal year in question, timely filing would have been an impossibility, since the law was enacted seven and one-half months after the filing date. Any contrary construction would nullify the effective date of the statute as expressly fixed by the Legislature.

6. It would be inappropriate to grant relief by way of mandamus, since the Emergency Financial Control Board is not a party to this proceeding and has not waived any right it might have to object to any appropriation made and since the relationship between chapter 132 of the Laws of 1976 and the Financial Emergency Act (L 1975, ch 868) is expressly not now reached. Accordingly, the action is converted to one for a declaratory judgment.

 

379 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Wambat Realty Corp. v. State


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


41 N.Y.2d 490; 362 N.E.2d 581; 393 N.Y.S.2d 949; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 1910; 7 ELR 20363


February 9, 1977, Argued  
March 31, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff real property owner brought an action against defendant state to challenge the Adirondack Park Agency Act, Executive Law, art. 27 as violative of the home rule protection afforded local governments in N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 3. The Supreme Court at Special Term entered a judgment declaring the Act valid. The real property owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The real property owner brought an action against the state to challenge the Adirondack Park Agency Act, Executive Law, art. 27 as violative of the home rule protection afforded local governments in N.Y. Const. art. IX. The trial court entered a judgment declaring the Act valid. On appeal, the property owner contended that the Act, which set forth a comprehensive zoning and planning program for all the public and private lands within the park, unconstitutionally deprived the town of its zoning and planning powers. The court held (1) that a proper concern of the state might also touch upon local concerns did not mean that the state might not freely legislate with respect to such concerns, (2) the Act related to the life and health of the people of the state, (3) neither the Constitution nor a home rule statute was designed to disable the state from responding to problems of significant state concern.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment declaring the state statute valid.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- home rule -- Adirondack Park Agency Act.

The comprehensive zoning and planning legislation of the Adirondack Park Agency Act (Executive Law, art 27), enacted at a single session of the Legislature, is not invalid because it encroaches upon the zoning and planning powers of local governments and there is no violation of the home rule provisions of the State Constitution and the Statute of Local Governments for failure of the Legislature to re-enact the statute in a second legislative session. The future of the Adirondack Park region is not a matter of purely local concern and to categorize it as such would alter substantially the meaning of matters "other than the property, affairs or government of a local government", power over which is expressly reserved to the State (NY Const, art IX, § 3, subd [a], par [3]). In matters of State concern the Legislature may supersede the powers granted in the Statute of Local Governments by ordinary legislative enactment. In view of the 80-year history of State protection of the Adirondack Park, with its unique resources, the Adirondack Park Agency Act not only addresses itself to a matter of substantial State concern, but relates to the life, health and quality of life of the people of the State. And, for legislation to involve a State concern it is not imperative that it be rooted in a particular provision of the Constitution. Although the constitutional reservation of power to the State provides for express exceptions, the grant of local zoning and planning powers under the Statute of Local Governments does not require that there be two successive enactments in order to legislate in a matter of State concern. In the Statute of Local Governments itself, there is no analogous provision for exceptions to the reservation of power to the State, and, historically, the constitutional powers reserved to the State have not been subordinated to the powers granted to localities under the Statute of Local Governments.

 

380 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Wein v. Carey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


41 N.Y.2d 498; 362 N.E.2d 587; 393 N.Y.S.2d 955; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 1911


March 22, 1977, Argued  
March 31, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers appealed from the decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) that affirmed the grant of summary judgment to defendants, Governor of the State of New York and state officials, in the taxpayers' action for a declaratory judgment and injunctive relief concerning tax and revenue anticipation notes issued by the state.


OVERVIEW: Defendants adopted a purportedly balanced budget plan, implemented by statutes providing for revenue raised by taxes and appropriations for the operation of state government. Included in the plan was provision for the redemption of anticipation notes, shortly to mature, which was the product of a deficit resulting from a prior fiscal year. Under the state constitution and applicable state law, these deficit notes were to be and were paid in the following fiscal year from revenues impounded by the state comptroller. The taxpayers argued that the state failed in its constitutional obligation to provide for a balanced budget and that the recurring issuance of anticipation notes in successive years violated the New York Constitution. The court reasoned the taxpayers could not prevail by merely showing successive deficits or that provisions were made for successive series of deficit anticipation notes. Rather, the proof of improper budget manipulation had to be found in the estimates of revenues and expenditures to constitute a planned, and therefore an unconstitutional, deficit. Because the taxpayers presented no fact issue that required a trial, summary judgment was justified.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision that upheld the grant of summary judgment to defendants in the taxpayers' action for a declaratory judgment and injunctive relief concerning the alleged unconstitutional issuance of tax and revenue anticipation notes.


HEADNOTES

State -- tax anticipation notes -- deficit budget.

1. Under article 7-A of the State Finance Law and the case of Boryszewski v Brydges (37 NY2d 361), plaintiff taxpayer has standing to challenge as unconstitutional the issuance by the State of tax and revenue anticipation notes on the ground that State officials knew that there was no authentic balance between income and expenditures in the fiscal year, and State officials, at least in the case of the Comptroller, do not possess sovereign immunity.

2. Bona fide holders of the notes in good faith for value without notice of defects may enforce them, regardless of their validity, since there is explicit constitutional authority for the issue and the investor may rely on such patent authority. Lack of patent authority may render an issue wholly void, while remedies arising from latent infirmities are not enforceable against bona fide holders for value. Plaintiff's complaint alleging infirmities relating to procedures, findings, accounts and nonpublicized documents, not readily available to investors, cannot support a declaration of voidness in view of the patent authority for the issue. A contrary holding would render public bond issues vulnerable to constitutional attack at any time and would make such issues speculative.

3. The purportedly balanced budget in the year of issuance of the notes is not proved to have been knowingly unbalanced merely because it was one in a series of budgets which ultimately resulted in deficits in the year of repayment. Although a planned deficit is unconstitutional and although two successive deficits occurred, it cannot be said that there must have been a planned deficit, since all that is necessary for a deficit to occur is a shortfall in revenues or a rise in spending beyond estimates. As a practical matter, any honestly balanced budget will not remain in balance to the end of the fiscal year and will result in either a deficit or surplus. Even assuming an invalid rollover of anticipated deficits in the past, a current budget plan is valid if it provides for payment of such deficit in a balanced budget.

4. Proof of improper budget manipulation must be found in the estimates of revenues and expenditures in order to show the unconstitutional use of anticipation notes to balance an imbalanced budget, and the burden of proof thereon should be upon the challenger, regardless of a showing of successive deficits, since a courtroom would otherwise be converted into a super-auditing office duplicating the responsibilities of the Governor and Legislature.

5. Accordingly, plaintiff is not entitled to an injunction barring further issuance of tax anticipation notes pending a balancing of the budget.

 

381 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Amato v. Ward


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


41 N.Y.2d 469; 362 N.E.2d 566; 393 N.Y.S.2d 934; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 1906


February 14, 1977, Argued  
March 29, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state prisoner appealed an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the trial court's judgment dismissing petitioner's motion requesting that respondent correctional authorities restore six months "good time" behavior in determining his eligibility for conditional release. Petitioner asserted that respondent erred in not granting petitioner a full hearing.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner state prisoner was convicted of manslaughter in the second degree, and at the time of sentencing, petitioner was credited with four years of jail time but his prison record was also marked by several altercations with the prison authorities and numerous disciplinary actions. As petitioner's conditional release date drew near, he appeared before respondent correctional authorities' prison time allowance committee, in compliance with N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 8, § 261.3(b), (d), and respondent recommended that he be allowed two years and eight months of good time rather than the then-allowable maximum of three years and two months. Petitioner asserted that respondent erred in not granting petitioner a full hearing before reaching a decision on his good time allowance. The court affirmed the court of appeals' decision affirming the trial court's judgment dismissing petitioner's motion to restore six months of good time behavior because no charges were pending before respondent at the time and so there was no need to hold a hearing, and the court held that it was within respondent's discretion to have determined the amount of good time to allow.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the court of appeals' decision affirming the trial court's judgment that dismissed petitioner state prisoner's motion to restore six months of good time behavior because no charges were pending before respondent correctional authorities' prison time allowance committee and there was no need to hold a hearing. The court held that it was within the committee's discretion to determine the amount of good time to allow.


HEADNOTES

Prisons and prisoners -- allowance of time for good behavior -- due process requirements.

1. Due process does not require that petitioner prisoner be given a hearing with advance written notice and "charges" before he may be denied a full allowance of time for good behavior based upon his institutional record. The Constitution requires only that prisoners not be penalized for prison misconduct without notice and a hearing on charges of misconduct, and petitioner received hearings more protective than due process requires on each of the charges brought prior to the determination to fix his good behavior allowance at six months less than the maximum allowable. The prison authorities were entitled to rely upon the prior proceedings along with petitioner's over-all prison record in fixing the allowance, without any further hearing, since the time allowance committee does punish misconduct, but evaluates a prisoner's record and grants an allowance as a measure of achievement by the prisoner. Any further notice would be superfluous, since the prisoner knows what evidence is in his record and, there being no charges, there is no need for a hearing.

2. Petitioner's constant misbehavior in prison justified the determination that he receive less than a full allowance.

3. Although petitioner is on parole, recurring issues of public interest are presented and the case should not be dismissed as moot.

 

382 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Jeter v. Ellenville Cent. School Dist.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


41 N.Y.2d 283; 360 N.E.2d 1086; 392 N.Y.S.2d 403; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 1836


January 3, 1977, Argued  
February 15, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant board of education requested review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York) that modified and affirmed an order finding that N.Y. Educ. Law § 3202(5)(a) was constitutional and requiring compliance with the law.


OVERVIEW: Respondent child initiated the action to compel respondent school to provide public education to children residing in foster homes within the school district. The school district contended that the responsibility for the child's tuition was not distinguishable from the responsibility for the child's support. The board of education contended that N.Y. Educ. Law § 3202(5)(a) was unconstitutional. The court held that N.Y. Educ. Law § 3202(5)(a) imposed the cost of the child's instruction on the school district in which the child resided when the child became a public charge prior to January 1, 1974 and that § 3202(5)(a) distinguished between the responsibility for "support and maintenance" and responsibility for "tuition costs." The court also held that the units of municipal government did not have standing to raise the constitutional issues.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order modifying the judgment providing for the costs of tuition for foster children.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- liability for cost of instruction -- pupils placed in family homes.

1. Paragraph a of subdivision 5 of section 3202 of the Education Law (as amd by L 1974, ch 919) is to be interpreted as imposing the cost of instruction of pupils placed in family homes at board by social services districts and State agencies on the school district in which the pupil resided at the time the social services district or State agency assumed responsibility for his support and maintenance, provided that such cost of instruction shall continue to be borne by any social services district or State agency which assumed responsibility for tuition costs prior to January 1, 1974.

2. The proviso in paragraph a which refers to any social services district or State agency "which assumed responsibility for tuition costs for any such pupil" refers only to districts and agencies that, prior to January 1, 1974 assumed responsibility for tuition costs of welfare beneficiaries in addition to or as distinguished from responsibility for general support and maintenance. Use of the terms "support and maintenance" and "tuition costs" in close proximity supports the conclusion that the references were to assumptions of different responsibilities.

3. Arguments addressed to the economic and political wisdom of the cost allocation scheme of the statute are disputations outside the scope of judicial review.

4. While the New York City Board of Education and Department of Social Services and the Board of Education of the City of Yonkers have procedural standing to be heard on questions of statutory interpretation, they do not have the substantive right to raise constitutional challenges as to due process and equal protection.

5. There is no warrant in the statute or its legislative history for a presumption that the school district in which the pupil resided immediately before his transfer to the receiving school district is the municipal entity responsible for payment of the cost of instruction to the receiving district.

 

383 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Charles v. Diamond


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


41 N.Y.2d 318; 360 N.E.2d 1295; 392 N.Y.S.2d 594; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 1842; 7 ELR 20434


November 16, 1976, Argued  
February 15, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order modified, without costs, and the matter remitted to Supreme Court, Onondaga County, for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landowner brought a proceeding under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 against respondents, village, county official, and state official. The Supreme Court, Appellate Division, Fourth Judicial Department (New York), affirmed that part of an order that directed the village to comply with a consent order and directed the issuance of a permit. The village and the state official appealed.


OVERVIEW: The landowner planned to construct apartment buildings on his property. Such buildings were required to be connected to the village sewage system. The state official later informed the landowner that he could not connect into the village sewage system until the village began to correct the deficiencies of their sewage system. Subsequently, the landowner filed an art. 78 proceeding contending that the actions of the state, county, and village were arbitrary and capricious and resulted in an unconstitutional appropriation of his property without compensation. Following reinstatement of the action by the appellate division, the supreme court directed the village to proceed immediately to correct the deficiencies in its sewer system and directed the state official to issue a permit authorizing a sewer connection upon proof of compliance by the village. The claim for money damages was dismissed. On review, the appellate division affirmed, but reinstated the money damages claim. On further review, the court found the parties had not submitted the proof necessary for judicial evaluation, and, thus, the court was unable to determine the constitutionality of the village's action.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed with modifications and remitted for additional proceedings and proof.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- invalid police power regulation of real property.

1. A municipal requirement that property developed for human use be connected to public sewers, while the Department of Environmental Conservation has prohibited such connection because the municipal sewer system does not meet State standards, may be justified in denying petitioner developer the right to build only if the delay in providing sewer attachments is reasonably limited in time, since temporary restraints to promote the public interest are permissible, while permanent interference with the reasonable use of private property for the purposes for which it is suited is not. Police power regulation is reasonable if it is in response to dire necessity, is reasonably calculated to alleviate the crisis condition and if the municipality is taking steps to recitify the problem. The ultimate economic burden of a general community condition must be shared by the members of the community and cannot be placed entirely upon particular property owners.

2. Petitioner's allegations create a triable issue concerning the constitutionality of the village sewer ordinance as applied to his property. At trial it will be relevant to establish the nature of the village sewage disposal problem, what time and expense are necessary to correct the difficulty and what funds are available. The municipal delay in providing adequate sewage disposal, which will aggregate at least 14 years, is justifiable only upon a showing that the remedial steps taken are extensive and have been diligently pursued in good faith. It must be shown that petitioner has been deprived of any reasonable use of his property and that the village has acted or refused to act and placed the social cost of a benefit entirely upon particular landowners.

3. While the delay imposed upon petitioner has been substantial and has not been justified, petitioner has not shown economic injury, and it would, accordingly, be inappropriate to determine the constitutionality of the municipal action upon such an inadequate record.

4. The village was properly ordered to comply with State consent orders that it improve its sewer system, since the village is required to do nothing more than other lawful authority has already directed it to do.

5. If petitioner establishes that he has been subjected to an unreasonable delay, he is entitled to a declaration that the ordinance requiring use of public sewers may not constitutionally be applied to him and he would be free to develop his property by the use of a private sewer system complying with local law.

6. Petitioner is not entitled to a money judgment for permanent damages sustained as a result of an invalid police power regulation, since the government has neither intruded upon the property, inflicting irreversible damage nor does it intend eventual public ownership.

7. Consequential damages for an unreasonable temporary delay caused by an invalid police power regulation are unavailable where there has been no direct government encroachment or de facto taking. An award of damages would strip municipal officers of their discretionary power over the desirability of public construction, since there would be potential liability for failure to provide services to undeveloped land or for regulation over development, while prospective plaintiffs could cumulate their damages through inaction, in preference to immediate suit.

8. CPLR article 78 provides the proper remedy to compel the village to comply with State directives, and a declaratory judgment is the proper vehicle to challenge the constitutionality of the village's action.

9. The claim against the State should be dismissed, since any fault rests with the village, while the Department of Environmental Conservation is merely exercising its function in the prevention of water pollution.

 

384 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


New York Times Co. v. New York Com. on Human Rights


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


41 N.Y.2d 345; 361 N.E.2d 963; 393 N.Y.S.2d 312; 1977 N.Y. LEXIS 1846; 2 Media L. Rep. 1435; 14 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P7609


November 15, 1976, Argued  
February 10, 1977, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant City of New York Commission on Human Rights (commission) sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department, New York, which affirmed the judgment of the lower court, holding that the language of certain advertisements was not such as to indicate an intent on the part of respondent newspaper to participate in a program of discrimination.


OVERVIEW: The newspaper published separate advertisements of employment opportunities available in the Republic of South Africa. None of the advertisements contained references to race or to racially based discrimination. Appellants, committee and others, filed a complaint with the commission. The commission ordered the newspaper to cease and desist from printing such advertisements. The lower court granted a petition for review and set aside the order. The Appellate Division affirmed that judgment and granted the commission permission to appeal. The court affirmed the order of the Appellate Division, but on other grounds. The court held that advertisements of employment opportunities located within the Republic of South Africa which merely referred to that country as the situs of the employment and which did not recite, on the surface, any discriminatory conditions did not express discrimination within the meaning of the New York City antidiscrimination laws. Further, the court held that a newspaper, which published such employment advertisements, did not thereby aid and abet the invidious discrimination practiced within the Republic of South Africa under color of national law and policy.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the Appellate Division that affirmed the judgment that set aside the commission's order.


HEADNOTES

Civil rights -- discriminatory advertising.

1. As matter of law, advertisements of employment opportunities located within the Republic of South Africa which refer to that country as the situs of employment without reciting, on the surface, any discriminatory conditions do not directly or indirectly express discrimination within the meaning of the New York City antidiscrimination laws. No reference was made to race or racially based discrimination, and violation of the advertising restrictions (Administrative Code of City of New York, § B1-7.0, subd 1, par [d]) is measured by expression of discrimination, not by the actual practice thereof. Although code words indirectly signaling the practice of discrimination may not be utilized as a substitute for terms clearly proscribed by law, the use of the name South Africa is a mere geographical reference conveying essential employment information, while a code word is an unnecessary discriminatory addition to an otherwise complete advertisement, adding nothing but invidious unfairness.

2. Although factual findings of the New York City Commission on Human Rights are reviewable only upon a test of the sufficiency of the supporting evidence in the record, both the complaint and the determination of the commission that petitioner newspaper aided an unlawful discriminatory practice were predicated upon matters of law drawn from an undisputed set of facts, and, under the circumstances, the issue for review is the propriety of the commission's analysis of the law.

3. Accordingly, petitioner should not have been held to have abetted the publication of discriminatory advertisements.

People ex rel. Walsh v. Vincent


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


40 N.Y.2d 1049; 360 N.E.2d 919; 392 N.Y.S.2d 240; 1976 N.Y. LEXIS 3235


November 24, 1976, Argued  
December 28, 1976, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action involving respondent State, appellant correctional facility superintendent sought review of an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed a judgment dismissing a writ of habeas corpus, granted the petition for the writ, with prejudice, and restored the parolee to parole under the conditions theretofore in effect.


OVERVIEW: The parolee was charged with two new offenses and parole violation. He was remanded to a New York City jail. At a preliminary hearing, he requested a speedy final hearing. He plead guilty to one new offense, the other one was dismissed, and he was sentenced to time served. He was returned to a state correctional facility where a final parole revocation hearing was held, resulting in revocation of his parole. The lower appellate court found that the parolee's right to a prompt final parole revocation hearing was violated. On appeal, the court held that the parolee was entitled to a prompt final parole revocation hearing. The fact that he was lodged in a local correctional facility was immaterial because he was in a place subject to the convenience and practical control of the parole board, as demonstrated by the fact that he was brought from one local facility to another in order to give him a preliminary parole hearing. He was in the custody of a correction facility as an inmate in connection with which the parole board had parole jurisdiction. It was immaterial that the technical form of the custody was temporary detention due to inadequate detention facilities in New York City.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order.


HEADNOTES

Parole -- parole revocation -- right to prompt hearing.

People v. Drummond


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York

In People v Luis J.: Order reversed and the case remitted to Supreme Court, Kings County, for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.

Evans v. Carey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]
