392 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Parks


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


41 N.Y.2d 36; 359 N.E.2d 358; 390 N.Y.S.2d 848; 1976 N.Y. LEXIS 3204


September 15, 1976, Argued  
December 16, 1976, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the verdict convicting appellant of third degree rape and endangering the welfare of a child.


OVERVIEW: A jury found defendant guilty of third degree rape and endangering the welfare of a mentally retarded complainant under the age of 16. The jury of nine men and three women was selected in accordance with existing procedural law. At trial, the court allowed complainant and her teacher to testify. Defendant sought review of his conviction on grounds that the jury selection at trial was unconstitutional and the trial court erred in allowing complainant to testify. On appeal, the court affirmed the conviction. The state had wide discretion in formulating procedures for jury selection. Defendant was not entitled to a jury of any particular composition. Defendant failed to show that the exemption accorded women under then operative state law resulted in a systematic exclusion of women from jury service in his trial. Complainant was deemed competent to testify by the trial court and the court declined to disturb the finding because there was no abuse of discretion. To assist the court and the jury in their functions, the trial court properly permitted complainant's teacher to testify as to complainant's competency.


OUTCOME: Verdict of third degree rape and child endangering was affirmed where defendant failed to show a systematic exclusion of women from the jury. The trial court did not abuse its discretion by allowing testimony from the mentally retarded complainant who had sufficient intelligence to understand the nature of an oath and to give a reasonably accurate account of events. Complainant's teacher's testimony was proper to assist the jury.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- exemption of women from jury service -- expert testimony before jury regarding competency of witness.

1. The issue of defendant's challenge to the jury panel was preserved for review, despite failure to submit a written challenge specifying the underlying facts, since the parties had advance notice and there was no surprise or prejudice and, under the circumstances, the trial court could properly waive the formality of a writing.

2. The conclusion of the United States Supreme Court in Taylor v Louisiana (419 U.S. 522), that automatic exemptions from jury duty for women as a class are untenable if they result in almost totally male juries, establishes the appropriate test of the propriety of such exemptions. The test thereunder should be, not the constitutionality of the former governing statute on its face, but whether the statute in application results in the virtual exclusion of women.

3. No systematic exclusion of women from Nassau County juries may be found, since women constitute one third of prospective jurors in the county. Accordingly, despite exercise of the exemption, a very substantial number of women participated on jury panels, 5 out of 12 prospective jurors and 3 of the jurors ultimately sitting on the jury in defendant's trial being female.

4. It was not improper to permit the complainant's teacher to testify before the jury as to the mental condition of the complainant to explain an apparent mental deficiency, since the testimony was restricted to objective matters and did not touch upon the credibility of the complainant. Expert testimony regarding an alleged incompetent serves to alert the court and jury to the nature of the witness' condition and provides a framework within which the testimony and behavior of the witness may be considered. The ability of the witness to testify must, as here, be in issue.

5. It cannot be concluded that the complaining witness was so mentally deficient that the reception of her testimony was a clear abuse of discretion. The witness' statements that she could testify truthfully and distinguish truth from falsity and her demeanor and appearance support the finding of the trial court that she possessed sufficient capacity to testify.

6. The complainant's hospital records were properly admitted into evidence, since her abortion while under the age of consent is evidence that a crime was committed by someone and, thus, sufficiently corroborates defendant's admissions of guilt.

7. Accordingly, the convictions of defendant for the crimes of rape in the third degree and endangering the welfare of a child were properly affirmed.

 

393 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Lighthouse Shores, Inc. v. Islip


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


41 N.Y.2d 7; 359 N.E.2d 337; 390 N.Y.S.2d 827; 1976 N.Y. LEXIS 3200; 7 ELR 20290


September 17, 1976, Argued  
December 2, 1976, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order modified, without costs, and case remitted to Supreme Court, Suffolk County, for entry of judgment in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant municipality sought review of the decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which unanimously affirmed a judgment of the Supreme Court at Special Term, Nassau County in plaintiff property owners' action seeking a declaratory judgment and certain injunctive relief, which declared that the municipality's ordinance regulating the use of vehicles within said municipality was unreasonable and void.


OVERVIEW: The municipality passed an ordinance that barred property owners from private walks and curtailed operation of vehicles by restricting permits to those residing solely there year round. The municipality passed the ordinance to control erosion problems that were exacerbated by vehicular traffic. The trial court found that regulation of private walks was unreasonable and that restriction of permits to year-round residents constituted invidious discrimination. The appellate court affirmed without opinion. The court determined that the municipality's ordinance was entitled to a presumption of validity, and the property owners had the burden of demonstrating beyond a reasonable doubt that the ordinance was unconstitutional and that there was a total absence of any reasonable basis for it. The court found that expert testimony substantiated the municipality's ecological concerns. Vehicles on narrow private walks frequently were forced to pull over on the sand to make way for other vehicles or pedestrians. The court also found that the differentiation between year-round and seasonal residents was a reasonable attempt to provide permits to those who had the greater need for transportation.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that the judgment of the appellate court should be modified, without costs, and the matter remitted to the trial court for entry of judgment upholding the constitutionality of the municipality's ordinance.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- regulation of traffic.

1. So much of an ordinance of the Town of Islip which purports to regulate traffic over State-owned and maintained portions of an inland route leading into and through an area of Fire Island is beyond the powers of the town. A public highway is any highway and the term "highway" encompasses every publicly maintained way open to public vehicular traffic (Vehicle and Traffic Law, §§ 118, 134). Section 1604 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law prohibits local restriction of public highways, and an affirmed finding of Special Term, made upon adequate proof, that the route was a public highway should not be disturbed.

2. Provisions of the ordinance requiring permits for operation of motor vehicles on Fire Island and restricting such permits to year-round residents were properly embraced by the notice of hearing published prior to adoption and, accordingly, were properly adopted. The notice set forth proposals that all vehicular use be by permit, with a limitation on the number of permits and the hours and periods of the year during which travel would be allowed and one proposal was to prohibit all travel except by public utilities and those engaged in refuse collection.

3. The restrictions as applied to vehicular traffic on private walkways, were not arbitrary, since they were related to the reasonable public purpose of protecting the fragile ecology of the island from deterioration, and, under the strong presumption of constitutionality accorded municipal enactments, substantiating evidence of overuse by motor vehicles and consequent damage to beach areas was not overcome beyond a reasonable doubt.

4. The restriction of permits to year-round residents reasonably confined vehicular use to those having a greater need and, accordingly, the statutory discrimination against part-time residents does not offend equal protection.

 

394 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Flushing Nat'l Bank v. Municipal Assistance Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


40 N.Y.2d 731; 358 N.E.2d 848; 390 N.Y.S.2d 22; 1976 N.Y. LEXIS 3252


September 7, 1976, Argued  
November 19, 1976, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc. [See 40 NY2d 1088, 1094.]


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, bank on behalf of other holders of notes, sought review of the order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which affirmed a judgment entered granting defendant, City of New York, summary judgment and declaring that the provision of the New York State Emergency Moratorium Act for the City of New York, Pub. L. 1975, ch. 874, was valid and constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The City of New York passed the New York State Emergency Moratorium Act for the City of New York (Act), Pub. L. 1975, ch. 874, which imposed a three-year moratorium on actions to enforce the city's outstanding short-term obligations. The bank, on behalf of other holders of notes, filed an action alleging that the Act violated both the state and federal constitutions. The appellate court affirmed a judgment entering summary judgment in favor of the city and declaring the Act valid and constitutional. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that N.Y. Const. art. VIII, § 2 stated that a city could not have contractual indebtedness unless it had pledged its faith and credit for the payment of the principal thereof and the interest thereon. The court held that the constitutional prescription of a pledge of faith and credit was designed to protect the rights vulnerable in the even of difficult economic circumstances and that it was conclusive that the constitution permitted no escape for the municipality from performing its obligations.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order from the appellate court. The court held that the New York Constitution provided that defendant, the City of New York, could not have contractual indebtedness unless it had pledged its faith and credit for the payment of the principal thereof and the interest thereon.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- New York State Emergency Moratorium Act for City of New York (L 1975, ch 874, as amd by ch 875) is declared unconstitutional.

1. The New York State Emergency Moratorium Act for the City of New York (L 1975, ch 874, as amd by ch 875), which imposes a three-year moratorium on actions to enforce the city's short-term obligations, to be effective only with respect to the holders of such obligations who have been offered, and have declined, an opportunity to exchange their notes for an equal principal amount of long-term bonds issued by the Municipal Assistance Corporation for the City of New York, violates the State Constitution in denying faith and credit to the short-term anticipation notes of the city. The State Constitution prohibits the city from contracting any indebtedness unless it pledges its faith and credit for the payment of the principal of the indebtedness (art VIII, § 2), and, by depriving short-term noteholders of judicial remedies for at least three years, the act makes meaningless the pledge of faith and credit which the city gave to them.

2. Federal issues are not reached.

3. The act is declared unconstitutional, but since, for well over a year, many financial transactions have occurred on the assumption that the moratorium would be constitutionally acceptable, and in order to minimize market and governmental disruptions which might ensue, extraordinary remedies in the nature of injunction and peremptory mandamus, sought in this action by a holder of New York City short-term anticipation notes, are not allowed at this time.

Toia v. Regan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


40 N.Y.2d 837; 356 N.E.2d 276; 387 N.Y.S.2d 832; 1976 N.Y. LEXIS 3010


September 13, 1976, Argued

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county officials challenged a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the trial court's judgment granting a petition to compel them to provide funds necessary for the administration of the home relief, aid to dependent children, medical assistance, and day care programs and directed them to reimburse the state for funds advanced pursuant to prior court orders.


OVERVIEW: Respondent Commissioner of the New York State Department of Social Services filed a N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 petition against the county officials. On appeal, the court affirmed the lower courts' orders. The court held that the New York Social Services Law provisions that effected a 50 percent contribution of the non-federal outlay for the public assistance programs by the county were constitutional under both the United States and New York State Constitutions. The county officials argued that the cost of public assistance amounted to 56 percent of the county's total budget and that the statutory straitjacket, requiring the local expenditure of one half of the non-federal expenses for public assistance, severely limited the availability of sufficient tax revenues for traditional county services, thus rendering meaningless the county's right to manage its own affairs. The applicable statutes of the New York Social Services Law were, however, general laws and pursuant to N.Y. Const. art IX, § 2(b)(2), the state legislature had the power to act in relation to the property, affairs, or government of any local government by general law. To that extent, the general laws were controlling.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's order, which affirmed the trial court's judgment granting a petition to compel the county officials to provide funds necessary for the administration of the home relief, aid to dependent children, medical assistance, and day care programs in the county and that directed them to reimburse the state for funds advanced pursuant to any prior court orders.


HEADNOTES

Social services -- funding of public assistance programs -- imposition of 50% of non-Federal costs upon county.

397 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Tuller v. Central School Dist.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


40 N.Y.2d 487; 354 N.E.2d 826; 387 N.Y.S.2d 87; 1976 N.Y. LEXIS 2921; 92 L.R.R.M. 3347


June 9, 1976, Argued  
July 13, 1976, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner teachers and respondent school district appealed the affirmance by the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York) of a supreme court order that annulled the district's termination of the teachers' employment and directed the teachers' reinstatement for the remainder of their probationary terms when they were not assigned to classrooms.


OVERVIEW: Two tenured public school teachers who were discharged without a hearing during a year's probation for their participation in an illegal strike were reinstated pursuant to a court order. While on probation and without classroom assignments, the teachers were notified in writing that the district's superintendent would not recommend that they be appointed to tenure at the end of their probationary year because of their unsatisfactory services during probation. The district's board of education met with the teachers and their supervisors, received written and oral reports on the teachers' performance levels, and voted not to grant them tenure. On the teachers' petition, the supreme court reinstated the teachers to an additional four months' probation and evaluation. The appellate court affirmed. On appeal, the court reversed because the teachers were not entitled to a hearing for their discharge for unsatisfactory service at the end of their probationary period. The district complied with N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 4, § 4.5 (i) by giving the teachers the mandated observations and feedback and by giving the teachers more than adequate notification before school recessed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that reinstated the teachers, and dismissed the teachers' petition.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- probation imposed under Taylor Law -- termination -- notice given by school district to petitioner teachers of its intent not to grant tenure at end of penalty probationary year imposed under Taylor Law was properly given prior to end of such year; Taylor Law requires penalty probation to have same effect upon teachers as other public employees and 4 NYCRR 4.5 (a) (3) allows termination for unsatisfactory performance only after completion of minimum period of probation, while Taylor Law probation is for specific, rather than minimum period; treatment of period as maximum rather than minimum accords best with legislative intent, since treatment as minimum would require that notice be given only on final day of period -- petitioners were properly advised under 4 NYCRR 4.5 (i) of their status and progress after careful observation and written report was properly submitted to appointing authority; oral reports at board of education meeting, affidavits and evaluation reports indicate that mandated observations and status reports were made; four-month lapse in petitioners' teaching duties, caused by earlier termination, found invalid, did not deprive them of right to evaluation -- there is no right to hearing prior to termination for unsatisfactory service at end of probation.

1. Notice given by respondent school district to petitioner teachers of its intent not to grant them tenure at the end of a penalty probationary year imposed under the Taylor Law (Civil Service Law, § 210, subd 2, par [f]) was properly given prior to the end of such year. The Taylor Law requires that the effect of probation imposed thereunder be no different for teachers than for other public employees. Under 4 NYCRR 4.5 (a) (3), a probationer's employment may be terminated for unsatisfactory performance only after completion of the minimum period and on or before completion of the maximum period of probation, while probation under the Taylor Law provides a specific, rather than minimum and maximum, probationary term. An overliberal interpretation would require the absurdity that notice be given on the final day of the period, while it accords better with the legislative intent to treat the one-year period as a maximum for purposes of 4 NYCRR 4.5.

2. Respondent properly complied with the provisions of 4 NYCRR 4.5 (i) to advise petitioner probationers of their status and progress after careful observation by their supervisor and to submit a written report to the appointing authority. Oral reports were given to the board of education at its meeting and affidavits and evaluation reports were submitted which indicate that the mandated observations and status reports were made. The preprobationary performance levels of petitioners, who were experienced teachers, indicate that they knew what standards applied to them. A four-month lapse in petitioners' teaching duties, caused by an earlier termination found invalid in a prior proceeding, did not deprive them of their right to be evaluated.

3. There is no due process right or right under 4 NYCRR 4.5 to accord probationers a hearing prior to termination for unsatisfactory service at the end of probation.

 

398 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


Koutrakos v. Long Island College Hospital


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


39 N.Y.2d 1026; 355 N.E.2d 301; 387 N.Y.S.2d 247; 1976 N.Y. LEXIS 2957


June 7, 1976, Argued  
July 13, 1976, Decided



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Workmen's compensation -- attorney's fees -- carrier's lien -- plaintiff received death benefits from workmen's compensation carrier and subsequently entered into settlement of wrongful death action against third party -- carrier asserted lien for benefits paid by it -- Supreme Court reduced carrier's lien, holding that section 29 (subd 1) of Workmen's Compensation Law was unconstitutional to extent it required carrier's lien to be satisfied in full without carrier proportionately sharing cost of plaintiff's attorney's fees in effectuating recovery in third-party  [***4] action -- Appellate Division modified by reinstating full amount of carrier's lien, stating that section 29 (subd 1) was not violative of State or Federal Constitutions and that rectification was for Legislature -- order of Appellate Division affirmed on opinion thereat, noting that while statute has since been amended (Workmen's Compensation Law, § 29, subd 1, as amd by L 1975, ch 190, § 1) its effective date did not precede settlement of third-party action or court's order fixing payment of lien. 

 

399 of 2465 DOCUMENTS


People v. Eason


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


40 N.Y.2d 297; 353 N.E.2d 587; 386 N.Y.S.2d 673; 1976 N.Y. LEXIS 2888


June 8, 1976, Argued  
July 8, 1976, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of eight counts of possessing and selling heroin. Defendant filed a motion to set aside the sentences imposed on the four counts that were class "A" felonies on the ground that N.Y. Penal Law § 65.00(1)(b) was unconstitutional. The trial court denied the motion and the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York) affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On the class "A" felonies, defendant was sentenced to imprisonment for a minimum of one-year and a maximum of life. Section 65.00(1)(b) provided that the court could sentence defendant to probation for class "A" felonies if defendant provided material assistance in the investigation or apprehension of a person for a felony only upon the recommendation of the prosecution. Defendant argued § 65.00(1)(b) violated the constitutional doctrines of separation of powers, due process, or equal protection. The court disagreed and affirmed the denial of defendant's motion. The court found the statute did not unconstitutionally vest the executive branch of the government with sentencing discretion because the final discretion to impose sentence was intact in the judiciary. The statute served only to limit sentencing options. Defendant was also not deprived of an impartial decision-maker by the requirement of prosecutorial consent because the court rendered the final determination as to sentence. No equal protection violation existed when the statute permitted selection of any given defendant for sentencing treatment that would vary from that of other defendants.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of defendant's motion to set aside the sentences imposed on the class "A" felonies.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sentence -- probation -- paragraph (b) of subdivision 1 of section 65.00 of Penal Law, which provides that court may sentence person to probation on conviction of class A-III felony if prosecutor so recommends on ground such person has provided material assistance in investigation and prosecution of any person for felony involving dangerous drugs, is constitutional in that it is not violative of doctrines of separation of powers, due process or equal protection -- defendant cannot automatically avail himself of provisions of statute by co-operating with authorities and, accordingly, although it was asserted that defendant had disclosed all he knew about local drug situation, defendant was properly sentenced to minimum of one year and maximum of life -- exclusion of public while undercover officers were testifying was not erroneous as matter of law or in violation of Constitutions.

1. Paragraph (b) of subdivision 1 of section 65.00 of the Penal Law, which provides that a court may sentence a person to a period of probation on conviction of a class A-III felony if the prosecutor so recommends on the ground that such person has provided material assistance in the investigation or prosecution of any person for a felony involving dangerous drugs, is constitutional.

2. The probation sentencing alternative does not violate the doctrine of separation of powers by vesting the executive branch of government with discretion reserved to the judiciary. So long as the statute does not take from courts the final discretion to impose sentence, it does not infringe on the constitutional division of responsibilities.

3. The statute does not violate due process by injecting prosecutorial consent into the sentencing process, thus depriving defendant of an impartial decision-maker. The final determination as to sentence is made by a neutral member of the judiciary.

4. Designed to get small drug dealers to co-operate in the apprehension of bigger traffickers, the statute is rational and satisfies the requirements of equal protection.

5. Since the presence of any pattern of consciously practiced discrimination has not been demonstrated, no claim of unequal enforcement can be made.

6. A defendant cannot automatically avail himself of the provisions of the statute by co-operating with the authorities. Accordingly, although it was asserted that defendant had disclosed all he knew about the local drug situation, defendant was properly sentenced, on his conviction on four class A felony counts, to a minimum of one year and a maximum of life.

7. The decision of the trial court to exclude the public while the undercover officers were testifying was not erroneous as a matter of law or in violation of the State or Federal Constitutions.

People v. Lawrence


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


39 N.Y.2d 956; 353 N.E.2d 849; 386 N.Y.S.2d 885; 1976 N.Y. LEXIS 2905


June 1, 1976, Argued  
July 1, 1976, Decided



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, by permission, challenged an order of the Rensselaer County Court (New York), which affirmed town court judgments upon pleas of guilty that convicted appellants of criminal possession of a dangerous drug in the sixth degree and imposed on each appellant a sentence of nine months' imprisonment in the county jail and a fine of $ 250.


OVERVIEW: A state trooper arrested appellants after he had stopped their automobile for a traffic infraction, noticed a smell of burnt marijuana, and observed marijuana cigarettes in plain view. In town court, appellants moved to suppress the physical evidence. Upon respondent's request pursuant to N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 710.40(3), the hearing on the motion was held during the jury trial, and the motion was denied. Appellants were convicted of criminal possession of a dangerous drug in the sixth degree, and the county court affirmed. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court rejected appellants' contentions that they were denied due process and equal protection of the law because, pursuant to the explicit provisions of § 710.40(3), at the request of respondent the town court determined appellants' pretrial motion to suppress during the course of their trial. The court found that appellants did not suffer any actual prejudice in consequence of the application of the statutory direction, and therefore the mid-trial determination of the motion to suppress involved no constitutional infirmity. As to the denial of the motion to suppress itself, the court ruled that there was no error.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the county court's order that affirmed appellants' convictions, because the application of the statute permitting the mid-trial determination of appellants' motion to suppress involved no constitutional infirmity, and because the town court did not err when it denied appellants' motion to suppress.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- suppression of evidence -- following denial of their motions to suppress evidence, heard during jury trial upon People's request under CPL 710.40 (subd 3), defendants entered pleas of guilty to criminal possession of dangerous drug in sixth degree, and County Court affirmed convictions -- contentions that trial court erroneously denied motion to suppress, that CPL 710.40 (subd 3) was unconstitutional, and that sentences were excessive -- order of County Court affirmed -- absent demonstration of substantial actual prejudice in particular circumstances of individual case, midtrial determination of motion to suppress involves no constitutional infirmity -- no error in denial of motion to suppress -- contention that sentences  [**3] were excessive not within scope of review.
